Compiling this L](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [A](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [TEX source has the effect of gen-](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [erating a device-independent representation of a textbook, which can be converted to other formats](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [and printed.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

The L A [TEX source for this book is available from](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [http://www.thinkpython.com](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

# [Preface](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

#### The strange history of this book

[In January 1999 I was preparing to teach an introductory programming class in Java. I had](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) taught it three times and I was getting frustrated. The failure rate in the class was too high [and, even for students who succeeded, the overall level of achievement was too low.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

One of the problems I saw was the books. They were too big, with too much unnecessary [detail about Java, and not enough high-level guidance about how to program. And they all](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [suffered from the trap door effect: they would start out easy, proceed gradually, and then](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [somewhere around Chapter 5 the bottom would fall out. The students would get too much](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [new material, too fast, and I would spend the rest of the semester picking up the pieces.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[Two weeks before the first day of classes, I decided to write my own book. My goals were:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

- Keep it short. It is better for students to read 10 pages than not read 50 pages.

- [• Be careful with vocabulary. I tried to minimize jargon and define each term at first](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [use.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

- [• Build gradually. To avoid trap doors, I took the most difficult topics and split them](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [into a series of small steps.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

- Focus on programming, not the programming language. I included the minimum [useful subset of Java and left out the rest.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

I needed a title, so on a whim I chose How to Think Like a Computer Scientist .

[My first version was rough, but it worked. Students did the reading, and they understood](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) enough that I could spend class time on the hard topics, the interesting topics and (most [important) letting the students practice.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

[I released the book under the GNU Free Documentation License, which allows users to](http://gutenberg.org/) [copy, modify, and distribute the book.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[What happened next is the cool part. Jeff Elkner, a high school teacher in Virginia, adopted](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [my book and translated it into Python. He sent me a copy of his translation, and I had the](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [unusual experience of learning Python by reading my own book. As Green Tea Press, I](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [published the first Python version in 2001.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

[In 2003 I started teaching at Olin College and I got to teach Python for the first time. The](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [contrast with Java was striking. Students struggled less, learned more, worked on more](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) interesting projects, and generally had a lot more fun.

vi Chapter 0. Preface

[Since then I’ve continued to develop the book, correcting errors, improving some of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) examples and adding material, especially exercises.

[The result is this book, now with the less grandiose title](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) Think Python . Some of the changes [are:](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

- [• I added a section about debugging at the end of each chapter. These sections present](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [general techniques for finding and avoiding bugs, and warnings about Python pit-](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [falls.](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

- [• I added more exercises, ranging from short tests of understanding to a few substantial](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [projects. Most exercises include a link to my solution.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

- [• I added a series of case studies—longer examples with exercises, solutions, and dis-](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [cussion.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

- [• I expanded the discussion of program development plans and basic design patterns.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

- [• I added appendices about debugging and analysis of algorithms.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

The second edition of Think Python has these new features:

- [• The book and all supporting code have been updated to Python 3.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

- [• I added a few sections, and more details on the web, to help beginners get started](https://thinkpython.com/code/GoodKangaroo.py) running Python in a browser, so you don’t have to deal with installing Python until you want to.

- For Chapter 4.1 I switched from my own turtle graphics package, called Swampy, to a more standard Python module, turtle , which is easier to install and more powerful.

- [• I added a new chapter called “The Goodies”, which introduces some additional](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) [Python features that are not strictly necessary, but sometimes handy.](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[I hope you enjoy working with this book, and that it helps you learn to program and think](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [like a computer scientist, at least a little bit.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[Allen B. Downey](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

Olin College

#### [Contributor List](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[More than 100 sharp-eyed and thoughtful readers have sent in suggestions and corrections](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [over the past few years. Their contributions, and enthusiasm for this project, have been a](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [huge help.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

If you have a suggestion or correction, please send email to feedback@thinkpython.com . [If I make a change based on your feedback, I will add you to the contributor list (unless](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) you ask to be omitted).

[If you include at least part of the sentence the error appears in, that makes it easy for me to](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) search. Page and section numbers are fine, too, but not quite as easy to work with. Thanks!

[In addition, people who spotted typos or made corrections include Czeslaw Czapla, Dale Wil-](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [son, Francesco Carlo Cimini, Richard Fursa, Brian McGhie, Lokesh Kumar Makani, Matthew](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [Shultz, Viet Le, Victor Simeone, Lars O.D. ## [Chapter 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

# [The way of the program](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[The goal of this book is to teach you to think like a computer scientist. This way of think-](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [ing combines some of the best features of mathematics, engineering, and natural science.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [Like mathematicians, computer scientists use formal languages to denote ideas (specifi-](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [cally computations). Like engineers, they design things, assembling components into sys-](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [tems and evaluating tradeoffs among alternatives. Like scientists, they observe the behav-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [ior of complex systems, form hypotheses, and test predictions.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[The single most important skill for a computer scientist is](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [problem solving](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [. Problem solv-](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) ing means the ability to formulate problems, think creatively about solutions, and express a solution clearly and accurately. As it turns out, the process of learning to program is an excellent opportunity to practice problem-solving skills. That’s why this chapter is called, [“The way of the program”.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[On one level, you will be learning to program, a useful skill by itself. On another level, you](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [will use programming as a means to an end. As we go along, that end will become clearer.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

## [1.1](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) What is a program?

A program is a sequence of instructions that specifies how to perform a computation. The [computation might be something mathematical, such as solving a system of equations or](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) finding the roots of a polynomial, but it can also be a symbolic computation, such as search- [ing and replacing text in a document or something graphical, like processing an image or](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [playing a video.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[The details look different in different languages, but a few basic instructions appear in just](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [about every language:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[input:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [Get data from the keyboard, a file, the network, or some other device.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[output:](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [Display data on the screen, save it in a file, send it over the network, etc.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

math: [Perform basic mathematical operations like addition and multiplication.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

conditional execution: Check for certain conditions and run the appropriate code.

Chapter 1. The way of the program

[repetition:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Perform some action repeatedly, usually with some variation.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Believe it or not, that’s pretty much all there is to it.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) Every program you’ve ever used, no matter how complicated, is made up of instructions that look pretty much like these. [So you can think of programming as the process of breaking a large, complex task into](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [smaller and smaller subtasks until the subtasks are simple enough to be performed with](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) one of these basic instructions.

## 1.2 [Running Python](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

One of the challenges of getting started with Python is that you might have to install Python and related software on your computer. If you are familiar with your operating [system, and especially if you are comfortable with the command-line interface, you will](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) have no trouble installing Python. But for beginners, it can be painful to learn about sys- [tem administration and programming at the same time.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

To avoid that problem, I recommend that you start out running Python in a browser. Later, [when you are comfortable with Python, I’ll make suggestions for installing Python on your](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [computer.](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py)

[There are a number of web pages you can use to run Python. If you already have a fa-](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [vorite, go ahead and use it. Otherwise I recommend PythonAnywhere. I provide detailed](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [instructions for getting started at](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) .

There are two versions of Python, called Python 2 and Python 3. They are very similar, so if you learn one, it is easy to switch to the other. In fact, there are only a few differences you [will encounter as a beginner. This book is written for Python 3, but I include some notes](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) about Python 2.

[The Python](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [interpreter](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [is a program that reads and executes Python code.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [Depending](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) on your environment, you might start the interpreter by clicking on an icon, or by typing [python](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [on a command line. When it starts, you should see output like this:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

Python 3.4.0 (default, Jun 19 2015, 14:20:21) [GCC 4.8.2] on linux Type "help", "copyright", "credits" or "license" for more information. >>>

[The first three lines contain information about the interpreter and the operating system it’s](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [running on, so it might be different for you. But you should check that the version number,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) which is 3.4.0 [in this example, begins with 3, which indicates that you are running Python](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [3. If it begins with 2, you are running (you guessed it) Python 2.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

The last line is a [prompt](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [that indicates that the interpreter is ready for you to enter code. If](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [you type a line of code and hit Enter, the interpreter displays the result:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

### >>> 1 + 1

[Now you’re ready to get started. From here on, I assume that you know how to start the](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) Python interpreter and run code.

### 1.3. The first program

## [1.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [The first program](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Traditionally, the first program you write in a new language is called “Hello, World!” be-](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) cause all it does is display the words “Hello, World!”. In Python, it looks like this:

### [>>> print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [Hello, World!](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

This is an example of a [print statement](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [, although it doesn’t actually print anything on](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [paper. It displays a result on the screen. In this case, the result is the words](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set)

### [Hello, World!](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[The quotation marks in the program mark the beginning and end of the text to be dis-](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [played; they don’t appear in the result.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[The parentheses indicate that](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [print](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [is a function. We’ll get to functions in Chapter 3.](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

In Python 2, the print statement is slightly different; it is not a function, so it doesn’t use [parentheses.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

### [>>> print](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [Hello, World!](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) ['](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[This distinction will make more sense soon, but that’s enough to get started.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

## [1.4](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [Arithmetic operators](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

[After “Hello, World”, the next step is arithmetic. Python provides](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [operators](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [, which are](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [special symbols that represent computations like addition and multiplication.](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

[The operators](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [+](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [,](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [-](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [, and](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [*](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) perform addition, subtraction, and multiplication, as in the fol- [lowing examples:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

### [>>> 40 + 2](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### [>>> 43 - 1](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [>>> 6 * 7](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py)

The operator / performs division:

### >>> 84 / 2 42.0

[You might wonder why the result is](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [42.0](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [instead of](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) . I’ll explain in the next section.

[Finally, the operator](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [**](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [performs exponentiation; that is, it raises a number to a power:](http://gutenberg.org/)

### >>> 62 + 6

[In some other languages,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [^](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [is used for exponentiation, but in Python it is a bitwise operator](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [called XOR. If you are not familiar with bitwise operators, the result will surprise you:](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

### [>>> 6 ^ 2](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

[I won’t cover bitwise operators in this book, but you can read about them at](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [http://wiki.](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators .

Chapter 1. The way of the program

## [1.5](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Values and types](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[A](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [value](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [is one of the basic things a program works with, like a letter or a number. Some](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [values we have seen so far are](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [42.0](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) , and ' Hello, World! ' .

These values belong to different types [:](https://thinkpython.com/code) [is an](https://thinkpython.com/code) [integer](https://thinkpython.com/code) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code) [42.0](https://thinkpython.com/code) [is a](https://thinkpython.com/code) floating-point number , and ' Hello, World! ' is a string [, so-called because the letters it contains are strung together.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

[If you are not sure what type a value has, the interpreter can tell you:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

```
>>> type(2)
<class ' int ' >
>>> type(42.0)
<class ' float ' >
>>> type( ' Hello, World! ' )
<class ' str ' >
```

[In these results, the word “class” is used in the sense of a category; a type is a category of](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [values.](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

[Not surprisingly, integers belong to the type](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [int](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) , strings belong to str [and floating-point](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) numbers belong to [float](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) .

[What about values like](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [42.0](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [? They look like numbers, but they are in quotation](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poker) marks like strings.

| <class    | '   | str  | '   | >   |
| --------- | --- | ---- | --- | --- |
| >>> type( | '   | 42.0 | '   | )   |
| <class    | '   | str  | '   | >   |

They’re strings.

[When you type a large integer, you might be tempted to use commas between groups of](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) digits, as in [1,000,000](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [. This is not a legal](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [integer](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) in Python, but it is legal:

### >>> 1,000,000 [(1, 0, 0)](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py)

[That’s not what we expected at all! Python interprets](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) 1,000,000 [as a comma-separated](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) sequence of integers. We’ll learn more about this kind of sequence later.

## 1.6 [Formal and natural languages](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

Natural languages [are the languages people speak, such as English, Spanish, and French.](http://gutenberg.org/) [They were not designed by people (although people try to impose some order on them);](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [they evolved naturally.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

Formal languages [are languages that are designed by people for specific applications. For](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [example, the notation that mathematicians use is a formal language that is particularly](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [good at denoting relationships among numbers and symbols. Chemists use a formal lan-](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [guage to represent the chemical structure of molecules. And most importantly:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[Programming languages are formal languages that have been designed to](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) express computations.

### 1.6. Formal and natural languages

[Formal languages tend to have strict](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [syntax](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) rules that govern the structure of statements. For example, in mathematics the statement 3 [+](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [=](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [6 has correct syntax, but 3](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [+ =](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorting_algorithm) [3$6](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorting_algorithm) [does not. In chemistry](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [H](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [O](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) is a syntactically correct formula, but Zz [is not.](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

[Syntax rules come in two flavors, pertaining to](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) tokens and structure. Tokens are the basic [elements of the language, such as words, numbers, and chemical elements.](https://thinkpython.com/code) One of the problems with 3 + = [3$6 is that $ is not a legal token in mathematics (at least as far as I](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [know). Similarly,](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [Zz](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [is not legal because there is no element with the abbreviation](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) Zz .

[The second type of syntax rule pertains to the way tokens are combined.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [The equation](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [/3 is illegal because even though](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [+](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) and / are legal tokens, you can’t have one right [after the other. Similarly, in a chemical formula the subscript comes after the element name,](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book1.py) [not before.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

This is @ well-structured Engli$h sentence with invalid t*kens in it. This sentence all valid [tokens has, but invalid structure with.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[When you read a sentence in English or a statement in a formal language, you have to](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [figure out the structure (although in a natural language you do this subconsciously). This](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) process is called parsing .

[Although formal and natural languages have many features in common—tokens, struc-](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [ture, and syntax—there are some differences:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[ambiguity:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [Natural languages are full of ambiguity, which people deal with by using con-](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) textual clues and other information. Formal languages are designed to be nearly or completely unambiguous, which means that any statement has exactly one meaning, regardless of context.

[redundancy:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [In order to make up for ambiguity and reduce misunderstandings, natural](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [languages employ lots of redundancy. As a result, they are often verbose. Formal](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [languages are less redundant and more concise.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[literalness:](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) Natural languages are full of idiom and metaphor. If I say, “The penny [dropped”, there is probably no penny and nothing dropping (this idiom means that](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) someone understood something after a period of confusion). Formal languages mean exactly what they say.

[Because we all grow up speaking natural languages, it is sometimes hard to adjust to for-](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [mal languages. The difference between formal and natural language is like the difference](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [between poetry and prose, but more so:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Poetry:](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [Words are used for their sounds as well as for their meaning, and the whole poem](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [together creates an effect or emotional response. Ambiguity is not only common but](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [often deliberate.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

[Prose:](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [The literal meaning of words is more important, and the structure contributes more](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [meaning. Prose is more amenable to analysis than poetry but still often ambiguous.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[Programs:](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [The meaning of a computer program is unambiguous and literal, and can be](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) understood entirely by analysis of the tokens and structure.

Chapter 1. The way of the program

[Formal languages are more dense than natural languages, so it takes longer to read them.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Also, the structure is important, so it is not always best to read from top to bottom, left to](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [right. Instead, learn to parse the program in your head, identifying the tokens and inter-](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) preting the structure. Finally, the details matter. Small errors in spelling and punctuation, [which you can get away with in natural languages, can make a big difference in a formal](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) language.

## 1.7 [Debugging](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[Programmers make mistakes. For whimsical reasons, programming errors are called](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [bugs](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [and the process of tracking them down is called](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [debugging](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [.](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

Programming, and especially debugging, sometimes brings out strong emotions. If you are struggling with a difficult bug, you might feel angry, despondent, or embarrassed.

There is evidence that people naturally respond to computers as if they were people. When [they work well, we think of them as teammates, and when they are obstinate or rude, we](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [respond to them the same way we respond to rude, obstinate people (Reeves and Nass,](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) The Media Equation: How People Treat Computers, Television, and New Media Like Real People [and Places](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [).](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

Preparing for these reactions might help you deal with them. One approach is to think of [the computer as an employee with certain strengths, like speed and precision, and partic-](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [ular weaknesses, like lack of empathy and inability to grasp the big picture.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

Your job is to be a good manager: find ways to take advantage of the strengths and mitigate the weaknesses. And find ways to use your emotions to engage with the problem, without [letting your reactions interfere with your ability to work effectively.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[Learning to debug can be frustrating, but it is a valuable skill that is useful for many activ-](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [ities beyond programming. At the end of each chapter there is a section, like this one, with](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [my suggestions for debugging. I hope they help!](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

## 1.8 [Glossary](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

problem solving: The process of formulating a problem, finding a solution, and expressing it.

[high-level language:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [A programming language like Python that is designed to be easy for](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [humans to read and write.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

low-level language: [A programming language that is designed to be easy for a computer](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [to run; also called “machine language” or “assembly language”.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

portability: [A property of a program that can run on more than one kind of computer.](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py)

[interpreter:](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [A program that reads another program and executes it](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

prompt: [Characters displayed by the interpreter to indicate that it is ready to take input](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) from the user.

program: A set of instructions that specifies a computation.

### 1.9. Exercises

[print statement:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) An instruction that causes the Python interpreter to display a value on the screen.

[operator:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) A special symbol that represents a simple computation like addition, multipli- [cation, or string concatenation.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

value: [One of the basic units of data, like a number or string, that a program manipulates.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

[type:](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) A category of values. The types we have seen so far are integers (type [int](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [), floating-](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [point numbers (type](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [float](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [), and strings (type](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [str](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [).](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting)

[integer:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [A type that represents whole numbers.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

[floating-point:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [A type that represents numbers with fractional parts.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[string:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) A type that represents sequences of characters.

natural language: Any one of the languages that people speak that evolved naturally.

[formal language:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [Any one of the languages that people have designed for specific pur-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) poses, such as representing mathematical ideas or computer programs; all program- [ming languages are formal languages.](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

[token:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [One of the basic elements of the syntactic structure of a program, analogous to a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [word in a natural language.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

syntax: [The rules that govern the structure of a program.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[parse:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) To examine a program and analyze the syntactic structure.

[bug:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [An error in a program.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

[debugging:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [The process of finding and correcting bugs.](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

## [1.9](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) Exercises

[Exercise 1.1.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) It is a good idea to read this book in front of a computer so you can try out the examples as you go.

[Whenever you are experimenting with a new feature, you should try to make mistakes. For example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) in the “Hello, world!” program, what happens if you leave out one of the quotation marks? What if [you leave out both? What if you spell](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [print](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [wrong?](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

[This kind of experiment helps you remember what you read; it also helps when you are programming,](http://gutenberg.org/) [because you get to know what the error messages mean. It is better to make mistakes now and on](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [purpose than later and accidentally.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

## [1. In a print statement, what happens if you leave out one of the parentheses, or both?](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

## [Chapter 2](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

# [Variables, expressions and](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [statements](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

One of the most powerful features of a programming language is the ability to manipulate [variables](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [. A variable is a name that refers to a value.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

## [2.1](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [Assignment statements](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[An](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) assignment statement creates a new variable and gives it a value: >>> message = ' And now for something completely different ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [>>> n = 17](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [>>> pi = 3.1415926535897932](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [This example makes three assignments. The first assigns a string to a new variable named](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [message](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [; the second gives the integer](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [to](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [; the third assigns the (approximate) value of](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [π](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [to](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [pi](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[A common way to represent variables on paper is to write the name with an arrow pointing](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) to its value. This kind of figure is called a state diagram because it shows what state each of the variables is in (think of it as the variable’s state of mind). Figure 2.1 shows the result of the previous example.

## [2.2](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Variable names](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Programmers generally choose names for their variables that are meaningful—they docu-](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [ment what the variable is used for.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

### [message](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [’And now for something completely different’](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

[n](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

### [pi](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [3.1415926535897932](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[Figure 2.1: State diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py)

Chapter 2. Variables, expressions and statements

[Variable names can be as long as you like. They can contain both letters and numbers, but](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [they can’t begin with a number. It is legal to use uppercase letters, but it is conventional to](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [use only lower case for variables names.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[The underscore character,](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [_](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) , can appear in a name. It is often used in names with multiple words, such as your_name or [airspeed_of_unladen_swallow](https://thinkpython.com/code) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code)

If you give a variable an illegal name, you get a syntax error:

```
>>> 76trombones = ' big parade '
SyntaxError: invalid syntax
>>> more@ = 1000000
SyntaxError: invalid syntax
>>> class = ' Advanced Theoretical Zymurgy '
SyntaxError: invalid syntax
```

76trombones is illegal because it begins with a number. more@ is illegal because it contains [an illegal character,](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [@](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [. But what’s wrong with](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [class](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [?](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

It turns out that class [is one of Python’s](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [keywords](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) The interpreter uses keywords to [recognize the structure of the program, and they cannot be used as variable names.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[Python 3 has these keywords:](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

| False | class    | finally | is       | return |
| ----- | -------- | ------- | -------- | ------ |
| None  | continue | for     | lambda   | try    |
| True  | def      | from    | nonlocal | while  |
| and   | del      | global  | not      | with   |
| as    | elif     | if      | or       | yield  |

[You don’t have to memorize this list. In most development environments, keywords are](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [displayed in a different color; if you try to use one as a variable name, you’ll know.](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

## [2.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) Expressions and statements

An expression is a combination of values, variables, and operators. A value all by itself is considered an expression, and so is a variable, so the following are all legal expressions:

### >>> 42

### >>> n

### >>> n + 25

[When you type an expression at the prompt, the interpreter](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [evaluates](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [it, which means that](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [it finds the value of the expression. In this example,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [has the value 17 and](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [n + 25](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) has the value 42.

A statement [is a unit of code that has an effect, like creating a variable or displaying a](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) value.

### >>> n = 17 >>> print(n)

### 2.4. Script mode

[The first line is an assignment statement that gives a value to](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) n . The second line is a print statement that displays the value of [n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[When you type a statement, the interpreter](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) executes [it, which means that it does whatever](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [the statement says. In general, statements don’t have values.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

## [2.4](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [Script mode](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[So far we have run Python in](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [interactive mode](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [, which means that you interact directly](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [with the interpreter. Interactive mode is a good way to get started, but if you are working](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [with more than a few lines of code, it can be clumsy.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

The alternative is to save code in a file called a [script](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [and then run the interpreter in](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [script](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [mode](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) to execute the script. By convention, Python scripts have names that end with .py .

[If you know how to create and run a script on your computer, you are ready to go. Oth-](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [erwise I recommend using PythonAnywhere again. I have posted instructions for running](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [in script mode at](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

Because Python provides both modes, you can test bits of code in interactive mode before [you put them in a script. But there are differences between interactive mode and script](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [mode that can be confusing.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[For example, if you are using Python as a calculator, you might type](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

### [>>> miles = 26.2](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [>>> miles * 1.61](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [42.182](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[The first line assigns a value to](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) miles , but it has no visible effect. The second line is an ex- [pression, so the interpreter evaluates it and displays the result. It turns out that a marathon](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [is about 42 kilometers.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[But if you type the same code into a script and run it, you get no output at all. In script](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [mode an expression, all by itself, has no visible effect. Python evaluates the expression, but](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) it doesn’t display the result. To display the result, you need a print statement like this:

### [miles = 26.2](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) print(miles * 1.61)

This behavior can be confusing at first. To check your understanding, type the following [statements in the Python interpreter and see what they do:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

### x = 5 [x + 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Now put the same statements in a script and run it. What is the output? Modify the script](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [by transforming each expression into a print statement and then run it again.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

## [2.5](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [Order of operations](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

[When an expression contains more than one operator, the order of evaluation depends](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [on the](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [order of operations](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [For mathematical operators, Python follows mathematical](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) convention. The acronym PEMDAS is a useful way to remember the rules:

Chapter 2. Variables, expressions and statements

- [•](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [P](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [arentheses have the highest precedence and can be used to force an expression to](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [evaluate in the order you want. Since expressions in parentheses are evaluated first,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [2 * (3-1)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [is 4, and](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [(1+1)**(5-2)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [is 8.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) You can also use parentheses to make an [expression easier to read, as in](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) (minute * 100) / 60 , even if it doesn’t change the result.

- E [xponentiation has the next highest precedence, so](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [1 + 2**3](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [is 9, not 27, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [2 *](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [3**2](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [is 18, not 36.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

- M [ultiplication and](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [D](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [ivision have higher precedence than](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [A](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [ddition and](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [S](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [ubtraction.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) So [2*3-1](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [is 5, not 4, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [6+4/2](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [is 8, not 5.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py)

- [• Operators with the same precedence are evaluated from left to right (except exponen-](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [tiation). So in the expression](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [degrees / 2 * pi](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [, the division happens first and the](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [result is multiplied by](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) pi . To divide by 2 π , you can use parentheses or write degrees [/ 2 / pi](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[I don’t work very hard to remember the precedence of operators. If I can’t tell by looking](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [at the expression, I use parentheses to make it obvious.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

## 2.6 String operations

In general, you can’t perform mathematical operations on strings, even if the strings look [like numbers, so the following are illegal:](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

```
' chinese ' - ' food ' ' eggs ' / ' easy ' ' third ' * ' a charm '
```

[But there are two exceptions,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [+](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) * .

The + [operator performs](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [string concatenation](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [, which means it joins the strings by linking](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [them end-to-end. For example:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

```
>>> first = ' throat '
>>> second = ' warbler '
>>> first + second
throatwarbler
```

The * operator also works on strings; it performs repetition. For example, ' Spam ' *3 is ' SpamSpamSpam ' . If one of the values is a string, the other has to be an integer.

This use of [+](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [and](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [*](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [makes sense by analogy with addition and multiplication. Just as](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) 4*3 is equivalent to 4+4+4 [, we expect](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [Spam](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [*3](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [to be the same as](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [Spam](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) Spam ' + ' Spam ' , and [it is. On the other hand, there is a significant way in which string concatenation and repe-](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [tition are different from integer addition and multiplication. Can you think of a property](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [that addition has that string concatenation does not?](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

## [2.7](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [Comments](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

[As programs get bigger and more complicated, they get more difficult to read.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [Formal](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [languages are dense, and it is often difficult to look at a piece of code and figure out what](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) it is doing, or why.

### 2.8. Debugging

[For this reason, it is a good idea to add notes to your programs to explain in natural lan-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [guage what the program is doing. These notes are called](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [comments](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [, and they start with](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [the](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [symbol:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) # compute the percentage of the hour that has elapsed [percentage = (minute * 100) / 60](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [In this case, the comment appears on a line by itself. You can also put comments at the end](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [of a line:](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [percentage = (minute * 100) / 60](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [# percentage of an hour](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [Everything from the](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [to the end of the line is ignored—it has no effect on the execution of](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [the program.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[Comments are most useful when they document non-obvious features of the code. It is](http://www.thinkpython.com/) reasonable to assume that the reader can figure out [what](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [the code does; it is more useful to](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [explain](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [why](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

[This comment is redundant with the code and useless:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [v = 5](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [# assign 5 to v](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [This comment contains useful information that is not in the code:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) v = 5 # velocity in meters/second. [Good variable names can reduce the need for comments, but long names can make com-](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [plex expressions hard to read, so there is a tradeoff.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

## [2.8](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Debugging](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

Three kinds of errors can occur in a program: syntax errors, runtime errors, and semantic errors. It is useful to distinguish between them in order to track them down more quickly.

[Syntax error:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [“Syntax” refers to the structure of a program and the rules about that struc-](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [ture. For example, parentheses have to come in matching pairs, so](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [(1 + 2)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) is legal, [but](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [8)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [is a](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [syntax error](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[If there is a syntax error anywhere in your program, Python displays an error mes-](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [sage and quits, and you will not be able to run the program. During the first few](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) weeks of your programming career, you might spend a lot of time tracking down syntax errors. As you gain experience, you will make fewer errors and find them faster.

Runtime error: [The second type of error is a runtime error, so called because the error does](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [not appear until after the program has started running. These errors are also called](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [exceptions](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) because they usually indicate that something exceptional (and bad) has happened.

[Runtime errors are rare in the simple programs you will see in the first few chapters,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [so it might be a while before you encounter one.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[Semantic error:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [The third type of error is “semantic”, which means related to meaning.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [If there is a semantic error in your program, it will run without generating error](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [messages, but it will not do the right thing. It will do something else. Specifically, it](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [will do what you told it to do.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[Identifying semantic errors can be tricky because it requires you to work backward](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) by looking at the output of the program and trying to figure out what it is doing.

Chapter 2. Variables, expressions and statements

## [2.9](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

variable: A name that refers to a value.

[assignment:](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [A statement that assigns a value to a variable.](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

state diagram: [A graphical representation of a set of variables and the values they refer to.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

[keyword:](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) A reserved word that is used to parse a program; you cannot use keywords like if , [def](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [while](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [as variable names.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

operand: [One of the values on which an operator operates.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

expression: [A combination of variables, operators, and values that represents a single re-](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [sult.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

evaluate: [To simplify an expression by performing the operations in order to yield a single](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [value.](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

statement: [A section of code that represents a command or action. So far, the statements](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) we have seen are assignments and print statements.

[execute:](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [To run a statement and do what it says.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[interactive mode:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [A way of using the Python interpreter by typing code at the prompt.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[script mode:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [A way of using the Python interpreter to read code from a script and run it.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

script: [A program stored in a file.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[order of operations:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) Rules governing the order in which expressions involving multiple [operators and operands are evaluated.](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py)

concatenate: [To join two operands end-to-end.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

comment: [Information in a program that is meant for other programmers (or anyone read-](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) ing the source code) and has no effect on the execution of the program.

syntax error: An error in a program that makes it impossible to parse (and therefore impossible to interpret).

exception: [An error that is detected while the program is running.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

semantics: [The meaning of a program.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

semantic error: [An error in a program that makes it do something other than what the](http://gutenberg.org/) programmer intended.

## [2.10](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [Exercises](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

Exercise 2.1. [Repeating my advice from the previous chapter, whenever you learn a new feature,](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [you should try it out in interactive mode and make errors on purpose to see what goes wrong.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

- We’ve seen that n = 42 is legal. What about 42 = n ?

### 2.10. Exercises

- [•](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [How about](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [x = y = 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [?](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

- [In some languages every statement ends with a semi-colon,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [;](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [. What happens if you put a](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [semi-colon at the end of a Python statement?](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

- [•](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [What if you put a period at the end of a statement?](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

- In math notation you can multiply x and y like this: xy. What happens if you try that in [Python?](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)
