## [Chapter 3](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set)

# [Functions](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[In the context of programming, a](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [function](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) is a named sequence of statements that performs [a computation. When you define a function, you specify the name and the sequence of](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) statements. Later, you can “call” the function by name.

## [3.1](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [Function calls](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[We have already seen one example of a](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [function call](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

### >>> type(42) [<class](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) ['](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [int](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) ['](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [>](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[The name of the function is](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [type](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [. The expression in parentheses is called the](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [argument](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [of](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [the function. The result, for this function, is the type of the argument.](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[It is common to say that a function “takes” an argument and “returns” a result. The result](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [is also called the](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [return value](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

Python provides functions that convert values from one type to another. The int function [takes any value and converts it to an integer, if it can, or complains otherwise:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

### >>> int( ' ' )

### >>> int( ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [Hello](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ValueError: invalid literal for int(): Hello

[int](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [can convert floating-point values to integers, but it doesn’t round off; it chops off the](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [fraction part:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### >>> int(3.99999)

### [>>> int(-2.3)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [-2](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[float](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [converts integers and strings to floating-point numbers:](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

### [>>> float(32)](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [32.0](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [>>> float(](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) ['](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [3.14159](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) ['](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [)](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) 3.14159

Chapter 3. Functions

[Finally,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [str](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [converts its argument to a string:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### >>> str(32) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [>>> str(3.14159)](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) ' 3.14159 ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

## 3.2 [Math functions](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[Python has a math module that provides most of the familiar mathematical functions. A](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) module [is a file that contains a collection of related functions.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py)

Before we can use the functions in a module, we have to import it with an import statement :

### [>>> import math](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

[This statement creates a](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [module object](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [named math. If you display the module object, you](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) get some information about it:

### [>>> math](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [<module](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [math](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [(built-in)>](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[The module object contains the functions and variables defined in the module. To access](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [one of the functions, you have to specify the name of the module and the name of the](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) function, separated by a dot (also known as a period). This format is called dot notation .

### [>>> ratio = signal_power / noise_power](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [>>> decibels = 10 * math.log10(ratio)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

### [>>> radians = 0.7](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [>>> height = math.sin(radians)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[The first example uses](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [math.log10](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [to compute a signal-to-noise ratio in decibels (assuming](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [that](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [signal_power](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) and noise_power are defined). The math module also provides log , [which computes logarithms base](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [e](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

The second example finds the sine of radians . The variable name radians is a hint that sin and the other trigonometric functions ( cos , tan , etc.) take arguments in radians. To [convert from degrees to radians, divide by 180 and multiply by](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [π](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

### >>> degrees = 45 [>>> radians = degrees / 180.0 * math.pi](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) >>> math.sin(radians) 0.707106781187

The expression [math.pi](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [gets the variable](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [pi](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [from the math module. Its value is a floating-](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [point approximation of](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [π](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [, accurate to about 15 digits.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[If you know trigonometry, you can check the previous result by comparing it to the square](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [root of two, divided by two:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

### [>>> math.sqrt(2) / 2.0](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) 0.707106781187

### 3.3. Composition

## [3.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Composition](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

So far, [we have looked at the elements of a program—variables,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [expressions,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorting_algorithm) [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorting_algorithm) statements—in isolation, without talking about how to combine them.

[One of the most useful features of programming languages is their ability to take small](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) building blocks and [compose](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [them. For example, the argument of a function can be any](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [kind of expression, including arithmetic operators:](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

### x = math.sin(degrees / 360.0 * 2 * math.pi)

[And even function calls:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

### [x = math.exp(math.log(x+1))](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[Almost anywhere you can put a value, you can put an arbitrary expression, with one ex-](http://www.thinkpython.com/) ception: the left side of an assignment statement has to be a variable name. [Any other](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) expression on the left side is a syntax error (we will see exceptions to this rule later).

### [>>> minutes = hours * 60](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [# right](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [>>> hours * 60 = minutes](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) # wrong! [SyntaxError: can](https://thinkpython.com/code/zipf.py) ['](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [t assign to operator](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

## [3.4](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [Adding new functions](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[So far, we have only been using the functions that come with Python, but it is also possible](https://thinkpython.com/code/GoodKangaroo.py) to add new functions. A function definition [specifies the name of a new function and the](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [sequence of statements that run when the function is called.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[Here is an example:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

### def print_lyrics(): [print("I](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [m a lumberjack, and I](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [m okay.")](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [print("I sleep all night and I work all day.")](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[def](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [is a keyword that indicates that this is a function definition. The name of the function](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [is](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [print_lyrics](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [. The rules for function names are the same as for variable names: letters,](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [numbers and underscore are legal, but the first character can’t be a number. You can’t use a](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) keyword as the name of a function, and you should avoid having a variable and a function [with the same name.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

The empty parentheses after the name indicate that this function doesn’t take any arguments.

The first line of the function definition is called the header [; the rest is called the](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [body](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [. The](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [header has to end with a colon and the body has to be indented. By convention, indentation](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [is always four spaces. The body can contain any number of statements.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[The strings in the print statements are enclosed in double quotes. Single quotes and double](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [quotes do the same thing; most people use single quotes except in cases like this where a](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [single quote (which is also an apostrophe) appears in the string.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

[All quotation marks (single and double) must be “straight quotes”, usually located next](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [to Enter on the keyboard. “Curly quotes”, like the ones in this sentence, are not legal in](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [Python.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[If you type a function definition in interactive mode, the interpreter prints dots (](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) [...](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) [) to let](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) you know that the definition isn’t complete:

Chapter 3. Functions

[>>> def print_lyrics():](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ... print("I ' m a lumberjack, and I ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [m okay.")](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [...](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [print("I sleep all night and I work all day.")](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [...](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

[To end the function, you have to enter an empty line.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

Defining a function creates a function object , which has type function :

### [>>> print(print_lyrics)](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [<function print_lyrics at 0xb7e99e9c>](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [>>> type(print_lyrics)](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) <class ' [function](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [>](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[The syntax for calling the new function is the same as for built-in functions:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[>>> print_lyrics()](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) I ' [m a lumberjack, and I](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [m okay.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) I sleep all night and I work all day.

[Once you have defined a function, you can use it inside another function. For example, to](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [repeat the previous refrain, we could write a function called](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) repeat_lyrics :

### [def repeat_lyrics():](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [print_lyrics()](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [print_lyrics()](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[And then call](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [repeat_lyrics](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[>>> repeat_lyrics()](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [I](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) [m a lumberjack, and I](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) ' m okay. [I sleep all night and I work all day.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) I ' [m a lumberjack, and I](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [m okay.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [I sleep all night and I work all day.](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py)

[But that’s not really how the song goes.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

## [3.5](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [Definitions and uses](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

Pulling together the code fragments from the previous section, the whole program looks like this:

### def print_lyrics(): print("I ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [m a lumberjack, and I](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [m okay.")](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [print("I sleep all night and I work all day.")](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

### [def repeat_lyrics():](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [print_lyrics()](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [print_lyrics()](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

### [repeat_lyrics()](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

[This program contains two function definitions:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [print_lyrics](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) repeat_lyrics . Func- [tion definitions get executed just like other statements, but the effect is to create function](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [objects. The statements inside the function do not run until the function is called, and the](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) function definition generates no output.

### 3.6. Flow of execution

[As you might expect, you have to create a function before you can run it. In other words,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [the function definition has to run before the function gets called.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[As an exercise, move the last line of this program to the top, so the function call appears](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [before the definitions. Run the program and see what error message you get.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[Now move the function call back to the bottom and move the definition of](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) print_lyrics after the definition of repeat_lyrics . What happens when you run this program?

## [3.6](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [Flow of execution](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[To ensure that a function is defined before its first use, you have to know the order state-](http://www.thinkpython.com/) ments run in, which is called the [flow of execution](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Execution always begins at the first statement of the program. Statements are run one at a](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) time, in order from top to bottom.

[Function definitions do not alter the flow of execution of the program, but remember that](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [statements inside the function don’t run until the function is called.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

[A function call is like a detour in the flow of execution. Instead of going to the next state-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [ment, the flow jumps to the body of the function, runs the statements there, and then comes](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [back to pick up where it left off.](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

[That sounds simple enough, until you remember that one function can call another. While](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [in the middle of one function, the program might have to run the statements in another](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) function. Then, while running that new function, the program might have to run yet another function!

[Fortunately, Python is good at keeping track of where it is, so each time a function com-](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) [pletes, the program picks up where it left off in the function that called it. When it gets to](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [the end of the program, it terminates.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[In summary, when you read a program, you don’t always want to read from top to bottom.](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) Sometimes it makes more sense if you follow the flow of execution.

## 3.7 Parameters and arguments

Some of the functions we have seen require arguments. [For example, when you call](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [math.sin](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [you pass a number as an argument.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) Some functions take more than one ar- [gument:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [math.pow](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [takes two, the base and the exponent.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Inside the function, the arguments are assigned to variables called](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [parameters](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [. Here is a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [definition for a function that takes an argument:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

### [def print_twice(bruce):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [print(bruce)](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) print(bruce)

[This function assigns the argument to a parameter named](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) bruce . When the function is [called, it prints the value of the parameter (whatever it is) twice.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

This function works with any value that can be printed.

Chapter 3. Functions

### [>>> print_twice(](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Spam](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) Spam [Spam](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [>>> print_twice(42)](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

### >>> print_twice(math.pi) [3.14159265359](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [3.14159265359](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[The same rules of composition that apply to built-in functions also apply to programmer-](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [defined functions, so we can use any kind of expression as an argument for](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [print_twice](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [:](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format)

>>> print_twice( ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [Spam](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) ' *4) [Spam Spam Spam Spam](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [Spam Spam Spam Spam](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [>>> print_twice(math.cos(math.pi))](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) -1.0 -1.0

[The argument is evaluated before the function is called, so in the examples the expressions](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [Spam](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [*4](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [math.cos(math.pi)](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [are only evaluated once.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[You can also use a variable as an argument:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[>>> michael =](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) Eric, the half a bee. ['](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [>>> print_twice(michael)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Eric, the half a bee.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) Eric, the half a bee.

[The name of the variable we pass as an argument (](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [michael](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [) has nothing to do with the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [name of the parameter (](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [bruce](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [). It doesn’t matter what the value was called back home (in](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [the caller); here in](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [print_twice](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [, we call everybody](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [bruce](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

## [3.8](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [Variables and parameters are local](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

When you create a variable inside a function, it is local , which means that it only exists inside the function. For example:

### def cat_twice(part1, part2): [cat = part1 + part2](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [print_twice(cat)](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[This function takes two arguments, concatenates them, and prints the result twice. Here is](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [an example that uses it:](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

>>> line1 = ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [Bing tiddle](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) >>> line2 = ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [tiddle bang.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [>>> cat_twice(line1, line2)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [Bing tiddle tiddle bang.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [Bing tiddle tiddle bang.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

When [cat_twice](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [terminates, the variable](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [cat](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [is destroyed. If we try to print it, we get an](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) exception:

### 3.9. Stack diagrams

### [line1](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [’Bing tiddle ’](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [__main__](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [line2](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [’tiddle bang.’](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

### part1 ’Bing tiddle ’

### cat_twice [part2](https://thinkpython.com/code) [’tiddle bang.’](https://thinkpython.com/code)

### [cat](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [’Bing tiddle tiddle bang.’](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

### print_twice bruce [’Bing tiddle tiddle bang.’](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py)

[Figure 3.1: Stack diagram.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

### [>>> print(cat)](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [NameError: name](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) ' cat ' is not defined

Parameters are also local. [For example, outside](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [print_twice](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [, there is no such thing as](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [bruce](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [.](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

## [3.9](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [Stack diagrams](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[To keep track of which variables can be used where, it is sometimes useful to draw a](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [stack](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [diagram](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [. Like state diagrams, stack diagrams show the value of each variable, but they](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [also show the function each variable belongs to.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[Each function is represented by a](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) frame . [A frame is a box with the name of a function](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) beside it and the parameters and variables of the function inside it. The stack diagram for [the previous example is shown in Figure 3.1.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

The frames are arranged in a stack that indicates which function called which, and so [on.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [In this example,](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [print_twice](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [was called by](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [cat_twice](http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=k4RRi_ntQc8) [, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [cat_twice](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [was called](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [by](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [__main__](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [, which is a special name for the topmost frame. When you create a variable](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) outside of any function, it belongs to __main__ .

Each parameter refers to the same value as its corresponding argument. So, part1 has the same value as line1 , part2 has the same value as line2 , and bruce has the same value as cat .

If an error occurs during a function call, Python prints the name of the function, the name [of the function that called it, and the name of the function that called](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) that , all the way back [to](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [__main__](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[For example, if you try to access](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [cat](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [from within](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [print_twice](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) , you get a NameError :

[Traceback (innermost last):](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [File "test.py", line 13, in __main__](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [cat_twice(line1, line2)](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [File "test.py", line 5, in cat_twice](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [print_twice(cat)](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [File "test.py", line 9, in print_twice](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [print(cat)](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) NameError: name ' cat ' is not defined

Chapter 3. Functions

[This list of functions is called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [traceback](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) . It tells you what program file the error occurred [in, and what line, and what functions were executing at the time. It also shows the line of](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [code that caused the error.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[The order of the functions in the traceback is the same as the order of the frames in the](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) stack diagram. The function that is currently running is at the bottom.

## 3.10 [Fruitful functions and void functions](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[Some of the functions we have used, such as the math functions, return results; for lack of](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [a better name, I call them](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [fruitful functions](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [. Other functions, like](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [print_twice](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) , perform an action but don’t return a value. They are called [void functions](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

[When you call a fruitful function, you almost always want to do something with the result;](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) for example, you might assign it to a variable or use it as part of an expression:

### x = math.cos(radians) [golden = (math.sqrt(5) + 1) / 2](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[When you call a function in interactive mode, Python displays the result:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal)

### [>>> math.sqrt(5)](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [2.2360679774997898](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

But in a script, if you call a fruitful function all by itself, the return value is lost forever!

### [math.sqrt(5)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py)

This script computes the square root of 5, but since it doesn’t store or display the result, it is not very useful.

[Void functions might display something on the screen or have some other effect, but they](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [don’t have a return value. If you assign the result to a variable, you get a special value](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [called](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [None](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

```
>>> result = print_twice( ' Bing ' )
Bing
Bing
>>> print(result)
None
```

The value None [is not the same as the string](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [None](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [. It is a special value that has its own](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) type:

### >>> type(None) <class ' NoneType ' >

[The functions we have written so far are all void. We will start writing fruitful functions in](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) a few chapters.

## 3.11 [Why functions?](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[It may not be clear why it is worth the trouble to divide a program into functions. There](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) are several reasons:

### 3.12. Debugging

- [• Creating a new function gives you an opportunity to name a group of statements,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [which makes your program easier to read and debug.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

- Functions can make a program smaller by eliminating repetitive code. Later, if you [make a change, you only have to make it in one place.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

- [• Dividing a long program into functions allows you to debug the parts one at a time](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [and then assemble them into a working whole.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

- Well-designed functions are often useful for many programs. [Once you write and](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [debug one, you can reuse it.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

## [3.12](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) Debugging

[One of the most important skills you will acquire is debugging. Although it can be frus-](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) trating, debugging is one of the most intellectually rich, challenging, and interesting parts of programming.

In some ways debugging is like detective work. You are confronted with clues and you [have to infer the processes and events that led to the results you see.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal)

[Debugging is also like an experimental science. Once you have an idea about what is going](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [wrong, you modify your program and try again. If your hypothesis was correct, you can](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) predict the result of the modification, and you take a step closer to a working program. If [your hypothesis was wrong, you have to come up with a new one. As Sherlock Holmes](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) pointed out, “When you have eliminated the impossible, whatever remains, however im- [probable, must be the truth.” (A. Conan Doyle,](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) The Sign of Four [)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

[For some people, programming and debugging are the same thing. That is, programming](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [is the process of gradually debugging a program until it does what you want. The idea is](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [that you should start with a working program and make small modifications, debugging](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [them as you go.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

For example, Linux is an operating system that contains millions of lines of code, but it [started out as a simple program Linus Torvalds used to explore the Intel 80386 chip. Ac-](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) cording to Larry Greenfield, “One of Linus’s earlier projects was a program that would switch between printing AAAA and BBBB. This later evolved to Linux.” ( The Linux Users’ Guide Beta Version 1).

## [3.13](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[function:](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [A named sequence of statements that performs some useful operation. Func-](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [tions may or may not take arguments and may or may not produce a result.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[function definition:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [A statement that creates a new function, specifying its name, param-](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [eters, and the statements it contains.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

[function object:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [A value created by a function definition. The name of the function is a](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [variable that refers to a function object.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

header: The first line of a function definition.

Chapter 3. Functions

[body:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [The sequence of statements inside a function definition.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

parameter: [A name used inside a function to refer to the value passed as an argument.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[function call:](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) A statement that runs a function. It consists of the function name followed [by an argument list in parentheses.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

argument: A value provided to a function when the function is called. This value is as- [signed to the corresponding parameter in the function.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

local variable: [A variable defined inside a function.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [A local variable can only be used](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [inside its function.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[return value:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [The result of a function. If a function call is used as an expression, the return](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [value is the value of the expression.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

fruitful function: A function that returns a value.

[void function:](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [A function that always returns](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) None .

None : [A special value returned by void functions.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

module: [A file that contains a collection of related functions and other definitions.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal)

[import statement:](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [A statement that reads a module file and creates a module object.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[module object:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [A value created by an](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [import](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [statement that provides access to the values](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [defined in a module.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

[dot notation:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) The syntax for calling a function in another module by specifying the mod- [ule name followed by a dot (period) and the function name.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

composition: [Using an expression as part of a larger expression, or a statement as part of](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [a larger statement.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[flow of execution:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [The order statements run in.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[stack diagram:](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) A graphical representation of a stack of functions, their variables, and the [values they refer to.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

frame: A box in a stack diagram that represents a function call. It contains the local variables and parameters of the function.

traceback: [A list of the functions that are executing, printed when an exception occurs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

By default, print [advances to the next line, but you can override that behavior and put a](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [space at the end, like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

```
print( ' + ' , end= ' ' )
print( ' - ' )
```

# [Case study: interface design](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

## [4.1](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) The turtle module

To check whether you have the turtle module, open the Python interpreter and type

### >>> import turtle [>>> bob = turtle.Turtle()](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

When you run this code, it should create a new window with small arrow that represents [the turtle. Close the window.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

Create a file named mypolygon.py [and type in the following code:](http://gutenberg.org/)

### [import turtle](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [bob = turtle.Turtle()](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [print(bob)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [turtle.mainloop()](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

[The](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [turtle](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [module (with a lowercase ’t’) provides a function called](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [Turtle](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [(with an up-](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [percase ’T’) that creates a Turtle object, which we assign to a variable named](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) bob . Printing bob displays something like:

### <turtle.Turtle object at 0xb7bfbf4c>

```
Chapter 4. Case study: interface design
```

[This means that](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [bob](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [refers to an object with type](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Turtle](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) as defined in module turtle .

mainloop [tells the window to wait for the user to do something, although in this case](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [there’s not much for the user to do except close the window.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Once you create a Turtle, you can call a](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [method](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [to move it around the window. A method](https://thinkpython.com/code) [is similar to a function, but it uses slightly different syntax. For example, to move the turtle](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [forward:](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

### [bob.fd(100)](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

The method, [fd](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [, is associated with the turtle object we’re calling](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [bob](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [. Calling a method is](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [like making a request: you are asking](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [bob](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [to move forward.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

The argument of [fd](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [is a distance in pixels, so the actual size depends on your display.](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

[Other methods you can call on a Turtle are](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) bk to move backward, lt for left turn, and rt [right turn. The argument for](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [lt](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [rt](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [is an angle in degrees.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[Also, each Turtle is holding a pen, which is either down or up; if the pen is down, the Turtle](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [leaves a trail when it moves. The methods](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [pu](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [pd](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) stand for “pen up” and “pen down”.

[To draw a right angle, add these lines to the program (after creating](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [bob](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [and before calling](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [mainloop](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [):](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

### bob.fd(100) [bob.lt(90)](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [bob.fd(100)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py)

When you run this program, you should see bob move east and then north, leaving two [line segments behind.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[Now modify the program to draw a square. Don’t go on until you’ve got it working!](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

## 4.2 [Simple repetition](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[Chances are you wrote something like this:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

### bob.fd(100) bob.lt(90)

### bob.fd(100) bob.lt(90)

### bob.fd(100) bob.lt(90)

### bob.fd(100)

[We can do the same thing more concisely with a](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [for](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [statement.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [Add this example to](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) mypolygon.py and run it again:

### [for i in range(4):](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) print( ' Hello! ' )

You should see something like this:

### 4.3. Exercises

[Hello!](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) Hello! [Hello!](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Hello!](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [This is the simplest use of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) statement; we will see more later. But that should be [enough to let you rewrite your square-drawing program. Don’t go on until you do.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

[Here is a](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [for](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [statement that draws a square:](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [for i in range(4):](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [bob.fd(100)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [bob.lt(90)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [The syntax of a](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [statement is similar to a function definition. It has a header that ends](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [with a colon and an indented body. The body can contain any number of statements.](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

[A](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) statement is also called a loop because the flow of execution runs through the body [and then loops back to the top. In this case, it runs the body four times.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[This version is actually a little different from the previous square-drawing code because it](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [makes another turn after drawing the last side of the square. The extra turn takes more](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) time, but it simplifies the code if we do the same thing every time through the loop. This [version also has the effect of leaving the turtle back in the starting position, facing in the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [starting direction.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

## [4.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [Exercises](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

The following is a series of exercises using the turtle module. They are meant to be fun, but they have a point, too. While you are working on them, think about what the point is.

[The following sections have solutions to the exercises, so don’t look until you have finished](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [(or at least tried).](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

1. [1. Write a function called](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [square](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [that takes a parameter named](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [t](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [, which is a turtle. It](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [should use the turtle to draw a square.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

Write a function call that passes bob as an argument to square , and then run the [program again.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

2. Add another parameter, named length , to square . Modify the body so length of the sides is length , and then modify the function call to provide a second argument. Run [the program again. Test your program with a range of values for](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) length .

3. [3. Make a copy of](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [square](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [and change the name to](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) polygon . Add another parameter named [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [and modify the body so it draws an n-sided regular polygon. Hint: The](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [exterior angles of an n-sided regular polygon are 360/](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [n](http://gutenberg.org/) [degrees.](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py)

4. [4. Write a function called](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [circle](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [that takes a turtle,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) t [, and radius,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [r](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [, as parameters and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [that draws an approximate circle by calling](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [polygon](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [with an appropriate length and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [number of sides. Test your function with a range of values of](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) [r](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

[Hint: figure out the circumference of the circle and make sure that](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [length * n =](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) circumference [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

5. [5. Make a more general version of](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [circle](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [called](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [arc](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [that takes an additional parameter](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newline) [angle](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [, which determines what fraction of a circle to draw.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [angle](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) [is in units of degrees,](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) so when angle=360 , arc should draw a complete circle.

```
Chapter 4. Case study: interface design
```

## [4.4](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Encapsulation](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[The first exercise asks you to put your square-drawing code into a function definition and](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [then call the function, passing the turtle as a parameter. Here is a solution:](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

### def square(t): for i in range(4): [t.fd(100)](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [t.lt(90)](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

### square(bob)

[The innermost statements,](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [fd](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [lt](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [are indented twice to show that they are inside the](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) for [loop, which is inside the function definition. The next line,](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [square(bob)](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [, is flush with](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) the left margin, which indicates the end of both the for loop and the function definition.

[Inside the function,](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [t](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [refers to the same turtle](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [bob](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [, so](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [t.lt(90)](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [has the same effect as](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [bob.lt(90)](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) . In that case, why not call the parameter bob ? The idea is that t can be any turtle, not just [bob](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [, so you could create a second turtle and pass it as an argument to](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) square [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

### alice = turtle.Turtle() square(alice)

[Wrapping a piece of code up in a function is called](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [encapsulation](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [. One of the benefits of](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [encapsulation is that it attaches a name to the code, which serves as a kind of documenta-](https://thinkpython.com/code/GoodKangaroo.py) tion. Another advantage is that if you re-use the code, it is more concise to call a function [twice than to copy and paste the body!](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

## 4.5 Generalization

[The next step is to add a](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [length](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [parameter to](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [square](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) . Here is a solution:

### def square(t, length): [for i in range(4):](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [t.fd(length)](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [t.lt(90)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

### square(bob, 100)

[Adding a parameter to a function is called](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [generalization](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [because it makes the function](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [more general: in the previous version, the square is always the same size; in this version it](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) can be any size.

[The next step is also a generalization. Instead of drawing squares,](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) polygon draws regular [polygons with any number of sides. Here is a solution:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

### [def polygon(t, n, length):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [angle = 360 / n](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [for i in range(n):](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [t.fd(length)](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [t.lt(angle)](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py)

### polygon(bob, 7, 70)

### 4.6. Interface design

[This example draws a 7-sided polygon with side length 70.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

If you are using Python 2, the value of [angle](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [might be off because of integer division. A](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [simple solution is to compute](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [angle = 360.0 / n](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [. Because the numerator is a floating-](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [point number, the result is floating point.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[When a function has more than a few numeric arguments, it is easy to forget what they are,](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) or what order they should be in. In that case it is often a good idea to include the names of [the parameters in the argument list:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

### [polygon(bob, n=7, length=70)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[These are called](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [keyword arguments](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) because they include the parameter names as “key- [words” (not to be confused with Python keywords like](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) while [and](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [def](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [).](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format)

[This syntax makes the program more readable. It is also a reminder about how arguments](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) and parameters work: when you call a function, the arguments are assigned to the param- [eters.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

## 4.6 Interface design

[The next step is to write](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [circle](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [, which takes a radius,](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [r](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [, as a parameter. Here is a simple](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [solution that uses](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [polygon](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [to draw a 50-sided polygon:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

### [import math](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

### [def circle(t, r):](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [circumference = 2 * math.pi * r](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) n = 50 [length = circumference / n](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [polygon(t, n, length)](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[The first line computes the circumference of a circle with radius](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [r](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [using the formula 2](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) π r . [Since we use](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [math.pi](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [, we have to import](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [math](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [By convention,](http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=k4RRi_ntQc8) [import](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [statements are](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [usually at the beginning of the script.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[n](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [is the number of line segments in our approximation of a circle, so](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [length](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [is the length](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [of each segment. Thus,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) polygon draws a 50-sided polygon that approximates a circle with radius r .

[One limitation of this solution is that](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [is a constant, which means that for very big circles,](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) the line segments are too long, and for small circles, we waste time drawing very small [segments. One solution would be to generalize the function by taking](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) n [as a parameter.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [This would give the user (whoever calls](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) circle ) more control, but the interface would be less clean.

[The](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [interface](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [of a function is a summary of how it is used: what are the parameters? What](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [does the function do? And what is the return value? An interface is “clean” if it allows the](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [caller to do what they want without dealing with unnecessary details.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[In this example,](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) r [belongs in the interface because it specifies the circle to be drawn.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [is](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [less appropriate because it pertains to the details of](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [how](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) the circle should be rendered.

[Rather than clutter up the interface, it is better to choose an appropriate value of](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) [depend-](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) ing on circumference :

```
Chapter 4. Case study: interface design
```

```
def circle(t, r):
circumference = 2 * math.pi * r
n = int(circumference / 3) + 3
length = circumference / n
polygon(t, n, length)
```

Now the number of segments is an integer near [circumference/3](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [, so the length of each](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) segment is approximately 3, which is small enough that the circles look good, but big [enough to be efficient, and acceptable for any size circle.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

Adding 3 to [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [guarantees that the polygon has at least 3 sides.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

## 4.7 Refactoring

When I wrote [circle](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [, I was able to re-use](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [polygon](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) because a many-sided polygon is a good approximation of a circle. But arc [is not as cooperative; we can’t use](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [polygon](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [or](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [circle](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [to](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [draw an arc.](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

[One alternative is to start with a copy of](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [polygon](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) and transform it into [arc](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [The result](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [might look like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py)

```
def arc(t, r, angle):
arc_length = 2 * math.pi * r * angle / 360
n = int(arc_length / 3) + 1
step_length = arc_length / n
step_angle = angle / n
```

### for i in range(n): [t.fd(step_length)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [t.lt(step_angle)](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[The second half of this function looks like](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [polygon](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) , but we can’t re-use [polygon](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [without](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [changing the interface. We could generalize](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [polygon](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [to take an angle as a third argument,](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) but then [polygon](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) would no longer be an appropriate name! [Instead, let’s call the more](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [general function](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) polyline :

### [def polyline(t, n, length, angle):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) for i in range(n): t.fd(length) t.lt(angle)

[Now we can rewrite](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [polygon](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [and](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [arc](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [to use](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [polyline](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) :

### def polygon(t, n, length): [angle = 360.0 / n](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) polyline(t, n, length, angle)

```
def arc(t, r, angle):
arc_length = 2 * math.pi * r * angle / 360
n = int(arc_length / 3) + 1
step_length = arc_length / n
step_angle = float(angle) / n
polyline(t, n, step_length, step_angle)
```

Finally, we can rewrite circle to use arc :

### 4.8. A development plan

### [def circle(t, r):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) arc(t, r, 360)

[This process—rearranging a program to improve interfaces and facilitate code re-use—is](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [called](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [refactoring](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) . In this case, we noticed that there was similar code in arc and polygon , [so we “factored it out” into](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [polyline](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

If we had planned ahead, we might have written polyline [first and avoided refactoring,](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [but often you don’t know enough at the beginning of a project to design all the interfaces.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [Once you start coding, you understand the problem better. Sometimes refactoring is a sign](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [that you have learned something.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

## [4.8](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) A development plan

[A](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [development plan](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [is a process for writing programs. The process we used in this case](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) study is “encapsulation and generalization”. The steps of this process are:

1. [1. Start by writing a small program with no function definitions.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

2. [2. Once you get the program working, identify a coherent piece of it, encapsulate the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [piece in a function and give it a name.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

3. [3. Generalize the function by adding appropriate parameters.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

4. [4. Repeat steps 1–3 until you have a set of working functions. Copy and paste working](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [code to avoid retyping (and re-debugging).](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

5. [5. Look for opportunities to improve the program by refactoring. For example, if you](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [have similar code in several places, consider factoring it into an appropriately general](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [function.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[This process has some drawbacks—we will see alternatives later—but it can be useful if](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) you don’t know ahead of time how to divide the program into functions. This approach [lets you design as you go along.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

#### 4.9 [docstring](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

[A](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [docstring](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [is a string at the beginning of a function that explains the interface (“doc” is](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [short for “documentation”). Here is an example:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[def polyline(t, n, length, angle):](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) ["""Draws n line segments with the given length and](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [angle (in degrees) between them.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [t is a turtle.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) ["""](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [for i in range(n):](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) t.fd(length) [t.lt(angle)](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[By convention, all docstrings are triple-quoted strings, also known as multiline strings](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) because the triple quotes allow the string to span more than one line.

```
Chapter 4. Case study: interface design
```

[It is terse, but it contains the essential information someone would need to use this func-](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [tion. It explains concisely what the function does (without getting into the details of how](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [it does it). It explains what effect each parameter has on the behavior of the function and](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [what type each parameter should be (if it is not obvious).](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

[Writing this kind of documentation is an important part of interface design.](https://thinkpython.com/code) A well- [designed interface should be simple to explain; if you have a hard time explaining one](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [of your functions, maybe the interface could be improved.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

## 4.10 [Debugging](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[An interface is like a contract between a function and a caller. The caller agrees to provide](http://www.thinkpython.com/) certain parameters and the function agrees to do certain work.

For example, [polyline](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [requires four arguments:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [t](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [has to be a Turtle;](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [n](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [has to be an integer;](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [length](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [should be a positive number; and](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) angle has to be a number, which is understood to be in degrees.

These requirements are called preconditions because they are supposed to be true before [the function starts executing. Conversely, conditions at the end of the function are](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [post-](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [conditions](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [. Postconditions include the intended effect of the function (like drawing line](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [segments) and any side effects (like moving the Turtle or making other changes).](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

[Preconditions are the responsibility of the caller.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [If the caller violates a (properly docu-](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [mented!) precondition and the function doesn’t work correctly, the bug is in the caller, not](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) the function.

If the preconditions are satisfied and the postconditions are not, the bug is in the function. [If your preand postconditions are clear, they can help with debugging.](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py)

## 4.11 [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[method:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [A function that is associated with an object and called using dot notation.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

loop: A part of a program that can run repeatedly.

encapsulation: The process of transforming a sequence of statements into a function definition.

generalization: [The process of replacing something unnecessarily specific (like a number)](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [with something appropriately general (like a variable or parameter).](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[keyword argument:](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [An argument that includes the name of the parameter as a “key-](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [word”.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py)

interface: [A description of how to use a function, including the name and descriptions of](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [the arguments and return value.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

refactoring: [The process of modifying a working program to improve function interfaces](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) and other qualities of the code.

development plan: A process for writing programs.

[postcondition:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [A requirement that should be satisfied by the function before it ends.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)
