## Chapter 5

# Conditionals and recursion

[The main topic of this chapter is the](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [if](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) statement, which executes different code depending [on the state of the program. But first I want to introduce two new operators: floor division](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [and modulus.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py)

## [5.1](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point2_soln.py) Floor division and modulus

[The](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) floor division operator, // , divides two numbers and rounds down to an integer. For example, suppose the run time of a movie is 105 minutes. You might want to know how long that is in hours. Conventional division returns a floating-point number:

### [>>> minutes = 105](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [>>> minutes / 60](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [1.75](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[But we don’t normally write hours with decimal points. Floor division returns the integer](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) number of hours, rounding down:

### [>>> minutes = 105](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) >>> hours = minutes // 60 >>> hours

[To get the remainder, you could subtract off one hour in minutes:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

### [>>> remainder = minutes - hours * 60](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) >>> remainder

[An alternative is to use the](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [modulus operator](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [%](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [, which divides two numbers and returns](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [the remainder.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

### [>>> remainder = minutes % 60](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [>>> remainder](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

[The modulus operator is more useful than it seems. For example, you can check whether](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) one number is divisible by another—if x % y is zero, then x is divisible by y .

Chapter 5. Conditionals and recursion

[Also, you can extract the right-most digit or digits from a number. For example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) x % 10 yields the right-most digit of x [(in base 10). Similarly](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [x % 100](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [yields the last two digits.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[If you are using Python 2, division works differently. The division operator,](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) / [, performs](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [floor division if both operands are integers, and floating-point division if either operand is](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) a float .

## 5.2 [Boolean expressions](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

A [boolean expression](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [is an expression that is either true or false. The following examples](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [use the operator](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [==](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [, which compares two operands and produces](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [True](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [if they are equal](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) and False otherwise:

### [>>> 5 == 5](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) True [>>> 5 == 6](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) False

True and False are special values that belong to the type bool [; they are not strings:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py)

```
>>> type(True)
<class ' bool ' >
>>> type(False)
<class ' bool ' >
```

[The](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [==](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [operator is one of the](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [relational operators](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [; the others are:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

| x != y | # x is not equal to y             |
| ------ | --------------------------------- |
| x > y  | # x is greater than y             |
| x < y  | # x is less than y                |
| x >= y | # x is greater than or equal to y |
| x <= y | # x is less than or equal to y    |

[Although these operations are probably familiar to you, the Python symbols are different](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) from the mathematical symbols. A common error is to use a single equal sign ( [=](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [) instead of](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [a double equal sign (](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [==](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [). Remember that](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [=](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [is an assignment operator and](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [==](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [is a relational](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [operator. There is no such thing as](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) =< or => .

## 5.3 Logical operators

There are three [logical operators](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [or](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [not](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) . The semantics (meaning) of these operators is similar to their meaning in English. For example, [x > 0 and x < 10](http://gutenberg.org/) [is true](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) only if x is greater than 0 [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [less than 10.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[n%2 == 0 or n%3 == 0](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [is true if](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [either or both](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [of the conditions is true, that is, if the number](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) is divisible by 2 [or](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [3.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

Finally, the [not](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [operator negates a boolean expression, so](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [not (x > y)](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [is true if](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [x > y](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [is](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) false, that is, if [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [is less than or equal to](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [y](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[Strictly speaking, the operands of the logical operators should be boolean expressions, but](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) Python is not very strict. Any nonzero number is interpreted as True :

### 5.4. Conditional execution

### [>>> 42 and True](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) True

[This flexibility can be useful, but there are some subtleties to it that might be confusing.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) You might want to avoid it (unless you know what you are doing).

## [5.4](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [Conditional execution](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[In order to write useful programs, we almost always need the ability to check conditions](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [and change the behavior of the program accordingly.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [Conditional statements](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book1.py) [give us this](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [ability. The simplest form is the](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [if](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [statement:](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

### [if x > 0:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [print(](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) x is positive ' )

The boolean expression after if is called the condition . If it is true, the indented statement runs. If not, nothing happens.

if statements have the same structure as function definitions: a header followed by an [indented body. Statements like this are called](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [compound statements](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal)

[There is no limit on the number of statements that can appear in the body, but there has to](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [be at least one. Occasionally, it is useful to have a body with no statements (usually as a](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [place keeper for code you haven’t written yet). In that case, you can use the](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [pass](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [statement,](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [which does nothing.](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

### [if x < 0:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [pass](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) # TODO: need to handle negative values!

## [5.5](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Alternative execution](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[A second form of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) if statement is “alternative execution”, in which there are two possi- [bilities and the condition determines which one runs. The syntax looks like this:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

```
if x % 2 == 0:
print( ' x is even ' )
else:
print( ' x is odd ' )
```

[If the remainder when](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) is divided by 2 is 0, then we know that x is even, and the program displays an appropriate message. [If the condition is false, the second set of statements](http://gutenberg.org/) [runs. Since the condition must be true or false, exactly one of the alternatives will run. The](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [alternatives are called](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [branches](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) , because they are branches in the flow of execution.

## [5.6](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [Chained conditionals](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[Sometimes there are more than two possibilities and we need more than two branches.](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) One way to express a computation like that is a chained conditional :

Chapter 5. Conditionals and recursion

[if x < y:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) print( ' x is less than y ' ) [elif x > y:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [x is greater than y](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) else: print( ' x and y are equal ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

elif is an abbreviation of “else if”. Again, exactly one branch will run. There is no limit on the number of [elif](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [statements. If there is an](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [else](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) clause, it has to be at the end, but there [doesn’t have to be one.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

```
if choice == ' a ' :
draw_a()
elif choice == ' b ' :
draw_b()
elif choice == ' c ' :
draw_c()
```

Each condition is checked in order. If the first is false, the next is checked, and so on. If one [of them is true, the corresponding branch runs and the statement ends. Even if more than](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [one condition is true, only the first true branch runs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

## 5.7 [Nested conditionals](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

[One conditional can also be nested within another. We could have written the example in](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [the previous section like this:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

if x == y: print( ' x and y are equal ' ) else: [if x < y:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [x is less than y](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [else:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [x is greater than y](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) ' )

The outer conditional contains two branches. The first branch contains a simple statement. [The second branch contains another](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [if](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) statement, which has two branches of its own. Those two branches are both simple statements, although they could have been conditional statements as well.

[Although the indentation of the statements makes the structure apparent,](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [nested condi-](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) tionals [become difficult to read very quickly. It is a good idea to avoid them when you](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) can.

[Logical operators often provide a way to simplify nested conditional statements. For ex-](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [ample, we can rewrite the following code using a single conditional:](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

### if 0 < x: [if x < 10:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) ['](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [x is a positive single-digit number.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) ['](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [)](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

The print [statement runs only if we make it past both conditionals, so we can get the same](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) effect with the [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [operator:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

### [if 0 < x and x < 10:](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) print( ' x is a positive single-digit number. ' )

### 5.8. Recursion

[For this kind of condition, Python provides a more concise option:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### if 0 < x < 10: [print(](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ['](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [x is a positive single-digit number.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ' )

## 5.8 Recursion

[It is legal for one function to call another; it is also legal for a function to call itself. It may](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [not be obvious why that is a good thing, but it turns out to be one of the most magical](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [things a program can do. For example, look at the following function:](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

```
def countdown(n):
if n <= 0:
print( ' Blastoff! ' )
else:
print(n)
countdown(n-1)
```

If [n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [is 0 or negative, it outputs the word, “Blastoff!” Otherwise, it outputs](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) n [and then calls](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) a function named countdown —itself—passing n-1 as an argument.

[What happens if we call this function like this?](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

### [>>> countdown(3)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[The execution of](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [countdown](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [begins with](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [n=3](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [, and since](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [is greater than 0, it outputs the](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [value 3, and then calls itself...](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

The execution of countdown begins with n=2 , and since n is greater than 0, it [outputs the value 2, and then calls itself...](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[The execution of](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [countdown](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [begins with](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [n=1](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [, and since](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [is greater](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [than 0, it outputs the value 1, and then calls itself...](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[The execution of](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [countdown](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [begins with](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) n=0 , and since [n](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [is](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [not greater than 0, it outputs the word, “Blastoff!” and then](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [returns.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

The countdown that got n=1 returns.

[The](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) countdown that got n=2 returns.

The countdown that got n=3 returns.

[And then you’re back in](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [__main__](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [. So, the total output looks like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

### Blastoff!

[A function that calls itself is](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [recursive](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [; the process of executing it is called](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [recursion](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

[As another example, we can write a function that prints a string](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [times.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

### def print_n(s, n): [if n <= 0:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [return](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [print(s)](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) print_n(s, n-1)

### [countdown](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[Figure 5.1: Stack diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py)

[If](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [n <= 0](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [the](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [return statement](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) exits the function. The flow of execution immediately re- [turns to the caller, and the remaining lines of the function don’t run.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[The rest of the function is similar to](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) countdown : it displays s and then calls itself to display s n − [1 additional times. So the number of lines of output is](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [1 + (n - 1)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) , which adds up to n .

[For simple examples like this, it is probably easier to use a](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [for](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [loop.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [But we will see](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [examples later that are hard to write with a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [for](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [loop and easy to write with recursion, so it](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [is good to start early.](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

## 5.9 Stack diagrams for recursive functions

In Section 3.9, we used a stack diagram to represent the state of a program during a function [call. The same kind of diagram can help interpret a recursive function.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

Every time a function gets called, Python creates a frame to contain the function’s local [variables and parameters. For a recursive function, there might be more than one frame on](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [the stack at the same time.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

Figure 5.1 shows a stack diagram for countdown called with n = 3 [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

As usual, the top of the stack is the frame for __main__ . It is empty because we did not create any variables in __main__ or pass any arguments to it.

The four countdown [frames have different values for the parameter](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) . The bottom of the stack, where n=0 , is called the base case . It does not make a recursive call, so there are no more frames.

As an exercise, draw a stack diagram for print_n [called with](http://gutenberg.org/) [s =](http://gutenberg.org/) ['](http://gutenberg.org/) [Hello](http://gutenberg.org/) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [n=2](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [. Then](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [write a function called](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [do_n](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [that takes a function object and a number,](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [, as arguments, and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [that calls the given function](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [times.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

## 5.10 Infinite recursion

[If a recursion never reaches a base case, it goes on making recursive calls forever, and the](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [program never terminates. This is known as](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [infinite recursion](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) [, and it is generally not a](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) good idea. Here is a minimal program with an infinite recursion:

### 5.11. Keyboard input

[def recurse():](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) recurse() [In most programming environments, a program with infinite recursion does not really run](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) forever. Python reports an error message when the maximum recursion depth is reached: [File "<stdin>", line 2, in recurse](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [File "<stdin>", line 2, in recurse](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [File "<stdin>", line 2, in recurse](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [File "<stdin>", line 2, in recurse](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [RuntimeError: Maximum recursion depth exceeded](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [This traceback is a little bigger than the one we saw in the previous chapter. When the error](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [occurs, there are 1000](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) recurse frames on the stack!

[If you encounter an infinite recursion by accident, review your function to confirm that](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [there is a base case that does not make a recursive call. And if there is a base case, check](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [whether you are guaranteed to reach it.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

## [5.11](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [Keyboard input](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[The programs we have written so far accept no input from the user. They just do the same](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [thing every time.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

Python provides a built-in function called input that stops the program and waits for the [user to type something. When the user presses](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) Return or [Enter](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [, the program resumes and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [input](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) returns what the user typed as a string. [In Python 2, the same function is called](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [raw_input](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [>>> text = input()](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [What are you waiting for?](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [>>> text](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) What are you waiting for? ' [Before getting input from the user, it is a good idea to print a prompt telling the user what](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [to type.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [input](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) can take a prompt as an argument: >>> name = input( ' What...is your name?\n ' ) [What...is your name?](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) Arthur, King of the Britons! [>>> name](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Arthur, King of the Britons!](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) The sequence [\n](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [at the end of the prompt represents a](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [newline](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [, which is a special character](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [that causes a line break. That’s why the user’s input appears below the prompt.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

[If you expect the user to type an integer, you can try to convert the return value to](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [int](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [>>> prompt =](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [What...is the airspeed velocity of an unladen swallow?\n](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) ['](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) >>> speed = input(prompt) [What...is the airspeed velocity of an unladen swallow?](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

### [>>> int(speed)](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators)

Chapter 5. Conditionals and recursion

[But if the user types something other than a string of digits, you get an error:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) >>> speed = input(prompt) [What...is the airspeed velocity of an unladen swallow?](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [What do you mean, an African or a European swallow?](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) >>> int(speed) [ValueError: invalid literal for int() with base 10](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [We will see how to handle this kind of error later.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

## 5.12 [Debugging](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[When a syntax or runtime error occurs, the error message contains a lot of information, but](http://www.thinkpython.com/) it can be overwhelming. The most useful parts are usually:

- What kind of error it was, and

- [• Where it occurred.](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

[Syntax errors are usually easy to find, but there are a few gotchas. Whitespace errors can](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) be tricky because spaces and tabs are invisible and we are used to ignoring them. >>> x = 5 [>>>](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [y = 6](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [File "<stdin>", line 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [y = 6](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [^](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) IndentationError: unexpected indent In this example, the problem is that the second line is indented by one space. But the error message points to [y](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) , which is misleading. In general, error messages indicate where the [problem was discovered, but the actual error might be earlier in the code, sometimes on a](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [previous line.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_rankings)

[The same is true of runtime errors. Suppose you are trying to compute a signal-to-noise](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [ratio in decibels. The formula is](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) SNR db = 10 log [(](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [P](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [signal](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [/](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [P](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [noise](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [. In Python, you might](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [write something like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [import math](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) signal_power = 9 noise_power = 10 ratio = signal_power // noise_power [decibels = 10 * math.log10(ratio)](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) print(decibels) [When you run this program, you get an exception:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) Traceback (most recent call last): [File "snr.py", line 5, in ?](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [decibels = 10 * math.log10(ratio)](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [ValueError: math domain error](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [The error message indicates line 5, but there is nothing wrong with that line. To find the](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) real error, it might be useful to print the value of [ratio](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [, which turns out to be 0.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [The](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [problem is in line 4, which uses floor division instead of floating-point division.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[You should take the time to read error messages carefully, but don’t assume that everything](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) they say is correct.

### 5.13. Glossary

## [5.13](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

floor division: [An operator, denoted](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [//](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [, that divides two numbers and rounds down (to-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [ward negative infinity) to an integer.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[modulus operator:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [An operator, denoted with a percent sign (](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) % ), that works on integers [and returns the remainder when one number is divided by another.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

[boolean expression:](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [An expression whose value is either](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) True or False [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[relational operator:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [One of the operators that compares its operands:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [==](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [!=](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [>](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [<](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [>=](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [, and](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [<=](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[logical operator:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [One of the operators that combines boolean expressions:](http://www.thinkpython.com/) and , or , and not .

conditional statement: A statement that controls the flow of execution depending on some [condition.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[condition:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [The boolean expression in a conditional statement that determines which](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [branch runs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

[compound statement:](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [A statement that consists of a header and a body. The header ends](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [with a colon (:). The body is indented relative to the header.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[branch:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [One of the alternative sequences of statements in a conditional statement.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py)

[chained conditional:](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [A conditional statement with a series of alternative branches.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[nested conditional:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) A conditional statement that appears in one of the branches of another conditional statement.

[return statement:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [A statement that causes a function to end immediately and return to the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [caller.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[recursion:](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [The process of calling the function that is currently executing.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[base case:](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) A conditional branch in a recursive function that does not make a recursive call.

infinite recursion: A recursion that doesn’t have a base case, or never reaches it. Eventually, an infinite recursion causes a runtime error.

Exercise 5.3. [If you are given three sticks, you may or may not be able to arrange them in a triangle.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [For example, if one of the sticks is 12 inches long and the other two are one inch long, you will not](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) be able to get the short sticks to meet in the middle. For any three lengths, there is a simple test to [see if it is possible to form a triangle:](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[If any of the three lengths is greater than the sum of the other two, then you cannot](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) form a triangle. Otherwise, you can. (If the sum of two lengths equals the third, they [form what is called a “degenerate” triangle.)](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

## [Chapter 6](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

# Fruitful functions

[Many of the Python functions we have used, such as the math functions, produce return](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) values. But the functions we’ve written are all void: they have an effect, like printing a [value or moving a turtle, but they don’t have a return value. In this chapter you will learn](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [to write fruitful functions.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

## [6.1](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Return values](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

Calling the function generates a return value, which we usually assign to a variable or use [as part of an expression.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

### [e = math.exp(1.0)](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [height = radius * math.sin(radians)](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[The functions we have written so far are void.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [Speaking casually, they have no return](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) value; more precisely, their return value is None .

In this chapter, we are (finally) going to write fruitful functions. The first example is area , which returns the area of a circle with the given radius:

### def area(radius): [a = math.pi * radius2](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [return a](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

[We have seen the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [return](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [statement before, but in a fruitful function the](http://gutenberg.org/) return statement includes an expression. [This statement means: “Return immediately from this function](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [and use the following expression as a return value.”](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [The expression can be arbitrarily](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [complicated, so we could have written this function more concisely:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

### [def area(radius):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [return math.pi * radius2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

[On the other hand,](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [temporary variables](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [like](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) can make debugging easier.

[Sometimes it is useful to have multiple return statements, one in each branch of a condi-](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) tional:

Chapter 6. Fruitful functions

### [def absolute_value(x):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) if x < 0: [return -x](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [else:](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [return x](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

Since these return [statements are in an alternative conditional, only one runs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

| quent statements. Code that appears after a | return    | statement, or any other place the flow |
| ------------------------------------------- | --------- | -------------------------------------- |
| of execution can never reach, is called     | dead code | .                                      |

[In a fruitful function, it is a good idea to ensure that every possible path through the pro-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [gram hits a](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [return](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [statement. For example:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

### [def absolute_value(x):](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [if x < 0:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [return -x](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [if x > 0:](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [return x](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

| This function is incorrect because if | x      | happens to be 0, neither condition is true, and the |
| ------------------------------------- | ------ | --------------------------------------------------- |
| function ends without hitting a       | return | statement. If the flow of execution gets to the end |
| of a function, the return value is    | None   | , which is not the absolute value of 0.             |

### [>>> print(absolute_value(0))](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [None](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py)

[By the way, Python provides a built-in function called](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [abs](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [that computes absolute values.](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py)

[As an exercise, write a](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [compare](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) function that takes two values, x and y , and returns if x > y , if x == y , and -1 if x < y .

## 6.2 [Incremental development](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

As you write larger functions, you might find yourself spending more time debugging.

[To deal with increasingly complex programs, you might want to try a process called](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [in-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [cremental development](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) . The goal of incremental development is to avoid long debugging sessions by adding and testing only a small amount of code at a time.

[As an example, suppose you want to find the distance between two points, given by the](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) coordinates ( x , y ) and ( x , y ) . By the Pythagorean theorem, the distance is:

[√](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) distance = ( x − x [)](http://gutenberg.org/) [+ (](http://gutenberg.org/) [y](http://gutenberg.org/) [−](http://gutenberg.org/) [y](http://gutenberg.org/) [)](http://gutenberg.org/)

[The first step is to consider what a](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [distance](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) function should look like in Python. In other [words, what are the inputs (parameters) and what is the output (return value)?](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

[In this case, the inputs are two points, which you can represent using four numbers. The](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [return value is the distance represented by a floating-point value.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

[Immediately you can write an outline of the function:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

### [def distance(x1, y1, x2, y2):](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) return 0.0

### 6.2. Incremental development

[Obviously, this version doesn’t compute distances; it always returns zero. But it is syn-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [tactically correct, and it runs, which means that you can test it before you make it more](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [complicated.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[To test the new function, call it with sample arguments:](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) >>> distance(1, 2, 4, 6) 0.0 [I chose these values so that the horizontal distance is 3 and the vertical distance is 4; that](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [way, the result is 5, the hypotenuse of a 3-4-5 right triangle. When testing a function, it is](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [useful to know the right answer.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[At this point we have confirmed that the function is syntactically correct, and we can start](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [adding code to the body.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [A reasonable next step is to find the differences](http://www.thinkpython.com/) x − x and y − y [. The next version stores those values in temporary variables and prints them.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) def distance(x1, y1, x2, y2): [dx = x2 - x1](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) dy = y2 - y1 print( ' dx is ['](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [, dx)](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [print(](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [dy is](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [, dy)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) return 0.0 [If the function is working, it should display](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [dx is 3](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [dy is 4](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [. If so, we know that the](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [function is getting the right arguments and performing the first computation correctly. If](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [not, there are only a few lines to check.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

Next we compute the sum of squares of [dx](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [and](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [dy](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) def distance(x1, y1, x2, y2): [dx = x2 - x1](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) dy = y2 - y1 [dsquared = dx**2 + dy**2](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [dsquared is:](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) ['](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [, dsquared)](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [return 0.0](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [Again, you would run the program at this stage and check the output (which should be](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [25). Finally, you can use](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) math.sqrt [to compute and return the result:](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [def distance(x1, y1, x2, y2):](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [dx = x2 - x1](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) dy = y2 - y1 dsquared = dx**2 + dy**2 [result = math.sqrt(dsquared)](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [return result](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [If that works correctly, you are done.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) Otherwise, you might want to print the value of [result](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [before the return statement.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[The final version of the function doesn’t display anything when it runs; it only returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [a value. The](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [print](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [statements we wrote are useful for debugging, but once you get the](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [function working, you should remove them. Code like that is called](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) scaffolding because it [is helpful for building the program but is not part of the final product.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[When you start out, you should add only a line or two of code at a time. As you gain more](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [experience, you might find yourself writing and debugging bigger chunks.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) Either way, [incremental development can save you a lot of debugging time.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

The key aspects of the process are:

Chapter 6. Fruitful functions

1. [1. Start with a working program and make small incremental changes. At any point, if](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) there is an error, you should have a good idea where it is.

2. [2. Use variables to hold intermediate values so you can display and check them.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

3. [3. Once the program is working, you might want to remove some of the scaffolding or](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [consolidate multiple statements into compound expressions, but only if it does not](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [make the program difficult to read.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[As an exercise, use incremental development to write a function called](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [hypotenuse](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [that](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [returns the length of the hypotenuse of a right triangle given the lengths of the other two](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [legs as arguments. Record each stage of the development process as you go.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

## 6.3 [Composition](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[As you should expect by now, you can call one function from within another. As an exam-](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [ple, we’ll write a function that takes two points, the center of the circle and a point on the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) perimeter, and computes the area of the circle.

[Assume that the center point is stored in the variables](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) xc and yc [, and the perimeter point is](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [in](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [xp](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [yp](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [. The first step is to find the radius of the circle, which is the distance between](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [the two points. We just wrote a function,](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [distance](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [, that does that:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point2_soln.py)

### [radius = distance(xc, yc, xp, yp)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

The next step is to find the area of a circle with that radius; we just wrote that, too:

### result = area(radius)

[Encapsulating these steps in a function, we get:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

### [def circle_area(xc, yc, xp, yp):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [radius = distance(xc, yc, xp, yp)](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [result = area(radius)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [return result](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py)

[The temporary variables](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [radius](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [and](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [result](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [are useful for development and debugging,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) but once the program is working, we can make it more concise by composing the function calls:

### [def circle_area(xc, yc, xp, yp):](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) return area(distance(xc, yc, xp, yp))

## 6.4 Boolean functions

[Functions can return booleans, which is often convenient for hiding complicated tests in-](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [side functions. For example:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

### [def is_divisible(x, y):](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) if x % y == 0: [return True](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) else: return False

### 6.5. More recursion

[It](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [is](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [common](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [to](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [give](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [boolean](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [functions](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [names](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) that sound like yes/no questions; is_divisible returns either [True](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [or](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [False](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [to indicate whether](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [x](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [is divisible by](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [y](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorting_algorithm)

[Here is an example:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [>>> is_divisible(6, 4)](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) False >>> is_divisible(6, 3) [True](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [The result of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [==](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [operator is a boolean, so we can write the function more concisely by](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [returning it directly:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [def is_divisible(x, y):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [return x % y == 0](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) Boolean functions are often used in conditional statements: [if is_divisible(x, y):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [x is divisible by y](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [It might be tempting to write something like:](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [if is_divisible(x, y) == True:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) print( ' x is divisible by y ' ) [But the extra comparison is unnecessary.](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

[As an exercise, write a function](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [is_between(x, y, z)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [that returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [True](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [if](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [≤](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [y](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [≤](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [z](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [or](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [False](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [otherwise.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

## [6.5](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [More recursion](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[We have only covered a small subset of Python, but you might be interested to know that](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [this subset is a](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [complete](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [programming language, which means that anything that can be](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) computed can be expressed in this language. Any program ever written could be rewritten [using only the language features you have learned so far (actually, you would need a few](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) commands to control devices like the mouse, disks, etc., but that’s all).

Proving that claim is a nontrivial exercise first accomplished by Alan Turing, one of the [first computer scientists (some would argue that he was a mathematician, but a lot of early](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) computer scientists started as mathematicians). Accordingly, it is known as the Turing Thesis. For a more complete (and accurate) discussion of the Turing Thesis, I recommend [Michael Sipser’s book](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [Introduction to the Theory of Computation](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

To give you an idea of what you can do with the tools you have learned so far, we’ll eval- [uate a few recursively defined mathematical functions. A recursive definition is similar to](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [a circular definition, in the sense that the definition contains a reference to the thing being](http://gutenberg.org/) [defined. A truly circular definition is not very useful:](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[vorpal:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [An adjective used to describe something that is vorpal.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[If you saw that definition in the dictionary, you might be annoyed.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [On the other hand,](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [if you looked up the definition of the factorial function, denoted with the symbol !, you](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [might get something like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[0!](http://puzzlers.org/) [=](http://puzzlers.org/) n ! = n ( n − ) !

Chapter 6. Fruitful functions

[This definition says that the factorial of 0 is 1, and the factorial of any other value,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) n , is n multiplied by the factorial of n − 1.

[So 3! is 3 times 2!, which is 2 times 1!, which is 1 times 0!. Putting it all together, 3! equals 3](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [times 2 times 1 times 1, which is 6.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[If you can write a recursive definition of something, you can write a Python program to](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [evaluate it. The first step is to decide what the parameters should be. In this case it should](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [be clear that](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [factorial](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [takes an integer:](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

```
def factorial(n):
```

[If the argument happens to be 0, all we have to do is return 1:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

### [def factorial(n):](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [if n == 0:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [return 1](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5)

Otherwise, and this is the interesting part, we have to make a recursive call to find the [factorial of](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [n](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [−](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [1 and then multiply it by](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [n](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

### [def factorial(n):](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [if n == 0:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [return 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [else:](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [recurse = factorial(n-1)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [result = n * recurse](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [return result](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[The flow of execution for this program is similar to the flow of](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) countdown in Section 5.8. If we call factorial with the value 3:

[Since 3 is not 0, we take the second branch and calculate the factorial of](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [n-1](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [...](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Since 2 is not 0, we take the second branch and calculate the factorial of](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [n-1](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [...](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

Since 1 is not 0, we take the second branch and calculate the factorial of n-1 ...

Since 0 equals 0, we take the first branch and return 1 without making any more recursive calls.

[The return value, 1, is multiplied by](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [, which is 1, and the result is](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) returned.

[The return value, 1, is multiplied by](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [, which is 2, and the result is returned.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

The return value (2) is multiplied by [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [, which is 3, and the result, 6, becomes the return](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [value of the function call that started the whole process.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[Figure 6.1 shows what the stack diagram looks like for this sequence of function calls.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[The return values are shown being passed back up the stack.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [In each frame, the return](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [value is the value of](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [result](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [, which is the product of](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [recurse](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) .

[In the last frame, the local variables](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [recurse](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [and](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [result](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) [do not exist, because the branch](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) that creates them does not run.

### 6.6. Leap of faith

### [__main__](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### [factorial](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [n](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [recurse](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) result

### [factorial](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [recurse](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [result](https://thinkpython.com/code)

### factorial n recurse result

### [factorial](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[Figure 6.1: Stack diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py)

## [6.6](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) Leap of faith

Following the flow of execution is one way to read programs, but it can quickly become overwhelming. [An alternative is what I call the “leap of faith”.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) When you come to a [function call, instead of following the flow of execution, you](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [assume](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [that the function works](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) correctly and returns the right result.

[In fact, you are already practicing this leap of faith when you use built-in functions. When](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [you call](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [math.cos](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [or](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [math.exp](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [, you don’t examine the bodies of those functions. You just](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [assume that they work because the people who wrote the built-in functions were good](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [programmers.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

The same is true when you call one of your own functions. For example, in Section 6.4, we wrote a function called is_divisible [that determines whether one number is divisible by](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [another. Once we have convinced ourselves that this function is correct—by examining the](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [code and testing—we can use the function without looking at the body again.](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[The same is true of recursive programs.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) When you get to the recursive call, instead of [following the flow of execution, you should assume that the recursive call works (returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) the correct result) and then ask yourself, “Assuming that I can find the factorial of n − 1, [can I compute the factorial of](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [n](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) ?” It is clear that you can, by multiplying by [n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

Of course, it’s a bit strange to assume that the function works correctly when you haven’t finished writing it, but that’s why it’s called a leap of faith!

## [6.7](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [One more example](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[After](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) factorial [, the most common example of a recursively defined mathematical func-](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [tion is](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [fibonacci](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [, which has the following definition (see](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [http://en.wikipedia.org/](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [wiki/Fibonacci_number](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

| fibonacci | (   | ) = |
| --------- | --- | --- |
| fibonacci | (   | ) = |

Translated into Python, it looks like this:

Chapter 6. Fruitful functions

### [def fibonacci(n):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) if n == 0: [return 0](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [elif](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [n == 1:](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [return 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) else: return fibonacci(n-1) + fibonacci(n-2)

[If you try to follow the flow of execution here, even for fairly small values of](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) n [, your head](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) explodes. But according to the leap of faith, if you assume that the two recursive calls work [correctly, then it is clear that you get the right result by adding them together.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

## 6.8 [Checking types](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

[What happens if we call](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [factorial](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [and give it 1.5 as an argument?](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

### [>>> factorial(1.5)](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [RuntimeError: Maximum recursion depth exceeded](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

It looks like an infinite recursion. How can that be? The function has a base case—when [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) == 0 . But if [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [is not an integer, we can](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [miss](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [the base case and recurse forever.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal)

[In the first recursive call, the value of](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [is 0.5.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [In the next, it is -0.5.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [From there, it gets](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [smaller (more negative), but it will never be 0.](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

[We have two choices.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [We can try to generalize the](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [factorial](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [function to work with](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [floating-point numbers, or we can make](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [factorial](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) check the type of its argument. The first option is called the gamma function and it’s a little beyond the scope of this book. So we’ll go for the second.

We can use the built-in function [isinstance](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) [to verify the type of the argument.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [While](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [we’re at it, we can also make sure the argument is positive:](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[def factorial(n):](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [if not isinstance(n, int):](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) Factorial is only defined for integers. ' ) [return None](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [elif n < 0:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) print( ' Factorial is not defined for negative integers. ' ) return None elif n == 0: [return 1](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) else: [return n * factorial(n-1)](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[The first base case handles nonintegers; the second handles negative integers.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) In both [cases, the program prints an error message and returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [None](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [to indicate that something](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) went wrong:

[>>> print(factorial(](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [fred](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [))](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) Factorial is only defined for integers. None [>>> print(factorial(-2))](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [Factorial is not defined for negative integers.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) None

### 6.9. Debugging

[If we get past both checks, we know that](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) is a non-negative integer, so we can prove that the recursion terminates.

This program demonstrates a pattern sometimes called a guardian [. The first two condi-](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [tionals act as guardians, protecting the code that follows from values that might cause an](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [error. The guardians make it possible to prove the correctness of the code.](https://thinkpython.com/code)

[In Section 11.4 we will see a more flexible alternative to printing an error message: raising](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [an exception.](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

## [6.9](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [Debugging](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

Breaking a large program into smaller functions creates natural checkpoints for debugging. [If a function is not working, there are three possibilities to consider:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

- [• There is something wrong with the arguments the function is getting; a precondition](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) is violated.

- [• There is something wrong with the function; a postcondition is violated.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

- [• There is something wrong with the return value or the way it is being used.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[To rule out the first possibility, you can add a](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [print](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) statement at the beginning of the function and display the values of the parameters (and maybe their types). Or you can write code that checks the preconditions explicitly.

[If the parameters look good, add a](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [print](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [statement before each](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [return](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [statement and](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) display the return value. If possible, check the result by hand. Consider calling the function [with values that make it easy to check the result (as in Section 6.2).](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[If the function seems to be working, look at the function call to make sure the return value](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [is being used correctly (or used at all!).](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

Adding print statements at the beginning and end of a function can help make the flow of execution more visible. For example, here is a version of factorial with print statements:

```
def factorial(n):
space = ' ' * (4 * n)
print(space, ' factorial ' , n)
if n == 0:
print(space, ' returning 1 ' )
return 1
else:
recurse = factorial(n-1)
result = n * recurse
print(space, ' returning ' , result)
return result
```

[space](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [is a string of space characters that controls the indentation of the output. Here is the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) result of factorial(4) :

Chapter 6. Fruitful functions

```
factorial 4
factorial 3
factorial 2
factorial 1
factorial 0
returning 1
returning 1
returning 2
returning 6
returning 24
```

[If you are confused about the flow of execution, this kind of output can be helpful. It takes](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [some time to develop effective scaffolding, but a little bit of scaffolding can save a lot of](http://www.thinkpython.com/) debugging.

## [6.10](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [Glossary](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

[temporary variable:](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [A variable used to store an intermediate value in a complex calcula-](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) tion.

[dead code:](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [Part of a program that can never run, often because it appears after a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [return](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [statement.](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

[incremental development:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [A program development plan intended to avoid debugging by](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [adding and testing only a small amount of code at a time.](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

scaffolding: Code that is used during program development but is not part of the final [version.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

guardian: [A programming pattern that uses a conditional statement to check for and han-](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [dle circumstances that might cause an error.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[Exercise 6.3.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [A palindrome is a word that is spelled the same backward and forward, like “noon”](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) and “redivider”. Recursively, a word is a palindrome if the first and last letters are the same and the [middle is a palindrome.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

The following are functions that take a string argument and return the first, last, and middle letters:

### [def first(word):](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [return word[0]](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

### [def last(word):](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [return word[-1]](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

### [def middle(word):](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [return word[1:-1]](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

[We’ll see how they work in Chapter 8.](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

One letter? What about the empty string,](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) which is written '' and contains no letters?

Remember that you can use the built-in function len to check the length of a string.

Write a [function called](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [is_power](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [that takes parameters](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) a and b and returns True if a is a power of b . Note: you will have to think about the base case. [Exercise 6.5.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [The greatest common divisor (GCD) of a and b is the largest number that divides](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [both of them with no remainder.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[One way to find the GCD of two numbers is based on the observation that if r is the remainder when](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [a is divided by b, then gcd](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [(](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [a](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [,](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [b](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [) =](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [gcd](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [(](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [b](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [,](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [r](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [. As a base case, we can use gcd](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [(](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [a](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [, 0](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [) =](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [a.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

[Write a function called](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [gcd](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [that takes parameters](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [b](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) and returns their greatest common divisor.
