## Chapter 7

# Iteration

[This chapter is about iteration, which is the ability to run a block of statements repeatedly.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [We saw a kind of iteration, using recursion, in Section 5.8. We saw another kind, using a](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [loop, in Section 4.2. In this chapter we’ll see yet another kind, using a](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [while](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) statement. [But first I want to say a little more about variable assignment.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

## [7.1](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Reassignment](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

As you may have discovered, it is legal to make more than one assignment to the same [variable.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) A new assignment makes an existing variable refer to a new value (and stop [referring to the old value).](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

### [>>> x = 5](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [>>> x](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [>>> x = 7](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [>>> x](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

The first time we display x , its value is 5; the second time, its value is 7.

[Figure 7.1 shows what](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [reassignment](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [looks like in a state diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

[At this point I want to address a common source of confusion. Because Python uses the](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [equal sign (](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [=](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [) for assignment, it is tempting to interpret a statement like](http://gutenberg.org/) [a = b](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [as a mathe-](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [matical proposition of equality; that is, the claim that](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) b are equal. But this interpre- [tation is wrong.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py)

[First, equality is a symmetric relationship and assignment is not. For example, in math-](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [ematics, if](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [a](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [=](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [7 then 7](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [=](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [a](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [But in Python, the statement](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [a = 7](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [is legal and](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [7 = a](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [is](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [not.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

[Also, in mathematics, a proposition of equality is either true or false for all time. If](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [a](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [=](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [b](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [now, then](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [a](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [will always equal](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [b](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [In Python, an assignment statement can make two](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) variables equal, but they don’t have to stay that way:

Chapter 7. Iteration

[x](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Figure 7.1: State diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

### [>>> a = 5](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [>>> b = a](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [# a and b are now equal](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) >>> a = 3 [# a and b are no longer equal](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) >>> b

[The third line changes the value of](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [but does not change the value of](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [b](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [, so they are no](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) longer equal.

[Reassigning variables is often useful, but you should use it with caution. If the values of](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) variables change frequently, it can make the code difficult to read and debug.

## 7.2 [Updating variables](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

[A common kind of reassignment is an](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [update](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [, where the new value of the variable depends](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [on the old.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py)

### [>>> x = x + 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py)

This means “get the current value of x , add one, and then update x with the new value.”

If you try to update a variable that doesn’t exist, you get an error, because Python evaluates [the right side before it assigns a value to](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

### [>>> x = x + 1](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_rankings) [NameError: name](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [is not defined](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[Before you can update a variable, you have to](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [initialize](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [it, usually with a simple assign-](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [ment:](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py)

### [>>> x = 0](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) >>> x = x + 1

Updating a variable by adding 1 is called an increment ; subtracting 1 is called a decrement .

## 7.3 The while statement

[Computers are often used to automate repetitive tasks. Repeating identical or similar tasks](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [without making errors is something that computers do well and people do poorly. In a](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [computer program, repetition is also called](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [iteration](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[We have already seen two functions,](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [countdown](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [print_n](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [, that iterate using recursion.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [Because iteration is so common, Python provides language features to make it easier. One](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) is the for [statement we saw in Section 4.2. We’ll get back to that later.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

Another is the while statement. Here is a version of countdown that uses a while statement:

### 7.3. The while statement

```
def countdown(n):
while n > 0:
print(n)
n = n - 1
print( ' Blastoff! ' )
```

You can almost read the [while](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [statement as if it were English. It means, “While](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) n is greater than 0, display the value of n and then decrement n . When you get to 0, display the word [Blastoff!](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [”](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[More formally, here is the flow of execution for a](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [while](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [statement:](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting)

1. [1. Determine whether the condition is true or false.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

2. [2. If false, exit the](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [while](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [statement and continue execution at the next statement.](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

3. If the condition is true, run the body and then go back to step 1.

This type of flow is called a loop because the third step loops back around to the top.

[The body of the loop should change the value of one or more variables so that the condition](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) becomes false eventually and the loop terminates. Otherwise the loop will repeat forever, [which is called an](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [infinite loop](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [. An endless source of amusement for computer scientists](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [is the observation that the directions on shampoo, “Lather, rinse, repeat”, are an infinite](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [loop.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py)

[In the case of](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) countdown , we can prove that the loop terminates: if [n](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [is zero or negative, the](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [loop never runs. Otherwise,](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [n](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [gets smaller each time through the loop, so eventually we](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) have to get to 0.

For some other loops, it is not so easy to tell. For example:

```
def sequence(n):
while n != 1:
print(n)
if n % 2 == 0: # n is even
n = n / 2
else: # n is odd
n = n*3 + 1
```

The condition for this loop is n != 1 , so the loop will continue until n is , which makes the condition false.

[Each time through the loop, the program outputs the value of](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [and then checks whether](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [it is even or odd. If it is even,](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [is divided by 2. If it is odd, the value of](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) n is replaced with [n*3 + 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [. For example, if the argument passed to](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) sequence is 3, the resulting values of n are 3, 10, 5, 16, 8, 4, 2, 1.

[Since](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [n](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [sometimes increases and sometimes decreases, there is no obvious proof that](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [will](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [ever reach 1, or that the program terminates. For some particular values of](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [, we can prove](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [termination.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [For example, if the starting value is a power of two,](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) will be even every [time through the loop until it reaches 1. The previous example ends with such a sequence,](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [starting with 16.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

[The hard question is whether we can prove that this program terminates for](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [all](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [posi-](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [tive values of](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [n](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [So far, no one has been able to prove it](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) [or](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) [disprove it!](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_db.py) [(See](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) [http:](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) //en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture .)

Chapter 7. Iteration

[As an exercise, rewrite the function](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [print_n](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) from Section 5.8 using iteration instead of recursion.

#### 7.4 break

Sometimes you don’t know it’s time to end a loop until you get half way through the body. [In that case you can use the](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [break](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [statement to jump out of the loop.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set)

[For example, suppose you want to take input from the user until they type](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [done](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [. You could](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) write: [while True:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [line = input(](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [>](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [if line ==](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [done](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) : [break](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [print(line)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

print( ' Done! ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) The loop condition is True , which is always true, so the loop runs until it hits the break statement.

[Each time through, it prompts the user with an angle bracket. If the user types](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [done](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [, the](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) break [statement exits the loop. Otherwise the program echoes whatever the user types and](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [goes back to the top of the loop. Here’s a sample run:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [> not done](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [not done](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) > done Done! [This way of writing](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [while](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) [loops is common because you can check the condition anywhere](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) in the loop (not just at the top) and you can express the stop condition affirmatively (“stop [when this happens”) rather than negatively (“keep going until that happens”).](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

## [7.5](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [Square roots](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

Loops are often used in programs that compute numerical results by starting with an approximate answer and iteratively improving it.

For example, one way of computing square roots is Newton’s method. Suppose that you [want to know the square root of](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [. If you start with almost any estimate,](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) x , you can com- [pute a better estimate with the following formula:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[x](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [/](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [y](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [=](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

For example, if [a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [is 4 and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [x](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [is 3:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [>>> a = 4](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) >>> x = 3 [>>> y = (x + a/x) / 2](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) >>> y 2.16666666667

### 7.6. Algorithms

[√](https://thinkpython.com/code/letters.py) [The result is closer to the correct answer (](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [=](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) 2). If we repeat the process with the new estimate, it gets even closer:

### [>>> x = y](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [>>> y = (x + a/x) / 2](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [>>> y](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) 2.00641025641

[After a few more updates, the estimate is almost exact:](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

### [>>> x = y](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [>>> y = (x + a/x) / 2](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [>>> y](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [2.00001024003](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [>>> x = y](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [>>> y = (x + a/x) / 2](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [>>> y](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [2.00000000003](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[In general we don’t know ahead of time how many steps it takes to get to the right answer,](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [but we know when we get there because the estimate stops changing:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

### >>> x = y [>>> y = (x + a/x) / 2](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [>>> y](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [2.0](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [>>> x = y](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [>>> y = (x + a/x) / 2](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [>>> y](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [2.0](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[When](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [y == x](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) , we can stop. Here is a loop that starts with an initial estimate, x , and im- [proves it until it stops changing:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

### [while True:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [print(x)](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [y = (x + a/x) / 2](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) if y == x: [break](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [x = y](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

For most values of a this works fine, but in general it is dangerous to test float equality. Floating-point values are only approximately right: most rational numbers, like 1/3, and [√](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [irrational numbers, like](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [2, can’t be represented exactly with a](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [float](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

[Rather than checking whether](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [y](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) are exactly equal, it is safer to use the built-in func- [tion](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [abs](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [to compute the absolute value, or magnitude, of the difference between them:](http://gutenberg.org/)

### [if abs(y-x) < epsilon:](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [break](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[Where](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [epsilon](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [has a value like](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [0.0000001](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [that determines how close is close enough.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

## [7.6](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [Algorithms](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[Newton’s method is an example of an](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [algorithm](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) [: it is a mechanical process for solving a](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_db.py) category of problems (in this case, computing square roots).

Chapter 7. Iteration

[To understand what an algorithm is, it might help to start with something that is not an](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [algorithm. When you learned to multiply single-digit numbers, you probably memorized](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [the multiplication table.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [In effect, you memorized 100 specific solutions.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [That kind of](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [knowledge is not algorithmic.](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

But if you were “lazy”, you might have learned a few tricks. For example, to find the product of n and 9, you can write [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [−](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [1 as the first digit and 10](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [−](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [as the second digit.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [This trick is a general solution for multiplying any single-digit number by 9.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [That’s an](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [algorithm!](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[Similarly, the techniques you learned for addition with carrying, subtraction with borrow-](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [ing, and long division are all algorithms. One of the characteristics of algorithms is that](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [they do not require any intelligence to carry out.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [They are mechanical processes where](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [each step follows from the last according to a simple set of rules.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

Executing algorithms is boring, but designing them is interesting, intellectually challeng- [ing, and a central part of computer science.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[Some of the things that people do naturally, without difficulty or conscious thought, are](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [the hardest to express algorithmically. Understanding natural language is a good example.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) We all do it, but so far no one has been able to explain how [we do it, at least not in the form](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) of an algorithm.

## 7.7 [Debugging](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

As you start writing bigger programs, you might find yourself spending more time debugging. More code means more chances to make an error and more places for bugs to hide.

[One way to cut your debugging time is “debugging by bisection”. For example, if there](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [are 100 lines in your program and you check them one at a time, it would take 100 steps.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[Instead, try to break the problem in half. Look at the middle of the program, or near it, for](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [an intermediate value you can check. Add a](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) print [statement (or something else that has a](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [verifiable effect) and run the program.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

If the mid-point check is incorrect, there must be a problem in the first half of the program. If it is correct, the problem is in the second half.

[Every time you perform a check like this, you halve the number of lines you have to search.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [After six steps (which is fewer than 100), you would be down to one or two lines of code,](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) at least in theory.

In practice it is not always clear what the “middle of the program” is and not always pos- [sible to check it. It doesn’t make sense to count lines and find the exact midpoint. Instead,](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [think about places in the program where there might be errors and places where it is easy](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [to put a check. Then choose a spot where you think the chances are about the same that](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) [the bug is before or after the check.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

## 7.8 Glossary

reassignment: Assigning a new value to a variable that already exists.

### 7.9. Exercises

[update:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [An assignment where the new value of the variable depends on the old.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law)

initialization: [An assignment that gives an initial value to a variable that will be updated.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[increment:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [An update that increases the value of a variable (often by one).](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

decrement: An update that decreases the value of a variable.

[iteration:](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [Repeated execution of a set of statements using either a recursive function call](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [or a loop.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[infinite loop:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [A loop in which the terminating condition is never satisfied.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[algorithm:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [A general process for solving a category of problems.](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

## [Chapter 8](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set)

# [Strings](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[Strings are not like integers, floats, and booleans. A string is a](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) sequence [, which means it is](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [an ordered collection of other values. In this chapter you’ll see how to access the characters](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) that make up a string, and you’ll learn about some of the methods strings provide.

## [8.1](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [A string is a sequence](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[A string is a sequence of characters. You can access the characters one at a time with the](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) bracket operator:

### >>> fruit = ' banana ' [>>> letter = fruit[1]](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[The second statement selects character number 1 from](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [fruit](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) and assigns it to letter .

[The expression in brackets is called an](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [index](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [. The index indicates which character in the](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [sequence you want (hence the name).](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

But you might not get what you expect:

### [>>> letter](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) ' a '

For most people, the first letter of ' banana ' is b , not a . But for computer scientists, the [index is an offset from the beginning of the string, and the offset of the first letter is zero.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

### [>>> letter = fruit[0]](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [>>> letter](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ' b '

[So](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [b](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [is the 0th letter (“zero-eth”) of](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) ['](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [banana](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) ['](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [a](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [is the 1th letter (“one-eth”), and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [is the 2th](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [letter (“two-eth”).](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[As an index you can use an expression that contains variables and operators:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

```
>>> i = 1
>>> fruit[i]
' a '
>>> fruit[i+1]
' n '
```

Chapter 8. Strings

[But the value of the index has to be an integer. Otherwise you get:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### >>> letter = fruit[1.5] [TypeError: string indices must be integers](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

#### 8.2 len

[len](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) is a built-in function that returns the number of characters in a string:

### >>> fruit = ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [banana](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) >>> len(fruit)

To get the last letter of a string, you might be tempted to try something like this:

### [>>> length = len(fruit)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [>>> last = fruit[length]](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [IndexError: string index out of range](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

The reason for the [IndexError](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [is that there is no letter in](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [’banana’](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) with the index 6. Since we started counting at zero, the six letters are numbered 0 to 5. To get the last character, [you have to subtract 1 from](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [length](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

### [>>> last = fruit[length-1]](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) >>> last ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py)

[Or you can use negative indices, which count backward from the end of the string. The](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [expression](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) fruit[-1] yields the last letter, fruit[-2] yields the second to last, and so on.

## 8.3 [Traversal with a](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [for](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [loop](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[A lot of computations involve processing a string one character at a time. Often they start](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) at the beginning, select each character in turn, do something to it, and continue until the end. This pattern of processing is called a traversal . One way to write a traversal is with a [while](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [loop:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

### index = 0 while index < len(fruit): letter = fruit[index] print(letter) [index = index + 1](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

[This loop traverses the string and displays each letter on a line by itself. The loop condition](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) is index < len(fruit) [, so when](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [index](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [is equal to the length of the string, the condition is](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [false, and the body of the loop doesn’t run. The last character accessed is the one with the](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) index [len(fruit)-1](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [, which is the last character in the string.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

[As an exercise, write a function that takes a string as an argument and displays the letters](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [backward, one per line.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

[Another way to write a traversal is with a](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [loop:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

### [for letter in fruit:](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) print(letter)

### 8.4. String slices

[fruit](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### [’](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [b a n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [a ’](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[index](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Figure 8.1: Slice indices.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[Each time through the loop, the next character in the string is assigned to the variable](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [letter](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) . The loop continues until no characters are left.

[The following example shows how to use concatenation (string addition) and a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) for loop to generate an abecedarian series (that is, in alphabetical order). In Robert McCloskey’s [book](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [Make Way for Ducklings](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [, the names of the ducklings are Jack, Kack, Lack, Mack, Nack,](http://www.thinkpython.com/) Ouack, Pack, and Quack. This loop outputs these names in order:

### [prefixes =](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [JKLMNOPQ](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) suffix = ' ack '

### [for letter in prefixes:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) print(letter + suffix)

[The output is:](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

### [Jack](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [Kack](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [Lack](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [Mack](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Nack](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) [Oack](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Pack](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [Qack](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Of course, that’s not quite right because “Ouack” and “Quack” are misspelled.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) As an [exercise, modify the program to fix this error.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

## [8.4](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) String slices

A segment of a string is called a slice . Selecting a slice is similar to selecting a character:

```
>>> s = ' Monty Python '
>>> s[0:5]
' Monty '
>>> s[6:12]
' Python '
```

[The operator](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [[n:m]](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [returns the part of the string from the “n-eth” character to the “m-eth”](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [character, including the first but excluding the last. This behavior is counterintuitive, but](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [it might help to imagine the indices pointing](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [between](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [the characters, as in Figure 8.1.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

[If you omit the first index (before the colon), the slice starts at the beginning of the string.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [If you omit the second index, the slice goes to the end of the string:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

### [>>> fruit =](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) ['](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [banana](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) ['](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) >>> fruit[:3]

Chapter 8. Strings

### ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [ban](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) >>> fruit[3:] ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [ana](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) ['](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[If the first index is greater than or equal to the second the result is an](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) empty string , repre- [sented by two quotation marks:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

### >>> fruit = ' banana ' [>>> fruit[3:3]](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [''](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[An empty string contains no characters and has length 0, but other than that, it is the same](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) as any other string.

[Continuing this example, what do you think](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [fruit[:]](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [means? Try it and see.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

## [8.5](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [Strings are immutable](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

It is tempting to use the [] operator on the left side of an assignment, with the intention of [changing a character in a string. For example:](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

```
>>> greeting = ' Hello, world! '
>>> greeting[0] = ' J '
TypeError: ' str ' object does not support item assignment
```

[The “object” in this case is the string and the “item” is the character you tried to assign.](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) For now, an object is the same thing as a value, but we will refine that definition later (Section 10.10).

[The reason for the error is that strings are](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [immutable](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [, which means you can’t change an](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) existing string. The best you can do is create a new string that is a variation on the original:

```
>>> greeting = ' Hello, world! '
>>> new_greeting = ' J ' + greeting[1:]
>>> new_greeting
' Jello, world! '
```

This example concatenates a new first letter onto a slice of greeting . It has no effect on the original string.

## 8.6 [Searching](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[What does the following function do?](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

```
def find(word, letter):
index = 0
while index < len(word):
if word[index] == letter:
return index
index = index + 1
return -1
```

### 8.7. Looping and counting

[In a sense,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [find](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [is the inverse of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [[]](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) operator. Instead of taking an index and extracting [the corresponding character, it takes a character and finds the index where that character](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [appears. If the character is not found, the function returns](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) -1 .

[This is the first example we have seen of a](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) return statement inside a loop. If word[index] == letter [, the function breaks out of the loop and returns immediately.](https://thinkpython.com/code)

[If the character doesn’t appear in the string, the program exits the loop normally and re-](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [turns](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [-1](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[This pattern of computation—traversing a sequence and returning when we find what we](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [are looking for—is called a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [search](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[As an exercise, modify](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [find](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [so that it has a third parameter, the index in](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [word](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [where it](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [should start looking.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

## [8.7](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [Looping and counting](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[The following program counts the number of times the letter](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [appears in a string:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal)

```
word = ' banana '
count = 0
for letter in word:
if letter == ' a ' :
count = count + 1
print(count)
```

This program demonstrates another pattern of computation called a [counter](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [. The variable](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [count](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [is initialized to 0 and then incremented each time an](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [is found. When the loop exits,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [count](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) contains the result—the total number of a ’s.

[As an exercise, encapsulate this code in a function named](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [count](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [, and generalize it so that](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [it accepts the string and the letter as arguments.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[Then rewrite the function so that instead of traversing the string, it uses the three-](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [parameter version of](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) find from the previous section.

## [8.8](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [String methods](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

[Strings provide methods that perform a variety of useful operations. A method is similar](http://gutenberg.org/) [to a function—it takes arguments and returns a value—but the syntax is different.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [For](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [example, the method](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [upper](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [takes a string and returns a new string with all uppercase](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [letters.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

[Instead of the function syntax](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [upper(word)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [, it uses the method syntax](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [word.upper()](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

```
>>> word = ' banana '
>>> new_word = word.upper()
>>> new_word
' BANANA '
```

Chapter 8. Strings

[This form of dot notation specifies the name of the method,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) upper , and the name of the string to apply the method to, [word](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [The empty parentheses indicate that this method](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [takes no arguments.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[A method call is called an](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [invocation](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ; in this case, we would say that we are invoking upper on word .

As it turns out, there is a string method named find that is remarkably similar to the function we wrote:

```
>>> word = ' banana '
>>> index = word.find( ' a ' )
>>> index
```

[In this example, we invoke](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) find on word and pass the letter we are looking for as a parameter.

Actually, the find method is more general than our function; it can find substrings, not just characters:

### >>> word.find( ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [na](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

[By default,](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [find](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [starts at the beginning of the string, but it can take a second argument, the](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [index where it should start:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py)

### [>>> word.find(](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [na](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [, 3)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[This is an example of an](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) optional argument ; find can also take a third argument, the index [where it should stop:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

```
>>> name = ' bob '
>>> name.find( ' b ' , 1, 2)
-1
```

[This search fails because](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [b](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [does not appear in the index range from](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [to](http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=k4RRi_ntQc8) [, not including](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [Searching up to, but not including, the second index makes](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [find](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [consistent with the slice](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [operator.](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py)

## 8.9 The in operator

The word in is a boolean operator that takes two strings and returns [True](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [if the first ap-](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [pears as a substring in the second:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

>>> ' a ' [in](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [banana](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) True >>> ' seed ['](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [in](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) ['](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [banana](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) False

[For example, the following function prints all the letters from](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [word1](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [that also appear in](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [word2](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

### [def in_both(word1, word2):](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [for letter in word1:](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [if letter in word2:](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) print(letter)

### 8.10. String comparison

[With well-chosen variable names, Python sometimes reads like English. You could read](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [this loop, “for (each) letter in (the first) word, if (the) letter (appears) in (the second) word,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [print (the) letter.”](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Here’s what you get if you compare apples and oranges:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

```
>>> in_both( ' apples ' , ' oranges ' )
a
e
s
```

## [8.10](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [String comparison](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

The relational operators work on strings. To see if two strings are equal:

```
if word == ' banana ' :
print( ' All right, bananas. ' )
```

[Other relational operations are useful for putting words in alphabetical order:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

if word < ' banana ' : [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [Your word,](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [+ word +](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [, comes before banana.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [elif word >](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [banana](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [Your word,](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [+ word +](https://thinkpython.com/code/GoodKangaroo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/GoodKangaroo.py) [, comes after banana.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point2_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [else:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point2_soln.py) print( ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [All right, bananas.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) ['](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [)](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

Python does not handle uppercase and lowercase letters the same way people do. All the [uppercase letters come before all the lowercase letters, so:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[Your word, Pineapple, comes before banana.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[A common way to address this problem is to convert strings to a standard format, such as](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [all lowercase, before performing the comparison. Keep that in mind in case you have to](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [defend yourself against a man armed with a Pineapple.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

## [8.11](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) Debugging

When you use indices to traverse the values in a sequence, it is tricky to get the beginning [and end of the traversal right. Here is a function that is supposed to compare two words](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [and return](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [True](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) if one of the words is the reverse of the other, but it contains two errors:

### [def is_reverse(word1, word2):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) if len(word1) != len(word2): [return False](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

### [i = 0](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [j = len(word2)](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

### [while j > 0:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [if word1[i] != word2[j]:](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [return False](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) i = i+1

Chapter 8. Strings

### [j = j-1](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[return True](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [The first](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [if](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) statement checks whether the words are the same length. If not, we can return False [immediately. Otherwise, for the rest of the function, we can assume that the words](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [are the same length. This is an example of the guardian pattern in Section 6.8.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

| find two letters that don’t match, we can return | False | immediately. If we get through the |
| ------------------------------------------------ | ----- | ---------------------------------- |
| whole loop and all the letters match, we return  | True  | .                                  |

[If we test this function with the words “pots” and “stop”, we expect the return value](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [True](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [but we get an IndexError:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) >>> is_reverse( ' pots ' , ' stop ' ) [...](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [File "reverse.py", line 15, in is_reverse](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) if word1[i] != word2[j]: IndexError: string index out of range [For debugging this kind of error, my first move is to print the values of the indices imme-](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) diately before the line where the error appears. [while j > 0:](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [print(i, j)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [# print here](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[if word1[i] != word2[j]:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [return False](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [i = i+1](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) [j = j-1](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Now when I run the program again, I get more information:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [>>> is_reverse(](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_rankings) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [pots](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) ['](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [stop](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) ['](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [)](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) 0 4 [...](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [IndexError: string index out of range](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) The first time through the loop, the value of j is 4, which is out of range for the [string](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [pots](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [The index of the last character is 3, so the initial value for](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [j](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [should be](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) len(word2)-1 .

If I fix that error and run the program again, I get: >>> is_reverse( ' [pots](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [stop](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) 0 3 1 2 2 1 True [This time we get the right answer, but it looks like the loop only ran three times, which is](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [suspicious. To get a better idea of what is happening, it is useful to draw a state diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) [During the first iteration, the frame for](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [is_reverse](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [is shown in Figure 8.2.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

[I took some license by arranging the variables in the frame and adding dotted lines to show](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) that the values of [i](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [j](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [indicate characters in](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [word1](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [word2](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

Find and fix the second error in this function.

## [8.12](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[object:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [Something a variable can refer to.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [For now, you can use “object” and “value”](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [interchangeably.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[sequence:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [An ordered collection of values where each value is identified by an integer](http://www.thinkpython.com/) index.

item: One of the values in a sequence.

[index:](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) An integer value used to select an item in a sequence, such as a character in a string. [In Python indices start from 0.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

slice: A part of a string specified by a range of indices.

[empty string:](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) A string with no characters and length 0, represented by two quotation [marks.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[immutable:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point2_soln.py) The property of a sequence whose items cannot be changed.

[traverse:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [To iterate through the items in a sequence, performing a similar operation on](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [each.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[search:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) A pattern of traversal that stops when it finds what it is looking for.

[counter:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [A variable used to count something, usually initialized to zero and then incre-](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [mented.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[invocation:](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) A statement that calls a method.

optional argument: A function or method argument that is not required.

You might want to experiment with some of them [to make sure you understand how they work.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [strip](http://gutenberg.org/) [and](http://gutenberg.org/) [replace](http://gutenberg.org/) [are particularly useful.](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py)

[The](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [documentation](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [uses](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [syntax](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [that](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [might](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [be](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [confusing.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [For](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [example,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [in](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [find(sub[, start[, end]])](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [, the brackets indicate optional arguments. So](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [sub](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [is required, but](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [start](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) [is optional, and if you include](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [start](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) [, then](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [end](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [is optional.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [Exercise 8.2.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [There is a string method called](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [count](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [that is similar to the function in Section 8.7.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [Read the documentation of this method and write an invocation that counts the number of](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [’s in](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) ' [banana](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [Exercise 8.3.](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [A string slice can take a third index that specifies the “step size”; that is, the number](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [of spaces between successive characters. A step size of 2 means every other character; 3 means every](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) third, etc.

Chapter 8. Strings

### [>>> fruit =](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [banana](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) >>> fruit[0:5:2] ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [bnn](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) ['](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[A step size of -1 goes through the word backwards, so the slice](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [::-1] generates a reversed string.

Use this idiom to write a one-line version of [is_palindrome](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [from Exercise 6.3.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) Exercise 8.4. The following functions are all intended to check whether a string contains any [lowercase letters, but at least some of them are wrong. For each function, describe what the function](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [actually does (assuming that the parameter is a string).](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

### [def any_lowercase1(s):](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [for c in s:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [if c.islower():](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) return True [else:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [return False](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

```
def any_lowercase2(s):
for c in s:
if ' c ' .islower():
return ' True '
else:
return ' False '
```

### [def any_lowercase3(s):](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [for c in s:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [flag = c.islower()](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) return flag

### [def any_lowercase4(s):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [flag = False](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [for c in s:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [flag = flag or c.islower()](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [return flag](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py)

[def any_lowercase5(s):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) for c in s: if not c.islower(): return False [return True](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) Exercise 8.5. A Caesar cypher is a weak form of encryption that involves “rotating” each letter by a fixed number of places. To rotate a letter means to shift it through the alphabet, wrapping around [to the beginning if necessary, so ’A’ rotated by 3 is ’D’ and ’Z’ rotated by 1 is ’A’.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[To rotate a word, rotate each letter by the same amount. For example, “cheer” rotated by 7 is “jolly”](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [and “melon” rotated by -10 is “cubed”. In the movie](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [2001: A Space Odyssey](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [, the ship computer](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [is called HAL, which is IBM rotated by -1.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

[Write a function called](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [rotate_word](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [that takes a string and an integer as parameters, and returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [a new string that contains the letters from the original string rotated by the given amount.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

You might want to use the built-in function ord , which converts a character to a numeric code, and

### 8.13. Exercises

[chr](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [, which converts numeric codes to characters. Letters of the alphabet are encoded in alphabetical](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) order, so for example:

```
>>> ord( ' c ' ) - ord( ' a ' )
```

Because ' c ' [is the two-eth letter of the alphabet.](https://thinkpython.com/code) But beware: the numeric codes for upper case letters are different.

Potentially offensive jokes on the Internet are sometimes encoded in ROT13, which is a Caesar [cypher with rotation 13. If you are not easily offended, find and decode some of them. Solution:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [https: // thinkpython. com/ code/ rotate. py](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

# [Case study: word play](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[This chapter presents the second case study, which involves solving word puzzles by](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [searching for words that have certain properties. For example, we’ll find the longest palin-](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) dromes in English and search for words whose letters appear in alphabetical order. ## [9.1](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Reading word lists](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

For the exercises in this chapter we need a list of English words. There are lots of word lists available on the Web, but the one most suitable for our purpose is one of the word lists [collected and contributed to the public domain by Grady Ward as part of the Moby lexi-](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [con project (see](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [). It is a list of 113,809 official](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [crosswords; that is, words that are considered valid in crossword puzzles and other word](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [games. In the Moby collection, the filename is](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [113809of.fic](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [; you can download a copy,](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) with the simpler name words.txt , from [https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) .

[This file is in plain text, so you can open it with a text editor, but you can also read it from](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) Python. The built-in function open takes the name of the file as a parameter and returns a file object you can use to read the file.

### [>>> fin = open(](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [words.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

[fin](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [is a common name for a file object used for input.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) The file object provides several [methods for reading, including](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [readline](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) , which reads characters from the file until it gets [to a newline and returns the result as a string:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### >>> fin.readline() ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [aa\n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

[The first word in this particular list is “aa”, which is a kind of lava.](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) The sequence \n [represents the newline character that separates this word from the next.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[The file object keeps track of where it is in the file, so if you call](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [readline](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [again, you get](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [the next word:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

### [>>> fin.readline()](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) ' aah\n '

```
Chapter 9. Case study: word play
```

[The next word is “aah”, which is a perfectly legitimate word, so stop looking at me like](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) that. [Or, if it’s the newline character that’s bothering you, we can get rid of it with the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [string method](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [strip](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [>>> line = fin.readline()](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [>>> word = line.strip()](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) >>> word ['](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [aahed](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) ['](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[You can also use a file object as part of a](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) for loop. [This program reads](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [words.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [prints each word, one per line:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

### fin = open( ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [words.txt](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [for line in fin:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) word = line.strip() [print(word)](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)
