### 9.3. Search

## [9.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Search](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[All of the exercises in the previous section have something in common; they can be solved](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) with the search pattern we saw in Section 8.6. The simplest example is:

```
def has_no_e(word):
for letter in word:
if letter == ' e ' :
return False
return True
```

[The](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [loop traverses the characters in](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [word](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) . If we find the letter “e”, we can immediately return [False](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [; otherwise we have to go to the next letter. If we exit the loop normally, that](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [means we didn’t find an “e”, so we return](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [True](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [.](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

You could write this function more concisely using the in operator, but I started with this version because it demonstrates the logic of the search pattern.

[avoids](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [is a more general version of](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) has_no_e but it has the same structure:

### [def avoids(word, forbidden):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [for letter in word:](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [if letter in forbidden:](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [return False](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [return True](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

| We can return | False | as soon as we find a forbidden letter; if we get to the end of the loop, |
| ------------- | ----- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| we return     | True  | .                                                                        |

[uses_only](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) is similar except that the sense of the condition is reversed:

### def uses_only(word, available): [for letter in word:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [if letter not in available:](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) return False [return True](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Instead of a list of forbidden letters, we have a list of available letters. If we find a letter in](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [word](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) that is not in available , we can return False .

[uses_all](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) is similar except that we reverse the role of the word and the string of letters:

### def uses_all(word, required): for letter in required: [if letter not in word:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [return False](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) return True

Instead of traversing the letters in word [, the loop traverses the required letters. If any of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [required letters do not appear in the word, we can return](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) False [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

[If you were really thinking like a computer scientist, you would have recognized that](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [uses_all](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [was an instance of a previously solved problem, and you would have written:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

### [def uses_all(word, required):](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [return uses_only(required, word)](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

[This is an example of a program development plan called](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [reduction to a previously solved](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newline) [problem](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [, which means that you recognize the problem you are working on as an instance](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) of a solved problem and apply an existing solution.

```
Chapter 9. Case study: word play
```

## [9.4](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Looping with indices](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[I wrote the functions in the previous section with](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [for](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) loops because I only needed the characters in the strings; I didn’t have to do anything with the indices.

For is_abecedarian [we have to compare adjacent letters, which is a little tricky with a](https://thinkpython.com/code) for loop:

### [def is_abecedarian(word):](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [previous = word[0]](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [for c in word:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [if c < previous:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [return False](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [previous = c](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [return True](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

An alternative is to use recursion:

```
def is_abecedarian(word):
if len(word) <= 1:
return True
if word[0] > word[1]:
return False
return is_abecedarian(word[1:])
```

[Another option is to use a](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [while](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [loop:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

### [def is_abecedarian(word):](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) i = 0 while i < len(word)-1: if word[i+1] < word[i]: [return False](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [i = i+1](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [return True](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[The loop starts at](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [i=0](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [and ends when](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [i=len(word)-1](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [. Each time through the loop, it com-](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) pares the [i](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) th character (which you can think of as the current character) to the [i](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [1th](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) character (which you can think of as the next).

If the next character is less than (alphabetically before) the current one, then we have discovered a break in the abecedarian trend, and we return False .

[If we get to the end of the loop without finding a fault, then the word passes the test. To](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) convince yourself that the loop ends correctly, consider an example like ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [flossy](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [The](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [length of the word is 6, so the last time the loop runs is when](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [i](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) is 4, which is the index of [the second-to-last character. On the last iteration, it compares the second-to-last character](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) to the last, which is what we want.

[Here is a version of](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [is_palindrome](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [(see Exercise 6.3) that uses two indices; one starts at](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [the beginning and goes up; the other starts at the end and goes down.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

### [def is_palindrome(word):](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [i = 0](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) j = len(word)-1

```
while i<j:
if word[i] != word[j]:
```

### 9.5. Debugging

### [return False](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) i = i+1 [j = j-1](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [return True](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[Or we could reduce to a previously solved problem and write:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

### [def is_palindrome(word):](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [return is_reverse(word, word)](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[Using](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [is_reverse](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [from Section 8.11.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

## [9.5](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) Debugging

[Testing programs is hard. The functions in this chapter are relatively easy to test because](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) you can check the results by hand. Even so, it is somewhere between difficult and impos- [sible to choose a set of words that test for all possible errors.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

Taking has_no_e as an example, there are two obvious cases to check: words that have an [‘e’ should return](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [False](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [, and words that don’t should return](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [True](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [You should have no](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [trouble coming up with one of each.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[Within each case, there are some less obvious subcases. Among the words that have an](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) “e”, you should test words with an “e” at the beginning, the end, and somewhere in the [middle. You should test long words, short words, and very short words, like the empty](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) string. The empty string is an example of a special case , which is one of the non-obvious [cases where errors often lurk.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[In addition to the test cases you generate, you can also test your program with a word list](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [like](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [words.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [. By scanning the output, you might be able to catch errors, but be careful:](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [you might catch one kind of error (words that should not be included, but are) and not](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [another (words that should be included, but aren’t).](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

In general, testing can help you find bugs, but it is not easy to generate a good set of [test cases, and even if you do, you can’t be sure your program is correct. According to a](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) legendary computer scientist:

Program testing can be used to show the presence of bugs, but never to show [their absence!](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

- [— Edsger W. Dijkstra](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

## [9.6](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[file object:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [A value that represents an open file.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[reduction to a previously solved problem:](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [A way of solving a problem by expressing it](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [as an instance of a previously solved problem.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[special case:](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [A test case that is atypical or non-obvious (and less likely to be handled cor-](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) rectly).

```

[Exercise 9.7.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [This question is based on a Puzzler that was broadcast on the radio program](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Car](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) Talk ( http: // www. cartalk. com/ content/ puzzlers ):

[Give me a word with three consecutive double letters. I’ll give you a couple of words](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) that almost qualify, but don’t. For example, the word committee, c-o-m-m-i-t-t-e-e. It [would be great except for the ‘i’ that sneaks in there. Or Mississippi: M-i-s-s-i-s-s-i-](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) p-p-i. If you could take out those i’s it would work. But there is a word that has three [consecutive pairs of letters and to the best of my knowledge this may be the only word.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [Of course there are probably 500 more but I can only think of one. What is the word?](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[Write a program to find it. Solution:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [https: // thinkpython. com/ code/ cartalk1. py](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [Exercise](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [9.8.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [Here’s](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) another Car Talk Puzzler ( http: // www. cartalk. com/ content/ puzzlers [):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5)

[“I was driving on the highway the other day and I happened to notice my odometer.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Like most odometers, it shows six digits, in whole miles only. So, if my car had 300,000](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [miles, for example, I’d see 3-0-0-0-0-0.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

[“Now, what I saw that day was very interesting. I noticed that the last 4 digits were](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [palindromic; that is, they read the same forward as backward. For example, 5-4-4-5 is a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [palindrome, so my odometer could have read 3-1-5-4-4-5.](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

“One mile later, the last 5 numbers were palindromic. For example, it could have read [3-6-5-4-5-6. One mile after that, the middle 4 out of 6 numbers were palindromic. And](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) you ready for this? One mile later, all 6 were palindromic!

[“The question is, what was on the odometer when I first looked?”](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[Write a Python program that tests all the six-digit numbers and prints any numbers that satisfy](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [these requirements. Solution:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) https: // thinkpython. com/ code/ cartalk2. py [.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Exercise 9.9.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Here’s another](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Car Talk](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Puzzler you can solve with a search (](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [http: // www.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [cartalk. com/ content/ puzzlers](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) ):

[“Recently I had a visit with my mom and we realized that the two digits that make](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) up my age when reversed resulted in her age. For example, if she’s 73, I’m 37. We wondered how often this has happened over the years but we got sidetracked with other topics and we never came up with an answer.

[“When I got home I figured out that the digits of our ages have been reversible six times](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [so far. I also figured out that if we’re lucky it would happen again in a few years, and](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [if we’re really lucky it would happen one more time after that. In other words, it would](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) have happened 8 times over all. So the question is, how old am I now?”

[Write a Python program that searches for solutions to this Puzzler. Hint: you might find the string](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) method [zfill](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [useful.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

## [Chapter 10](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

# [Lists](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

[This chapter presents one of Python’s most useful built-in types, lists. You will also learn](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) more about objects and what can happen when you have more than one name for the same [object.](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

## [10.1](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [A list is a sequence](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

Like a string, a list is a sequence of values. In a string, the values are characters; in a list, [they can be any type. The values in a list are called](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [elements](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [or sometimes](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [items](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

There are several ways to create a new list; the simplest is to enclose the elements in square [brackets (](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [[](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [and](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) []](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [):](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

```
[10, 20, 30, 40]
[ ' crunchy frog ' , ' ram bladder ' , ' lark vomit ' ]
```

The first example is a list of four integers. The second is a list of three strings. The elements of a list don’t have to be the same type. The following list contains a string, a float, an integer, and (lo!) another list:

### [ ' spam ' , 2.0, 5, [10, 20]]

A list within another list is nested .

[A list that contains no elements is called an empty list; you can create one with empty](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [brackets,](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [[]](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[As you might expect, you can assign list values to variables:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

```
>>> cheeses = [ ' Cheddar ' , ' Edam ' , ' Gouda ' ]
>>> numbers = [42, 123]
>>> empty = []
>>> print(cheeses, numbers, empty)
[ ' Cheddar ' , ' Edam ' , ' Gouda ' ] [42, 123] []
```

Chapter 10. Lists

### [list](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### cheeses [’Cheddar’](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

### [’Edam’](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [’Gouda’](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[Figure 10.1: State diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

## 10.2 [Lists are mutable](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

The syntax for accessing the elements of a list is the same as for accessing the characters [of a string—the bracket operator. The expression inside the brackets specifies the index.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [Remember that the indices start at 0:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [>>> cheeses[0]](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) ' Cheddar ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [Unlike strings, lists are mutable. When the bracket operator appears on the left side of an](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [assignment, it identifies the element of the list that will be assigned.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) >>> numbers = [42, 123] [>>> numbers[1] = 5](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [>>> numbers](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_rankings) [42, 5] [The one-eth element of](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [numbers](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [, which used to be 123, is now 5.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[Figure 10.1 shows the state diagram for](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) cheeses , numbers and empty .

[Lists are represented by boxes with the word “list” outside and the elements of the list](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) inside. cheeses refers to a list with three elements indexed 0, 1 and 2. numbers contains two elements; the diagram shows that the value of the second element has been reassigned from 123 to 5. empty [refers to a list with no elements.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

[List indices work the same way as string indices:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

- [• Any integer expression can be used as an index.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

- [• If you try to read or write an element that does not exist, you get an](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) IndexError .

- [• If an index has a negative value, it counts backward from the end of the list.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

The in [operator also works on lists.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [>>> cheeses = [](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) ['](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) Cheddar ' , ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [Edam](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [Gouda](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) []](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) >>> ' Edam ' [in cheeses](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) True >>> ' Brie ['](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [in cheeses](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) False

### 10.3. Traversing a list

## [10.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Traversing a list](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[The most common way to traverse the elements of a list is with a](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) for [loop. The syntax is](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [the same as for strings:](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

### for cheese in cheeses: print(cheese)

[This works well if you only need to read the elements of the list. But if you want to write](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [or update the elements, you need the indices. A common way to do that is to combine the](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [built-in functions](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [range](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [len](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

### [for i in range(len(numbers)):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [numbers[i] = numbers[i] * 2](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

This loop traverses the list and updates each element. len [returns the number of elements](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [in the list.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [range](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) returns a list of indices from 0 to n − 1, where n is the length of the list. [Each time through the loop](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [i](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [gets the index of the next element. The assignment statement](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [in the body uses](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [i](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) to read the old value of the element and to assign the new value.

[A](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [loop over an empty list never runs the body:](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

### [for x in []:](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [This never happens.](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[Although a list can contain another list, the nested list still counts as a single element. The](https://thinkpython.com/code/GoodKangaroo.py) [length of this list is four:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

```
[ ' spam ' , 1, [ ' Brie ' , ' Roquefort ' , ' Pol le Veq ' ], [1, 2, 3]]
```

## [10.4](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [List operations](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[The](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [+](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [operator concatenates lists:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [>>> a = [1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [>>> b = [4, 5, 6]](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [>>> c = a + b](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [>>> c](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6]

The * [operator repeats a list a given number of times:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

### [>>> [0] * 4](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [0, 0, 0, 0] [>>> [1, 2, 3] * 3](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [[1, 2, 3, 1, 2, 3, 1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[The first example repeats](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [[0]](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [four times. The second example repeats the list](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [[1, 2, 3]](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [three times.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

## 10.5 List slices

The slice operator also works on lists:

Chapter 10. Lists

```
>>> t = [ ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' , ' d ' , ' e ' , ' f ' ]
>>> t[1:3]
[ ' b ' , ' c ' ]
>>> t[:4]
[ ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' , ' d ' ]
>>> t[3:]
[ ' d ' , ' e ' , ' f ' ]
```

If you omit the first index, the slice starts at the beginning. If you omit the second, the slice [goes to the end. So if you omit both, the slice is a copy of the whole list.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

```
>>> t[:]
[ ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' , ' d ' , ' e ' , ' f ' ]
```

Since lists are mutable, it is often useful to make a copy before performing operations that [modify lists.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

A slice operator on the left side of an assignment can update multiple elements:

```
>>> t = [ ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' , ' d ' , ' e ' , ' f ' ]
>>> t[1:3] = [ ' x ' , ' y ' ]
>>> t
[ ' a ' , ' x ' , ' y ' , ' d ' , ' e ' , ' f ' ]
```

## [10.6](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) [List methods](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

Python provides methods that operate on lists. For example, append [adds a new element](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) to the end of a list:

```
>>> t = [ ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' ]
>>> t.append( ' d ' )
>>> t
[ ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' , ' d ' ]
```

[extend](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [takes a list as an argument and appends all of the elements:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

```
>>> t1 = [ ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' ]
>>> t2 = [ ' d ' , ' e ' ]
>>> t1.extend(t2)
>>> t1
[ ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' , ' d ' , ' e ' ]
```

This example leaves t2 unmodified.

sort [arranges the elements of the list from low to high:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

```
>>> t = [ ' d ' , ' c ' , ' e ' , ' b ' , ' a ' ]
>>> t.sort()
>>> t
[ ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' , ' d ' , ' e ' ]
```

[Most list methods are void; they modify the list and return](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [None](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) [. If you accidentally write](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) t = t.sort() , you will be disappointed with the result.

### 10.7. Map, filter and reduce

## [10.7](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Map, filter and reduce](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[To add up all the numbers in a list, you can use a loop like this:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [def add_all(t):](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [total = 0](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) for x in t: [total += x](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [return total](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[total](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [is initialized to 0.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [Each time through the loop,](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [x](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [gets one element from the list.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [The](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [+=](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [operator provides a short way to update a variable. This](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) augmented assignment [statement](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

### [total += x](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[is equivalent to](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5)

### total = total + x

As the loop runs, [total](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) accumulates the sum of the elements; a variable used this way is [sometimes called an](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [accumulator](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[Adding up the elements of a list is such a common operation that Python provides it as a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [built-in function,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [sum](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

### [>>> t = [1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [>>> sum(t)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point2_soln.py)

An operation like this that combines a sequence of elements into a single value is sometimes called reduce .

[Sometimes you want to traverse one list while building another. For example, the following](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) [function takes a list of strings and returns a new list that contains capitalized strings:](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

### [def capitalize_all(t):](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [res = []](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) for s in t: [res.append(s.capitalize())](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [return res](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

res is initialized with an empty list; each time through the loop, we append the next element. So [res](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [is another kind of accumulator.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

[An operation like](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [capitalize_all](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [is sometimes called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [map](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) because it “maps” a function [(in this case the method](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [capitalize](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ) onto each of the elements in a sequence.

[Another common operation is to select some of the elements from a list and return a sublist.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [For example, the following function takes a list of strings and returns a list that contains](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [only the uppercase strings:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

### [def only_upper(t):](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) res = [] [for s in t:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [if s.isupper():](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [res.append(s)](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) return res

Chapter 10. Lists

[isupper](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [is a string method that returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [True](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) if the string contains only upper case letters.

An operation like only_upper [is called a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [filter](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [because it selects some of the elements and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [filters out the others.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Most common list operations can be expressed as a combination of map, filter and reduce.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

## [10.8](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [Deleting elements](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[There are several ways to delete elements from a list. If you know the index of the element](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [you want, you can use](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [pop](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [>>> t = [](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [b](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [c](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) []](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) >>> x = t.pop(1) [>>> t](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [ ' a ' , ' [c](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) []](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) >>> x ' b ' pop [modifies the list and returns the element that was removed. If you don’t provide an](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [index, it deletes and returns the last element.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py)

[If you don’t need the removed value, you can use the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [del](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [operator:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) >>> t = [ ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [b](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) ## 10.9 [Lists and strings](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

[A string is a sequence of characters and a list is a sequence of values, but a list of characters](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [is not the same as a string. To convert from a string to a list of characters, you can use](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [list](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

```
>>> s = ' spam '
>>> t = list(s)
>>> t
[ ' s ' , ' p ' , ' a ' , ' m ' ]
```

### 10.10. Objects and values

### [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [’banana’](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) ’banana’ [b](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [’banana’](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [b](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[Figure 10.2: State diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[Because](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [list](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [is the name of a built-in function, you should avoid using it as a variable](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [name. I also avoid](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [l](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) because it looks too much like [. So that’s why I use](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [t](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py)

[The](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [list](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [function breaks a string into individual letters. If you want to break a string into](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [words, you can use the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [split](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [method:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

```
>>> s = ' pining for the fjords '
>>> t = s.split()
>>> t
[ ' pining ' , ' for ' , ' the ' , ' fjords ' ]
```

[An optional argument called a](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [delimiter](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) specifies which characters to use as word bound- [aries. The following example uses a hyphen as a delimiter:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

```
>>> s = ' spam-spam-spam '
>>> delimiter = ' - '
>>> t = s.split(delimiter)
>>> t
[ ' spam ' , ' spam ' , ' spam ' ]
```

[join](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [is the inverse of](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [split](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [. It takes a list of strings and concatenates the elements.](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [join](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [is](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) a string method, so you have to invoke it on the delimiter and pass the list as a parameter:

```
>>> t = [ ' pining ' , ' for ' , ' the ' , ' fjords ' ]
>>> delimiter = ' '
>>> s = delimiter.join(t)
>>> s
' pining for the fjords '
```

[In this case the delimiter is a space character, so](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) join [puts a space between words.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [To](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) concatenate strings without spaces, you can use the empty string, '' , as a delimiter.

## [10.10](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [Objects and values](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

[If we run these assignment statements:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### [a =](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) ['](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [banana](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) ['](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [b =](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [banana](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[We know that](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [b](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [both refer to a string, but we don’t know whether they refer to the](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [same](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [string. There are two possible states, shown in Figure 10.2.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

[In one case,](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [b](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [refer to two different objects that have the same value. In the second](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [case, they refer to the same object.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

To check whether two variables refer to the same object, you can use the is operator.

Chapter 10. Lists

### [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [[ 1, 2, 3 ]](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### [b](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [[ 1, 2, 3 ]](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[Figure 10.3: State diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

a [[ 1, 2, 3 ]](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [b](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[Figure 10.4: State diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py)

>>> a = ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [banana](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [>>> b =](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [banana](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) >>> a is b True

[In this example, Python only created one string object, and both](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) a and b refer to it. But [when you create two lists, you get two objects:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

### [>>> a = [1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [>>> b = [1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [>>> a is b](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [False](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py)

[So the state diagram looks like Figure 10.3.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

In this case we would say that the two lists are equivalent , because they have the same elements, but not [identical](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) , because they are not the same object. If two objects are identical, they are also equivalent, but if they are equivalent, they are not necessarily identical.

[Until now, we have been using “object” and “value” interchangeably, but it is more precise](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [to say that an object has a value. If you evaluate](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [[1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [, you get a list object whose](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [value is a sequence of integers.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [If another list has the same elements, we say it has the](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [same value, but it is not the same object.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

## 10.11 Aliasing

If a [refers to an object and you assign](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [b = a](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [, then both variables refer to the same object:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

### [>>> a = [1, 2, 3]](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) >>> b = a >>> b is a True

[The state diagram looks like Figure 10.4.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[The association of a variable with an object is called a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [reference](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [. In this example, there are](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [two references to the same object.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

[An object with more than one reference has more than one name, so we say that the object](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) is aliased .

If the aliased object is mutable, changes made with one alias affect the other:

### 10.12. List arguments

### [list](https://thinkpython.com/code/letters.py) [__main__](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [letters](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [’a’](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

### ’b’ [delete_head](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [t](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ’c’

Figure 10.5: Stack diagram.

### [>>> b[0] = 42](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [>>> a](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [[42, 2, 3]](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[Although this behavior can be useful, it is error-prone.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [In general, it is safer to avoid](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) aliasing when you are working with mutable objects.

[For immutable objects like strings, aliasing is not as much of a problem. In this example:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

### [a =](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [banana](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) b = ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [banana](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

It almost never makes a difference whether a and b [refer to the same string or not.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py)

## [10.12](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [List arguments](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[When you pass a list to a function, the function gets a reference to the list. If the function](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) modifies the list, the caller sees the change. For example, delete_head removes the first [element from a list:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

### [def delete_head(t):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [del t[0]](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Here’s how it is used:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

```
>>> letters = [ ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' ]
>>> delete_head(letters)
>>> letters
[ ' b ' , ' c ' ]
```

The parameter t and the variable letters are aliases for the same object. The stack diagram looks like Figure 10.5.

Since the list is shared by two frames, I drew it between them.

[It is important to distinguish between operations that modify lists and operations that cre-](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [ate new lists. For example, the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [append](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [method modifies a list, but the](http://gutenberg.org/) [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [operator creates a](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) new list.

[Here’s an example using](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [append](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

### [>>> t1 = [1, 2]](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) >>> t2 = t1.append(3) >>> t1 [[1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [>>> t2](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) None

Chapter 10. Lists

[The return value from](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [append](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [is](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [None](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

Here’s an example using the + [operator:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

### [>>> t3 = t1 + [4]](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) >>> t1 [1, 2, 3] [>>> t3](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [1, 2, 3, 4]

[The result of the operator is a new list, and the original list is unchanged.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[This difference is important when you write functions that are supposed to modify lists.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [For example, this function](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [does not](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [delete the head of a list:](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

### [def bad_delete_head(t):](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [t = t[1:]](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [# WRONG!](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[The slice operator creates a new list and the assignment makes](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [t](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) refer to it, but that doesn’t affect the caller.

### [>>> t4 = [1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [>>> bad_delete_head(t4)](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [>>> t4](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [[1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py)

[At the beginning of](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [bad_delete_head](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [,](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [t](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [and](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [t4](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [refer to the same list. At the end,](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [t](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [refers](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [to a new list, but](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) [t4](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) still refers to the original, unmodified list.

An alternative is to write a function that creates and returns a new list. For example, [tail](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [returns all but the first element of a list:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

### [def tail(t):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_rankings) [return t[1:]](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[This function leaves the original list unmodified. Here’s how it is used:](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

```
>>> letters = [ ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' ]
>>> rest = tail(letters)
>>> rest
[ ' b ' , ' c ' ]
```

## 10.13 Debugging

[Careless use of lists (and other mutable objects) can lead to long hours of debugging. Here](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [are some common pitfalls and ways to avoid them:](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

1. [1. Most list methods modify the argument and return](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [None](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [. This is the opposite of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [string methods, which return a new string and leave the original alone.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

If you are used to writing string code like this:

### word = word.strip()

It is tempting to write list code like this:

### 10.13. Debugging

### [t = t.sort()](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [# WRONG!](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Because](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [sort](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [returns](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [None](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) , the next operation you perform with [t](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [is likely to fail.](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

Before using list methods and operators, you should read the documentation care- [fully and then test them in interactive mode.](https://thinkpython.com/code)

2. [2. Pick an idiom and stick with it.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[Part of the problem with lists is that there are too many ways to do things. For exam-](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [ple, to remove an element from a list, you can use](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [pop](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [remove](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [del](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [, or even a slice](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [assignment.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[To add an element, you can use the](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [append](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [method or the](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [operator. Assuming that](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) t is a list and x is a list element, these are correct:

### [t.append(x)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [t = t + [x]](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [t += [x]](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

And these are wrong:

### [t.append([x])](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [# WRONG!](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [t = t.append(x)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [# WRONG!](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [t + [x]](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) # WRONG! [t = t + x](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [# WRONG!](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

Try out each of these examples in interactive mode to make sure you understand what they do. Notice that only the last one causes a runtime error; the other three are [legal, but they do the wrong thing.](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py)

3. [3. Make copies to avoid aliasing.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[If you want to use a method like](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [sort](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [that modifies the argument, but you need to](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) keep the original list as well, you can make a copy.

### >>> t = [3, 1, 2] >>> t2 = t[:] >>> t2.sort() >>> t [[3, 1, 2]](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [>>> t2](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [1, 2, 3]

[In this example you could also use the built-in function](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [sorted](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [, which returns a new,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [sorted list and leaves the original alone.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

### >>> t2 = sorted(t) [>>> t](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [[3, 1, 2]](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [>>> t2](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [1, 2, 3]

Chapter 10. Lists

## [10.14](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

list: A sequence of values.

[element:](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) One of the values in a list (or other sequence), also called items.

nested list: A list that is an element of another list.

[accumulator:](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [A variable used in a loop to add up or accumulate a result.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[augmented assignment:](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [A statement that updates the value of a variable using an opera-](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [tor like](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [+=](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[reduce:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [A processing pattern that traverses a sequence and accumulates the elements into](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [a single result.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[map:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) A processing pattern that traverses a sequence and performs an operation on each [element.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[filter:](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) A processing pattern that traverses a list and selects the elements that satisfy some criterion.

object: Something a variable can refer to. An object has a type and a value.

[equivalent:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [Having the same value.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

identical: [Being the same object (which implies equivalence).](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[reference:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [The association between a variable and its value.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[aliasing:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) A circumstance where two or more variables refer to the same object.

delimiter: A character or string used to indicate where a string should be split.

For example:

### 10.15. Exercises

[Exercise 10.4.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Write a function called](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [chop](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) that takes a list, modifies it by removing the first and last elements, and returns [None](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [. For example:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[>>> t = [1, 2, 3, 4]](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [>>> chop(t)](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [>>> t](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [2, 3] [Exercise 10.5.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [Write a function called](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [is_sorted](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) that takes a list as a parameter and returns True if the list is sorted in ascending order and False otherwise. For example:

[>>> is_sorted([1, 2, 2])](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [True](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [>>> is_sorted([](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [b](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [])](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [False](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [Exercise 10.6.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [Two words are anagrams if you can rearrange the letters from one to spell the other.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Write a function called](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) is_anagram that takes two strings and returns True if they are anagrams. [Exercise 10.7.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [Write a function called](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [has_duplicates](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [that takes a list and returns](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) True if there is any element that appears more than once. It should not modify the original list. [Exercise 10.8.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [This exercise pertains to the so-called Birthday Paradox, which you can read about](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [at](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [http: // en. wikipedia. org/ wiki/ Birthday_ paradox](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) .

[If there are 23 students in your class, what are the chances that two of them have the same birthday?](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [You can estimate this probability by generating random samples of 23 birthdays and checking for](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [matches.](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) Hint: you can generate random birthdays with the [randint](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [function in the](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [random](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [module.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[You can download my solution from](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [https: // thinkpython. com/ code/ birthday. py](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Exercise 10.9.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Write a function that reads the file](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) words.txt and builds a list with one element per word. Write two versions of this function, one using the [append](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [method and the other using](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [the idiom](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [t = t + [x]](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [. Which one takes longer to run? Why?](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[Solution:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [https: // thinkpython. com/ code/ wordlist. py](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [.](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [Exercise 10.10.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [To check whether a word is in the word list, you could use the](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [in](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) operator, but it [would be slow because it searches through the words in order.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

Because the words are in alphabetical order, we can speed things up with a bisection search (also [known as binary search), which is similar to what you do when you look a word up in the dictionary](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) (the book, not the data structure). You start in the middle and check to see whether the word you are looking for comes before the word in the middle of the list. If so, you search the first half of the list the same way. Otherwise you search the second half.

Either way, you cut the remaining search space in half. If the word list has 113,809 words, it will [take about 17 steps to find the word or conclude that it’s not there.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

Write a function called [in_bisect](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [that takes a sorted list and a target value and returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [True](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [if](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [the word is in the list and](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [False](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [if it’s not.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

[Or you could read the documentation of the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [bisect](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [module and use that!](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) Solution: https: [// thinkpython. com/ code/ inlist. py](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [Exercise 10.11.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [Two words are a “reverse pair” if each is the reverse of the other. Write a program](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [that finds all the reverse pairs in the word list. Solution:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) https: // thinkpython. com/ code/ [reverse_ pair. py](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [Exercise 10.12.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [Two words “interlock” if taking alternating letters from each forms a new](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) word. For example, “shoe” and “cold” interlock to form “schooled”. Solution: https: //
