## [Chapter 11](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

# [Dictionaries](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[This chapter presents another built-in type called a dictionary.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) Dictionaries are one of [Python’s best features; they are the building blocks of many efficient and elegant algo-](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [rithms.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py)

## [11.1](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [A dictionary is a mapping](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[A](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [dictionary](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [is like a list, but more general. In a list, the indices have to be integers; in a](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [dictionary they can be (almost) any type.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[A dictionary contains a collection of indices, which are called](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [keys](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [, and a collection of](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [values. Each key is associated with a single value. The association of a key and a value is](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) key-value pair or sometimes an item .

[In mathematical language, a dictionary represents a](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [mapping](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [from keys to values, so you](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [can also say that each key “maps to” a value. As an example, we’ll build a dictionary that](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) maps from English to Spanish words, so the keys and the values are all strings.

[The function](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) dict creates a new dictionary with no items. Because dict is the name of a built-in function, you should avoid using it as a variable name.

### [>>> eng2sp = dict()](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) >>> eng2sp [{}](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

[The squiggly-brackets,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [{}](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [, represent an empty dictionary. To add items to the dictionary,](http://gutenberg.org/) you can use square brackets:

### [>>> eng2sp[](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [one](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [] =](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [uno](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[This line creates an item that maps from the key](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [one](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [to the value](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [uno](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) . If we print the [dictionary again, we see a key-value pair with a colon between the key and value:](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

```
>>> eng2sp
{ ' one ' : ' uno ' }
```

[This output format is also an input format. For example, you can create a new dictionary](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) with three items:

Chapter 11. Dictionaries

```
>>> eng2sp = { ' one ' : ' uno ' , ' two ' : ' dos ' , ' three ' : ' tres ' }
```

But if you print eng2sp [, you might be surprised:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

```
>>> eng2sp
{ ' one ' : ' uno ' , ' three ' : ' tres ' , ' two ' : ' dos ' }
```

[The order of the key-value pairs might not be the same.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [If you type the same example](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) on your computer, you might get a different result. In general, the order of items in a [dictionary is unpredictable.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[But that’s not a problem because the elements of a dictionary are never indexed with inte-](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [ger indices. Instead, you use the keys to look up the corresponding values:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

### [>>> eng2sp[](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [two](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) []](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ' dos '

The key ' [two](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) always maps to the value ' dos ' so the order of the items doesn’t matter.

If the key isn’t in the dictionary, you get an exception:

```
>>> eng2sp[ ' four ' ]
KeyError: ' four '
```

The len [function works on dictionaries; it returns the number of key-value pairs:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal)

### [>>> len(eng2sp)](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py)

[The](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [in](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [operator works on dictionaries, too; it tells you whether something appears as a](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [key](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [in the dictionary (appearing as a value is not good enough).](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

```
>>> ' one ' in eng2sp
True
>>> ' uno ' in eng2sp
False
```

[To see whether something appears as a value in a dictionary, you can use the method](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) values [, which returns a collection of values, and then use the](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [in](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [operator:](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt)

### [>>> vals = eng2sp.values()](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [>>>](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [uno](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [in vals](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) True

The in operator uses different algorithms for lists and dictionaries. For lists, it searches the [elements of the list in order, as in Section 8.6. As the list gets longer, the search time gets](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [longer in direct proportion.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

[Python dictionaries use a data structure called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [hashtable](http://gutenberg.org/) that has a remarkable property: the in [operator takes about the same amount of time no matter how many items are in the](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [dictionary. I explain how that’s possible in Section B.4, but the explanation might not make](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [sense until you’ve read a few more chapters.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

## 11.2 [Dictionary as a collection of counters](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[Suppose you are given a string and you want to count how many times each letter appears.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) There are several ways you could do it:

### 11.2. Dictionary as a collection of counters

1. [1. You could create 26 variables, one for each letter of the alphabet. Then you could tra-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [verse the string and, for each character, increment the corresponding counter, proba-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [bly using a chained conditional.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

2. You could create a list with 26 elements. Then you could convert each character to a number (using the built-in function [ord](https://thinkpython.com/code) ), use the number as an index into the list, [and increment the appropriate counter.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

3. You could create a dictionary with characters as keys and counters as the correspond- [ing values. The first time you see a character, you would add an item to the dictionary.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [After that you would increment the value of an existing item.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

[Each of these options performs the same computation, but each of them implements that](http://www.thinkpython.com/) computation in a different way.

An implementation is a way of performing a computation; some implementations are [better than others. For example, an advantage of the dictionary implementation is that we](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [don’t have to know ahead of time which letters appear in the string and we only have to](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [make room for the letters that do appear.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

Here is what the code might look like:

### [def histogram(s):](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) d = dict() [for c in s:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) if c not in d: [d[c] = 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [else:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) d[c] += 1 [return d](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

[The name of the function is](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) [histogram](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [, which is a statistical term for a collection of counters](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk2.py) [(or frequencies).](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

The first line of the function creates an empty dictionary. The [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [loop traverses the string.](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) Each time through the loop, if the character c is not in the dictionary, we create a new item [with key](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [c](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [and the initial value 1 (since we have seen this letter once). If](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [c](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [is already in the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) dictionary we increment d[c] .

Here’s how it works:

```
>>> h = histogram( ' brontosaurus ' )
>>> h
{ ' a ' : 1, ' b ' : 1, ' o ' : 2, ' n ' : 1, ' s ' : 2, ' r ' : 2, ' u ' : 2, ' t ' : 1}
```

The histogram indicates that the letters ['](http://gutenberg.org/) [a](http://gutenberg.org/) ['](http://gutenberg.org/) [and](http://gutenberg.org/) ['](http://gutenberg.org/) [b](http://gutenberg.org/) ['](http://gutenberg.org/) [appear once;](http://gutenberg.org/) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [o](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [appears twice, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [so on.](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py)

[Dictionaries have a method called](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [get](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [that takes a key and a default value.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [If the key](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [appears in the dictionary,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [get](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [returns the corresponding value; otherwise it returns the](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [default value. For example:](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

```
>>> h = histogram( ' a ' )
>>> h
{ ' a ' : 1}
>>> h.get( ' a ' , 0)
```

Chapter 11. Dictionaries

### >>> h.get( ' c ' , 0)

[As an exercise, use](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [get](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [to write](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [histogram](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) more concisely. You should be able to eliminate the if [statement.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

## [11.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [Looping and dictionaries](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[If you use a dictionary in a](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [statement, it traverses the keys of the dictionary. For exam-](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) ple, print_hist [prints each key and the corresponding value:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

### [def print_hist(h):](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [for c in h:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [print(c, h[c])](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5)

[Here’s what the output looks like:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

```
>>> h = histogram( ' parrot ' )
>>> print_hist(h)
a 1
p 1
r 2
t 1
o 1
```

[Again, the keys are in no particular order. To traverse the keys in sorted order, you can use](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [the built-in function](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) sorted :

### [>>> for key in sorted(h):](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) ... [print(key, h[key])](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) a 1 o 1 p 1 r 2 [t 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py)

## 11.4 Reverse lookup

Given a dictionary [d](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [and a key](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [k](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [, it is easy to find the corresponding value](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [v = d[k]](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [. This](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [operation is called a](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [lookup](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

[But what if you have](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [v](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [and you want to find](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [k](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ? You have two problems: first, there might be more than one key that maps to the value [v](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [. Depending on the application, you might](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [be able to pick one, or you might have to make a list that contains all of them. Second,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [there is no simple syntax to do a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [reverse lookup](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [; you have to search.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[Here is a function that takes a value and returns the first key that maps to that value:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

### [def reverse_lookup(d, v):](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) for k in d: [if d[k] == v:](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [return k](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) raise LookupError()

### 11.5. Dictionaries and lists

[This function is yet another example of the search pattern, but it uses a feature we haven’t](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) seen before, raise . The [raise statement](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [causes an exception; in this case it causes a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [LookupError](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) , which is a built-in exception used to indicate that a lookup operation failed.

If we get to the end of the loop, that means [v](https://thinkpython.com/code) doesn’t appear in the dictionary as a value, so we raise an exception.

Here is an example of a successful reverse lookup:

```
>>> h = histogram( ' parrot ' )
>>> key = reverse_lookup(h, 2)
>>> key
' r '
```

[And an unsuccessful one:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

```
>>> key = reverse_lookup(h, 3)
Traceback (most recent call last):
File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>
File "<stdin>", line 5, in reverse_lookup
LookupError
```

[The effect when you raise an exception is the same as when Python raises one: it prints a](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [traceback and an error message.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[When you raise an exception, you can provide a detailed error message as an optional](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [argument. For example:](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

>>> raise LookupError( ' value does not appear in the dictionary ' ) [Traceback (most recent call last):](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [File "<stdin>", line 1, in ?](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [LookupError: value does not appear in the dictionary](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py)

A reverse lookup is much slower than a forward lookup; if you have to do it often, or if the [dictionary gets big, the performance of your program will suffer.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

## [11.5](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) Dictionaries and lists

Lists can appear as values in a dictionary. For example, if you are given a dictionary that [maps from letters to frequencies, you might want to invert it; that is, create a dictionary](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) that maps from frequencies to letters. Since there might be several letters with the same [frequency, each value in the inverted dictionary should be a list of letters.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

[Here is a function that inverts a dictionary:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[def invert_dict(d):](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [inverse = dict()](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [for key in d:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [val = d[key]](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [if val not in inverse:](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [inverse[val] = [key]](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [else:](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [inverse[val].append(key)](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) return inverse

Chapter 11. Dictionaries

### [dict](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) [dict](https://thinkpython.com/code/letters.py) [list](https://thinkpython.com/code/letters.py)

### hist ’a’ [inv](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [’a’](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

### [’p’](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ’p’

### [’r’](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) ’t’

### [’t’](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [’o’](https://thinkpython.com/code)

### ’o’ list

### [’r’](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py)

[Figure 11.1: State diagram.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

Each time through the loop, key gets a key from d [and](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [val](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [gets the corresponding value.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [If](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [val](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [is not in](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [inverse](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) , that means we haven’t seen it before, so we create a new item and initialize it with a [singleton](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [(a list that contains a single element). Otherwise we have seen](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) this value before, so we append the corresponding key to the list.

[Here is an example:](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

```
>>> hist = histogram( ' parrot ' )
>>> hist
{ ' a ' : 1, ' p ' : 1, ' r ' : 2, ' t ' : 1, ' o ' : 1}
>>> inverse = invert_dict(hist)
>>> inverse
{1: [ ' a ' , ' p ' , ' t ' , ' o ' ], 2: [ ' r ' ]}
```

Figure 11.1 is a state diagram showing hist and inverse . A dictionary is represented as a box with the type [dict](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) above it and the key-value pairs inside. If the values are integers, floats or strings, I draw them inside the box, but I usually draw lists outside the box, just to keep the diagram simple.

[Lists can be values in a dictionary, as this example shows, but they cannot be keys. Here’s](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [what happens if you try:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

```
>>> t = [1, 2, 3]
>>> d = dict()
>>> d[t] = ' oops '
Traceback (most recent call last):
File "<stdin>", line 1, in ?
TypeError: list objects are unhashable
```

I mentioned earlier that a dictionary is implemented using a hashtable and that means that the keys have to be [hashable](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

A hash [is a function that takes a value (of any kind) and returns an integer. Dictionaries](http://gutenberg.org/) [use these integers, called hash values, to store and look up key-value pairs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[This system works fine if the keys are immutable. But if the keys are mutable, like lists,](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [bad things happen. For example, when you create a key-value pair, Python hashes the key](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [and stores it in the corresponding location. If you modify the key and then hash it again, it](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [would go to a different location. In that case you might have two entries for the same key,](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [or you might not be able to find a key. Either way, the dictionary wouldn’t work correctly.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[That’s why keys have to be hashable, and why mutable types like lists aren’t. The simplest](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) way to get around this limitation is to use tuples, which we will see in the next chapter.

### 11.6. Memos

[fibonacci](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law)

| fibonacci | fibonacci |
| --------- | --------- |
| n         | n         |

| fibonacci | fibonacci | fibonacci | fibonacci |
| --------- | --------- | --------- | --------- |
| n         | n         | n         | n         |

| fibonacci | fibonacci |
| --------- | --------- |
| n         | n         |

Figure 11.2: Call graph.

[Since dictionaries are mutable, they can’t be used as keys, but they](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) can [be used as values.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

## [11.6](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [Memos](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[If you played with the](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [fibonacci](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [function from Section 6.7, you might have noticed that](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [the bigger the argument you provide, the longer the function takes to run. Furthermore,](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) the run time increases quickly.

To understand why, consider Figure 11.2, which shows the [call graph](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [for](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [fibonacci](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [with](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [n=4](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

A call graph shows a set of function frames, with lines connecting each frame to the frames [of the functions it calls. At the top of the graph,](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [fibonacci](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [with](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [n=4](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [calls](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [fibonacci](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [with](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [n=3](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [n=2](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [. In turn,](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) fibonacci with n=3 [calls](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [fibonacci](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [with](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [n=2](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [n=1](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [. And so on.](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt)

[Count how many times](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [fibonacci(0)](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [and](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) fibonacci(1) [are called. This is an inefficient](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) solution to the problem, and it gets worse as the argument gets bigger.

One solution is to keep track of values that have already been computed by storing them [in a dictionary. A previously computed value that is stored for later use is called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) memo . Here is a “memoized” version of fibonacci :

### known = {0:0, 1:1}

### def fibonacci(n): [if n in known:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [return known[n]](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

### [res = fibonacci(n-1) + fibonacci(n-2)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) known[n] = res [return res](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[known](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [is a dictionary that keeps track of the Fibonacci numbers we already know. It starts](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) with two items: 0 maps to 0 and 1 maps to 1.

Chapter 11. Dictionaries

[Whenever](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [fibonacci](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [is called, it checks](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [known](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) . If the result is already there, it can return immediately. [Otherwise it has to compute the new value, add it to the dictionary, and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [return it.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py)

[If you run this version of](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [fibonacci](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) and compare it with the original, you will find that it is much faster.

## [11.7](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [Global variables](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[In the previous example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [known](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [is created outside the function, so it belongs to the special](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [frame called](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [__main__](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [. Variables in](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [__main__](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [are sometimes called](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [global](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [because they](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [can be accessed from any function.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [Unlike local variables, which disappear when their](http://www.thinkpython.com/) function ends, global variables persist from one function call to the next.

[It is common to use global variables for](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [flags](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [; that is, boolean variables that indicate (“flag”)](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [whether a condition is true.](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) For example, some programs use a flag named verbose to [control the level of detail in the output:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

### verbose = True

### [def example1():](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [if verbose:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [Running example1](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[If you try to reassign a global variable, you might be surprised. The following example is](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) supposed to keep track of whether the function has been called:

### [been_called = False](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

### def example2(): [been_called = True](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [# WRONG](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[But if you run it you will see that the value of](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [been_called](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [doesn’t change. The problem](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) is that [example2](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [creates a new local variable named](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [been_called](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [. The local variable goes](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) away when the function ends, and has no effect on the global variable.

[To reassign a global variable inside a function you have to](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) declare the global variable before you use it:

### been_called = False

### def example2(): global been_called [been_called = True](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

The global statement [tells the interpreter something like, “In this function, when I say](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) been_called [, I mean the global variable; don’t create a local one.”](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[Here’s an example that tries to update a global variable:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

### [count = 0](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

### [def example3():](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) count = count + 1 # WRONG

If you run it you get:

### 11.8. Debugging

### [UnboundLocalError: local variable](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [count](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) referenced before assignment

Python assumes that [count](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [is local, and under that assumption you are reading it before](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [writing it. The solution, again, is to declare](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) count global.

### [def example3():](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) global count count += 1

If a global variable refers to a mutable value, you can modify the value without declaring [the variable:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

### [known = {0:0, 1:1}](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

### [def example4():](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [known[2] = 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

[So you can add, remove and replace elements of a global list or dictionary, but if you want](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) to reassign the variable, you have to declare it:

### [def example5():](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [global known](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [known = dict()](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py)

[Global variables can be useful, but if you have a lot of them, and you modify them fre-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [quently, they can make programs hard to debug.](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

## [11.8](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Debugging](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[As you work with bigger datasets it can become unwieldy to debug by printing and check-](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [ing the output by hand. Here are some suggestions for debugging large datasets:](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[Scale down the input:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [If possible, reduce the size of the dataset. For example if the pro-](http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=k4RRi_ntQc8) [gram reads a text file, start with just the first 10 lines, or with the smallest example](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) you can find. You can either edit the files themselves, or (better) modify the program [so it reads only the first](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [n](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [lines.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

If there is an error, you can reduce n to the smallest value that manifests the error, and then increase it gradually as you find and correct errors.

Check summaries and types: [Instead of printing and checking the entire dataset, consider](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [printing summaries of the data: for example, the number of items in a dictionary or](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [the total of a list of numbers.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[A common cause of runtime errors is a value that is not the right type. For debugging](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [this kind of error, it is often enough to print the type of a value.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[Write self-checks:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [Sometimes you can write code to check for errors automatically.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) For [example, if you are computing the average of a list of numbers, you could check that](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [the result is not greater than the largest element in the list or less than the smallest.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [This is called a “sanity check” because it detects results that are “insane”.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[Another kind of check compares the results of two different computations to see if](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) they are consistent. This is called a “consistency check”.

Chapter 11. Dictionaries

[Format the output:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Formatting debugging output can make it easier to spot an error. We](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [saw an example in Section 6.9. Another tool you might find useful is the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [pprint](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [mod-](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [ule, which provides a](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [pprint](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) function that displays built-in types in a more human- [readable format (](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [pprint](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) stands for “pretty print”).

[Again, time you spend building scaffolding can reduce the time you spend debugging.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

## 11.9 [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

mapping: [A relationship in which each element of one set corresponds to an element of](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [another set.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[dictionary:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) A mapping from keys to their corresponding values.

key-value pair: [The representation of the mapping from a key to a value.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

item: [In a dictionary, another name for a key-value pair.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

key: An object that appears in a dictionary as the first part of a key-value pair.

[value:](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [An object that appears in a dictionary as the second part of a key-value pair. This is](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) more specific than our previous use of the word “value”.

implementation: A way of performing a computation.

[hashtable:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) The algorithm used to implement Python dictionaries.

hash function: A function used by a hashtable to compute the location for a key.

hashable: [A type that has a hash function. Immutable types like integers, floats and strings](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) are hashable; mutable types like lists and dictionaries are not.

lookup: [A dictionary operation that takes a key and finds the corresponding value.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

reverse lookup: A dictionary operation that takes a value and finds one or more keys that [map to it.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

raise statement: A statement that (deliberately) raises an exception.

singleton: [A list (or other sequence) with a single element.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

call graph: [A diagram that shows every frame created during the execution of a program,](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [with an arrow from each caller to each callee.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

memo: [A computed value stored to avoid unnecessary future computation.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

global variable: [A variable defined outside a function. Global variables can be accessed](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [from any function.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

global statement: [A statement that declares a variable name global.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

flag: [A boolean variable used to indicate whether a condition is true.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

declaration: A statement like global that tells the interpreter something about a variable.

It doesn’t matter what the values are. [Exercise 11.5.](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [Two words are “rotate pairs” if you can rotate one of them and get the other (see](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [rotate_word](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [in Exercise 8.5).](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py)

[Write a program that reads a wordlist and finds all the rotate pairs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [Solution:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [https: //](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) thinkpython. com/ code/ rotate_ pairs. py . [Exercise 11.6.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Here’s another Puzzler from](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Car Talk](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [(](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [http: // www. cartalk. com/ content/](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [puzzlers](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) ):

This was sent in by a fellow named Dan O’Leary. He came upon a common one-syllable, [five-letter word recently that has the following unique property. When you remove the](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [first letter, the remaining letters form a homophone of the original word, that is a word](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [that sounds exactly the same. Replace the first letter, that is, put it back and remove](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [the second letter and the result is yet another homophone of the original word. And the](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [question is, what’s the word?](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[Now I’m going to give you an example that doesn’t work. Let’s look at the five-letter](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) word, ‘wrack.’ W-R-A-C-K, you know like to ‘wrack with pain.’ If I remove the first letter, I am left with a four-letter word, ’R-A-C-K.’ As in, ‘Holy cow, did you see the rack on that buck! It must have been a nine-pointer!’ It’s a perfect homophone. If you [put the ‘w’ back, and remove the ‘r,’ instead, you’re left with the word, ‘wack,’ which is](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) a real word, it’s just not a homophone of the other two words.

[But there is, however, at least one word that Dan and we know of, which will yield two](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [homophones if you remove either of the first two letters to make two, new four-letter](http://gutenberg.org/) [words. The question is, what’s the word?](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[You can use the dictionary from Exercise 11.1 to check whether a string is in the word list.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

[To](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [check](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [whether](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [two](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [words](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [are](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [homophones,](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [you](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [can](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [use](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [the](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [CMU](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [Pronouncing](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [Dictionary.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) You [can](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [download](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [it](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [from](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [http: // www. speech. cs. cmu. edu/ cgi-bin/](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) cmudict [or](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [from](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [https: // thinkpython. com/ code/ c06d](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) and you can also down- [load](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [https: // thinkpython. com/ code/ pronounce. py](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [, which provides a function named](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newline) [read_dictionary](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [that reads the pronouncing dictionary and returns a Python dictionary that](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) maps from each word to a string that describes its primary pronunciation.

## [Chapter 12](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

# [Tuples](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[This chapter presents one more built-in type, the tuple, and then shows how lists, dictionar-](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [ies, and tuples work together. I also present a useful feature for variable-length argument](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) lists, the gather and scatter operators.

[One note: there is no consensus on how to pronounce “tuple”.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [Some people say “tuh-](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [ple”, which rhymes with “supple”. But in the context of programming, most people say](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) [“too-ple”, which rhymes with “quadruple”.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

## [12.1](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Tuples are immutable](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[A tuple is a sequence of values.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [The values can be any type, and they are indexed by](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [integers, so in that respect tuples are a lot like lists. The important difference is that tuples](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [are immutable.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

Syntactically, a tuple is a comma-separated list of values:

```
>>> t = ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' , ' d ' , ' e '
```

[Although it is not necessary, it is common to enclose tuples in parentheses:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

```
>>> t = ( ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' , ' d ' , ' e ' )
```

To create a tuple with a single element, you have to include a final comma:

| >>> t1 = | '   | a     | '   | ,   |
| -------- | --- | ----- | --- | --- |
| <class   | '   | tuple | '   | >   |

[A value in parentheses is not a tuple:](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

| >>> t2 = ( | '   | a   | '   | )   |
| ---------- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| <class     | '   | str | '   | >   |

[Another way to create a tuple is the built-in function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [tuple](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [. With no argument, it creates](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [an empty tuple:](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

### [>>> t = tuple()](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [>>> t](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) ()

Chapter 12. Tuples

[If the argument is a sequence (string, list or tuple), the result is a tuple with the elements of](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) the sequence:

```
>>> t = tuple( ' lupins ' )
>>> t
( ' l ' , ' u ' , ' p ' , ' i ' , ' n ' , ' s ' )
```

Because tuple is the name of a built-in function, you should avoid using it as a variable [name.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[Most list operators also work on tuples. The bracket operator indexes an element:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

```
>>> t = ( ' a ' , ' b ' , ' c ' , ' d ' , ' e ' )
>>> t[0]
' a '
```

[And the slice operator selects a range of elements.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

```
>>> t[1:3]
( ' b ' , ' c ' )
```

[But if you try to modify one of the elements of the tuple, you get an error:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

### >>> t[0] = ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [A](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [TypeError: object doesn](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [t support item assignment](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[Because tuples are immutable, you can’t modify the elements.](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [But you can replace one](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [tuple with another:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

```
>>> t = ( ' A ' ,) + t[1:]
>>> t
( ' A ' , ' b ' , ' c ' , ' d ' , ' e ' )
```

[This statement makes a new tuple and then makes](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [t](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [refer to it.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

The relational operators work with tuples and other sequences; Python starts by comparing [the first element from each sequence. If they are equal, it goes on to the next elements, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) so on, until it finds elements that differ. Subsequent elements are not considered (even if [they are really big).](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py)

### [>>> (0, 1, 2) < (0, 3, 4)](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) True >>> (0, 1, 2000000) < (0, 3, 4) True

## 12.2 Tuple assignment

[It is often useful to swap the values of two variables. With conventional assignments, you](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [have to use a temporary variable. For example, to swap](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [b](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

### >>> temp = a [>>> a = b](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) >>> b = temp

This solution is cumbersome; [tuple assignment](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [is more elegant:](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

### >>> a, b = b, a

### 12.3. Tuples as return values

[The left side is a tuple of variables; the right side is a tuple of expressions.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) Each value [is assigned to its respective variable. All the expressions on the right side are evaluated](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [before any of the assignments.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[The number of variables on the left and the number of values on the right have to be the](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) same:

### [>>> a, b = 1, 2, 3](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [ValueError: too many values to unpack](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[More generally, the right side can be any kind of sequence (string, list or tuple). For exam-](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [ple, to split an email address into a user name and a domain, you could write:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

```
>>> addr = ' monty@python.org '
>>> uname, domain = addr.split( ' @ ' )
```

[The return value from](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [split](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) is a list with two elements; the first element is assigned to uname , the second to domain .

### [>>> uname](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [monty](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [>>> domain](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [python.org](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

## [12.3](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Tuples as return values](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

Strictly speaking, a function can only return one value, but if the value is a tuple, the effect [is the same as returning multiple values. For example, if you want to divide two integers](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [and compute the quotient and remainder, it is inefficient to compute](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [x//y](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [and then](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [x%y](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [. It](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [is better to compute them both at the same time.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[The built-in function](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) divmod [takes two arguments and returns a tuple of two values, the](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) quotient and remainder. You can store the result as a tuple:

### [>>> t = divmod(7, 3)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) >>> t (2, 1)

[Or use tuple assignment to store the elements separately:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

### [>>> quot, rem = divmod(7, 3)](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [>>> quot](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### [>>> rem](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

[Here is an example of a function that returns a tuple:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

### [def min_max(t):](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [return min(t), max(t)](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[max](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [and](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [min](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [are built-in functions that find the largest and smallest elements of a sequence.](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) min_max computes both and returns a tuple of two values.

Chapter 12. Tuples

## [12.4](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Variable-length argument tuples](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Functions can take a variable number of arguments. A parameter name that begins with](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [*](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [gathers](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [arguments into a tuple. For example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) printall takes any number of arguments and prints them:

### def printall(*args): [print(args)](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[The gather parameter can have any name you like, but](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [args](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [is conventional. Here’s how](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [the function works:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

### >>> printall(1, 2.0, ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) (1, 2.0, ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[The complement of gather is](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) scatter . If you have a sequence of values and you want to pass it to a function as multiple arguments, you can use the * operator. For example, divmod [takes exactly two arguments; it doesn’t work with a tuple:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

### >>> t = (7, 3) >>> divmod(t) TypeError: divmod expected 2 arguments, got 1

[But if you scatter the tuple, it works:](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

### >>> divmod(*t) [(2, 1)](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py)

[Many of the built-in functions use variable-length argument tuples. For example,](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [max](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [min](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) can take any number of arguments:

### >>> max(1, 2, 3)

But [sum](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_rankings) [does not.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### [>>> sum(1, 2, 3)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [TypeError: sum expected at most 2 arguments, got 3](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[As an exercise, write a function called](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [sum_all](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [that takes any number of arguments and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) returns their sum.

## 12.5 [Lists and tuples](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

zip [is a built-in function that takes two or more sequences and interleaves them. The name](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [of the function refers to a zipper, which interleaves two rows of teeth.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[This example zips a string and a list:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

### >>> s = ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [abc](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [>>> t = [0, 1, 2]](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) >>> zip(s, t) [<zip object at 0x7f7d0a9e7c48>](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

The result is a [zip object](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [that knows how to iterate through the pairs. The most common](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) use of zip is in a for loop:

### 12.5. Lists and tuples

[>>> for pair in zip(s, t):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ... print(pair) [...](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [(](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [, 0)](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [(](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [b](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [, 1)](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ( ' c ' , 2)

A zip object is a kind of iterator , which is any object that iterates through a sequence. [Iterators are similar to lists in some ways, but unlike lists, you can’t use an index to select](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [an element from an iterator.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[If you want to use list operators and methods, you can use a zip object to make a list:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

```
>>> list(zip(s, t))
[( ' a ' , 0), ( ' b ' , 1), ( ' c ' , 2)]
```

The result is a list of tuples; in this example, each tuple contains a character from the string [and the corresponding element from the list.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[If the sequences are not the same length, the result has the length of the shorter one.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

```
>>> list(zip( ' Anne ' , ' Elk ' ))
[( ' A ' , ' E ' ), ( ' n ' , ' l ' ), ( ' n ' , ' k ' )]
```

[You can use tuple assignment in a](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [loop to traverse a list of tuples:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal)

```
t = [( ' a ' , 0), ( ' b ' , 1), ( ' c ' , 2)]
for letter, number in t:
print(number, letter)
```

[Each time through the loop, Python selects the next tuple in the list and assigns the ele-](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [ments to](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [letter](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [and](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [number](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) . The output of this loop is:

### [0 a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) 1 b [2 c](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[If you combine](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [zip](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) and tuple assignment, you get a useful idiom for traversing two [(or more) sequences at the same time. For example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [has_match](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [takes two sequences,](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [t1](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [t2](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [, and returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [True](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) if there is an index [i](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [such that](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [t1[i] == t2[i]](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt)

### [def has_match(t1, t2):](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) for x, y in zip(t1, t2): if x == y: return True [return False](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

[If you need to traverse the elements of a sequence and their indices, you can use the built-in](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [function](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [enumerate](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### for index, element in enumerate( ['](http://gutenberg.org/) [abc](http://gutenberg.org/) ['](http://gutenberg.org/) [):](http://gutenberg.org/) [print(index, element)](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

[The result from](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [enumerate](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [is an enumerate object, which iterates a sequence of pairs; each](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [pair contains an index (starting from 0) and an element from the given sequence. In this](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [example, the output is](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

### [0 a](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [1 b](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) 2 c

Again.

Chapter 12. Tuples

## [12.6](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Dictionaries and tuples](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Dictionaries have a method called](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [items](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [that returns a sequence of tuples, where each](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [tuple is a key-value pair.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorting_algorithm)

```
>>> d = { ' a ' :0, ' b ' :1, ' c ' :2}
>>> t = d.items()
>>> t
dict_items([( ' c ' , 2), ( ' a ' , 0), ( ' b ' , 1)])
```

The result is a [dict_items](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [object, which is an iterator that iterates the key-value pairs. You](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) can use it in a [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [loop like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

### [>>> for key, value in d.items():](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) ... [print(key, value)](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ... [c 2](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) a 0 b 1

[As you should expect from a dictionary, the items are in no particular order.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

Going in the other direction, you can use a list of tuples to initialize a new dictionary:

```
>>> t = [( ' a ' , 0), ( ' c ' , 2), ( ' b ' , 1)]
>>> d = dict(t)
>>> d
{ ' a ' : 0, ' c ' : 2, ' b ' : 1}
```

[Combining](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [dict](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [with](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [zip](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [yields a concise way to create a dictionary:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

```
>>> d = dict(zip( ' abc ' , range(3)))
>>> d
{ ' a ' : 0, ' c ' : 2, ' b ' : 1}
```

[The dictionary method](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [update](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [also takes a list of tuples and adds them, as key-value pairs,](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [to an existing dictionary.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

It is common to use tuples as keys in dictionaries (primarily because you can’t use lists). For example, a telephone directory might map from last-name, first-name pairs to telephone [numbers. Assuming that we have defined](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [last](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [,](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [first](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [and](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) number [, we could write:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

### [directory[last, first] = number](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

The expression in brackets is a tuple. We could use tuple assignment to traverse this dictionary.

### for last, first in directory: [print(first, last, directory[last,first])](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

[This loop traverses the keys in](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [directory](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [, which are tuples. It assigns the elements of each](http://gutenberg.org/) tuple to last and first [, then prints the name and corresponding telephone number.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[There are two ways to represent tuples in a state diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [The more detailed version](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [shows the indices and elements just as they appear in a list.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [For example, the tuple](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) ( ' Cleese ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [John](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [would appear as in Figure 12.1.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[But in a larger diagram you might want to leave out the details. For example, a diagram of](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [the telephone directory might appear as in Figure 12.2.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[Here the tuples are shown using Python syntax as a graphical shorthand. The telephone](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) number in the diagram is the complaints line for the BBC, so please don’t call it.

### 12.7. Sequences of sequences

### [tuple](https://thinkpython.com/code/letters.py)

### [’Cleese’](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

### ’John’

[Figure 12.1: State diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code)

### [dict](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

### (’Cleese’, ’John’) [’08700 100 222’](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py)

### [(’Chapman’, ’Graham’)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [’08700 100 222’](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting)

### [(’Idle’, ’Eric’)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [’08700 100 222’](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

### [(’Gilliam’, ’Terry’)](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [’08700 100 222’](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

### (’Jones’, ’Terry’) [’08700 100 222’](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### (’Palin’, ’Michael’) ’08700 100 222’

[Figure 12.2: State diagram.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

## [12.7](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [Sequences of sequences](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal)

[I have focused on lists of tuples, but almost all of the examples in this chapter also work](https://thinkpython.com/code/GoodKangaroo.py) [with lists of lists, tuples of tuples, and tuples of lists. To avoid enumerating the possible](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [combinations, it is sometimes easier to talk about sequences of sequences.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

In many contexts, the different kinds of sequences (strings, lists and tuples) can be used interchangeably. So how should you choose one over the others?

[To start with the obvious, strings are more limited than other sequences because the ele-](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [ments have to be characters. They are also immutable. If you need the ability to change the](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [characters in a string (as opposed to creating a new string), you might want to use a list of](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [characters instead.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[Lists are more common than tuples, mostly because they are mutable. But there are a few](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) cases where you might prefer tuples:

1. [1. In some contexts, like a](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [return](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [statement, it is syntactically simpler to create a tuple](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) than a list.

2. [2. If you want to use a sequence as a dictionary key, you have to use an immutable type](http://gutenberg.org/) [like a tuple or string.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

3. [3. If you are passing a sequence as an argument to a function, using tuples reduces the](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [potential for unexpected behavior due to aliasing.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[Because tuples are immutable, they don’t provide methods like](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) sort and reverse , which [modify existing lists. But Python provides the built-in function](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [sorted](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [, which takes any](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [sequence and returns a new list with the same elements in sorted order, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) [reversed](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) which takes a sequence and returns an iterator that traverses the list in reverse order.

Chapter 12. Tuples

## [12.8](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Debugging](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Lists, dictionaries and tuples are examples of](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [data structures](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ; in this chapter we are starting to see compound data structures, like lists of tuples, or dictionaries that contain tuples as [keys and lists as values. Compound data structures are useful, but they are prone to what](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) I call shape errors [; that is, errors caused when a data structure has the wrong type, size, or](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) structure. For example, if you are expecting a list with one integer and I give you a plain [old integer (not in a list), it won’t work.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[To help debug these kinds of errors, I have written a module called](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [structshape](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [that](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [provides a function, also called](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [structshape](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [, that takes any kind of data structure as](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [an argument and returns a string that summarizes its shape. You can download it from](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[Here’s the result for a simple list:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

### [>>> from structshape import structshape](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [>>> t = [1, 2, 3]](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) >>> structshape(t) ' list of 3 int ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

[A fancier program might write “list of 3 int](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [s](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [”, but it was easier not to deal with plurals.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [Here’s a list of lists:](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

### [>>> t2 = [[1,2], [3,4], [5,6]]](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [>>> structshape(t2)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [list of 3 list of 2 int](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) '

If the elements of the list are not the same type, structshape groups them, in order, by type:

```
>>> t3 = [1, 2, 3, 4.0, ' ' , ' ' , [7], [8], 9]
>>> structshape(t3)
' list of (3 int, float, 2 str, 2 list of int, int) '
```

[Here’s a list of tuples:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

```
>>> s = ' abc '
>>> lt = list(zip(t, s))
>>> structshape(lt)
' list of 3 tuple of (int, str) '
```

And here’s a dictionary with 3 items that map integers to strings.

### >>> d = dict(lt) [>>> structshape(d)](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) ' [dict of 3 int->str](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[If you are having trouble keeping track of your data structures,](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [structshape](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [can help.](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py)

## 12.9 [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

tuple: An immutable sequence of elements.

[tuple assignment:](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [An assignment with a sequence on the right side and a tuple of vari-](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [ables on the left. The right side is evaluated and then its elements are assigned to the](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) variables on the left.

### 12.10. Exercises

[gather:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [An operation that collects multiple arguments into a tuple.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

scatter: [An operation that makes a sequence behave like multiple arguments.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[zip object:](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) The result of calling a built-in function zip ; an object that iterates through a [sequence of tuples.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

iterator: An object that can iterate through a sequence, but which does not provide list [operators and methods.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[data structure:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [A collection of related values, often organized in lists, dictionaries, tuples,](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [etc.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[shape error:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [An error caused because a value has the wrong shape; that is, the wrong type](http://www.thinkpython.com/) or size.

[Now, letters can be removed from either end, or the middle, but you can’t rearrange any](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [of the letters. Every time you drop a letter, you wind up with another English word. If](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) you do that, you’re eventually going to wind up with one letter and that too is going [to be an English word—one that’s found in the dictionary.
