## [13.2](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [Random numbers](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[Given the same inputs, most computer programs generate the same outputs every time,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [so they are said to be](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [deterministic](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) . Determinism is usually a good thing, since we expect [the same calculation to yield the same result. For some applications, though, we want the](http://www.thinkpython.com/) computer to be unpredictable. Games are an obvious example, but there are more.

[Making a program truly nondeterministic turns out to be difficult, but there are ways to](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) make it at least seem nondeterministic. One of them is to use algorithms that generate pseudorandom [numbers. Pseudorandom numbers are not truly random because they are](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [generated by a deterministic computation, but just by looking at the numbers it is all but](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) impossible to distinguish them from random.

[The](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [random](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [module provides functions that generate pseudorandom numbers (which I](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [will simply call “random” from here on).](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

| The function       | random | returns a random float between 0.0 and 1.0 (including 0.0 but not 1.0). |
| ------------------ | ------ | ----------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| Each time you call | random | , you get the next number in a long series. To see a sample, run        |

### import random

### [for i in range(10):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [x = random.random()](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [print(x)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[The function](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [randint](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) takes parameters low and high and returns an integer between low [and](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [high](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [(including both).](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

### [>>> random.randint(5, 10)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

### >>> random.randint(5, 10)

[To choose an element from a sequence at random, you can use](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [choice](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

### [>>> t = [1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) >>> random.choice(t)

### [>>> random.choice(t)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

[The](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [random](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [module also provides functions to generate random values from continuous](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [distributions including Gaussian, exponential, gamma, and a few more.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) Exercise 13.5. [Write a function named](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [choose_from_hist](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [that takes a histogram as defined in](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [Section 11.2 and returns a random value from the histogram, chosen with probability in proportion](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) to frequency. For example, for this histogram:

### 13.3. Word histogram

```
>>> t = [ ' a ' , ' a ' , ' b ' ]
>>> hist = histogram(t)
>>> hist
{ ' a ' : 2, ' b ' : 1}
```

[your function should return](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [with probability](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [2/3](https://thinkpython.com/code) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code) [b](https://thinkpython.com/code) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code) with probability 1/3 .

## [13.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [Word histogram](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[You should attempt the previous exercises before you go on.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) You can download my [solution from](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book1.py](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [You will also need](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [.](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

Here is a program that reads a file and builds a histogram of the words in the file:

### [import string](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

### [def process_file(filename):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) hist = dict() [fp = open(filename)](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [for line in fp:](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [process_line(line, hist)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [return hist](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

### [def process_line(line, hist):](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) line = line.replace( ' - ' , ' ' )

[for word in line.split():](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [word = word.strip(string.punctuation + string.whitespace)](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) word = word.lower() [hist[word] = hist.get(word, 0) + 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

### hist = process_file( ' emma.txt ' )

[This program reads](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [emma.txt](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [, which contains the text of](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [Emma](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [by Jane Austen.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

process_file loops through the lines of the file, passing them one at a time to process_line . The histogram hist is being used as an accumulator.

process_line uses the string method replace [to replace hyphens with spaces before using](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [split](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [to break the line into a list of strings. It traverses the list of words and uses](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [strip](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [lower](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [to remove punctuation and convert to lower case. (It is a shorthand to say that](http://gutenberg.org/) [strings are “converted”; remember that strings are immutable, so methods like](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [strip](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [lower](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [return new strings.)](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

[Finally,](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) [process_line](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [updates the histogram by creating a new item or incrementing an](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [existing one.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[To count the total number of words in the file, we can add up the frequencies in the his-](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [togram:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

### [def total_words(hist):](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) return sum(hist.values())

```
Chapter 13. Case study: data structure selection
```

[The number of different words is just the number of items in the dictionary:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### def different_words(hist): [return len(hist)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Here is some code to print the results:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

| print( | '   | Total number of words:     | '   | , total_words(hist))     |
| ------ | --- | -------------------------- | --- | ------------------------ |
| print( | '   | Number of different words: | '   | , different_words(hist)) |

[And the results:](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

### [Total number of words: 161080](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [Number of different words: 7214](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

## 13.4 [Most common words](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[To find the most common words, we can make a list of tuples, where each tuple contains a](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [word and its frequency, and sort it.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[The following function takes a histogram and returns a list of word-frequency tuples:](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

### [def most_common(hist):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) t = [] [for key, value in hist.items():](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [t.append((value, key))](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

### [t.sort(reverse=True)](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) return t

[In each tuple, the frequency appears first, so the resulting list is sorted by frequency. Here](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) is a loop that prints the ten most common words:

[t = most_common(hist)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) print( ' [The most common words are:](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) ' ) [for freq, word in t[:10]:](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [print(word, freq, sep=](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [\t](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [)](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

I use the keyword argument sep to tell print to use a tab character as a “separator”, rather than a space, so the second column is lined up. Here are the results from Emma :

The most common words are: to the and of i a it [her](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) was she

[This code can be simplified using the](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [key](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [parameter of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) [sort](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) [function. If you are curi-](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) ous, you can read about it at [https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) .

### 13.5. Optional parameters

## [13.5](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Optional parameters](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[We have seen built-in functions and methods that take optional arguments. It is possible](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) to write programmer-defined functions with optional arguments, too. For example, here is [a function that prints the most common words in a histogram](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[def print_most_common(hist, num=10):](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [t = most_common(hist)](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) print( ' The most common words are: ' [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [for freq, word in t[:num]:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [print(word, freq, sep=](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [\t](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

[The first parameter is required; the second is optional. The](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [default value](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book1.py) [of](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [num](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [is 10.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

If you only provide one argument:

### [print_most_common(hist)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5)

num gets the default value. If you provide two arguments:

### [print_most_common(hist, 20)](https://thinkpython.com/code/zipf.py)

num gets the value of the argument instead. In other words, the optional argument [over-](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [rides](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [the default value.](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

[If a function has both required and optional parameters, all the required parameters have](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [to come first, followed by the optional ones.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

## [13.6](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Dictionary subtraction](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[Finding the words from the book that are not in the word list from](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [words.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [is a problem](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [you might recognize as set subtraction; that is, we want to find all the words from one set](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [(the words in the book) that are not in the other (the words in the list).](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[subtract](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [takes dictionaries](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) d1 and d2 [and returns a new dictionary that contains all the](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [keys from](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) d1 that are not in d2 . Since we don’t really care about the values, we set them all [to None.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

### def subtract(d1, d2): res = dict() for key in d1: [if key not in d2:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [res[key] = None](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [return res](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[To find the words in the book that are not in](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [words.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [, we can use](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [process_file](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [to build](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [a histogram for](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [words.txt](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [, and then subtract:](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

### [words = process_file(](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [words.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [diff = subtract(hist, words)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

### [print("Words in the book that aren](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [t in the word list:")](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [for word in diff:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) print(word, end= [' '](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

But a few are common words that should really be in the list!](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) Exercise 13.6. [Python provides a data structure called](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [set](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [that provides many common set](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [operations.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [You can read about them in Section 19.5, or read the documentation at](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) http: // docs. python. org/ 3/ library/ stdtypes. html# types-set .

## [13.7](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Random words](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5)

To choose a random word from the histogram, the simplest algorithm is to build a list with [multiple copies of each word, according to the observed frequency, and then choose from](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) the list:

### [def random_word(h):](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [t = []](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [for word, freq in h.items():](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [t.extend([word] * freq)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

### [return random.choice(t)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

The expression [[word] * freq](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [creates a list with](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) freq copies of the string word . The extend method is similar to append except that the argument is a sequence.

[This algorithm works, but it is not very efficient; each time you choose a random word, it](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) rebuilds the list, which is as big as the original book. An obvious improvement is to build [the list once and then make multiple selections, but the list is still big.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

An alternative is:

1. [1. Use](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [keys](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [to get a list of the words in the book.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

2. Build a list that contains the cumulative sum of the word frequencies (see Exercise 10.2). The last item in this list is the total number of words in the book, n .

3. [3. Choose a random number from 1 to](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [n](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) . Use a bisection search (See Exercise 10.10) to [find the index where the random number would be inserted in the cumulative sum.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

4. [4. Use the index to find the corresponding word in the word list.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

Exercise 13.7. [Write a program that uses this algorithm to choose a random word from the book.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) Solution: [https: // thinkpython. com/ code/ analyze_ book3. py](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

## 13.8 [Markov analysis](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[If you choose words from the book at random, you can get a sense of the vocabulary, but](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) you probably won’t get a sentence:

### 13.8. Markov analysis

### [this the small regard harriet which knightley](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ' s it most things

[A series of random words seldom makes sense because there is no relationship between](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [successive words. For example, in a real sentence you would expect an article like “the” to](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) be followed by an adjective or a noun, and probably not a verb or adverb.

[One way to measure these kinds of relationships is Markov analysis, which characterizes,](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) for a given sequence of words, the probability of the words that might come next. For [example, the song](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [Eric, the Half a Bee](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [begins:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set)

[Half a bee, philosophically,](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [Must, ipso facto, half not be.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [But half the bee has got to be](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [Vis a vis, its entity. D’you see?](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

But can a bee be said to be [Or not to be an entire bee](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [When half the bee is not a bee](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [Due to some ancient injury?](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[In this text, the phrase “half the” is always followed by the word “bee”, but the phrase “the](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [bee” might be followed by either “has” or “is”.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[The result of Markov analysis is a mapping from each prefix (like “half the” and “the bee”)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) to all possible suffixes (like “has” and “is”).

Given this mapping, you can generate a random text by starting with any prefix and choosing at random from the possible suffixes. Next, you can combine the end of the prefix and the new suffix to form the next prefix, and repeat.

[For example, if you start with the prefix “Half a”, then the next word has to be “bee”,](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [because the prefix only appears once in the text.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [The next prefix is “a bee”, so the next](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [suffix might be “philosophically”, “be” or “due”.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

In this example the length of the prefix is always two, but you can do Markov analysis with [any prefix length.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [Exercise 13.8.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) Markov analysis:

1. Write a program to read a text from a file and perform Markov analysis. The result should be [a dictionary that maps from prefixes to a collection of possible suffixes. The collection might](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) be a list, tuple, or dictionary; it is up to you to make an appropriate choice. You can test your [program with prefix length two, but you should write the program in a way that makes it easy](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [to try other lengths.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

2. [2. Add a function to the previous program to generate random text based on the Markov analysis.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [Here is an example from](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [Emma](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [with prefix length 2:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[He was very clever, be it sweetness or be angry, ashamed or only amused, at such](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [a stroke. She had never thought of Hannah till you were never meant for me?" "I](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [cannot make speeches, Emma:" he soon cut it all himself.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

[For this example, I left the punctuation attached to the words. The result is almost syntacti-](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [cally correct, but not quite. Semantically, it almost makes sense, but not quite.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newline)

What happens if you increase the prefix length? Does the random text make more sense?

```
Chapter 13. Case study: data structure selection
```

3. [3. Once your program is working, you might want to try a mash-up: if you combine text from](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [two or more books, the random text you generate will blend the vocabulary and phrases from](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [the sources in interesting ways.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Credit: This case study is based on an example from Kernighan and Pike,](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) The Practice of Programming , Addison-Wesley, 1999.

[You should attempt this exercise before you go on;](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) then you can download my so- [lution from](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [You will also need](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [https://](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

## 13.9 [Data structures](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

Using Markov analysis to generate random text is fun, but there is also a point to this [exercise: data structure selection. In your solution to the previous exercises, you had to](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [choose:](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

- [• How to represent the prefixes.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

- [• How to represent the collection of possible suffixes.](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

- [• How to represent the mapping from each prefix to the collection of possible suffixes.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

The last one is easy: a dictionary is the obvious choice for a mapping from keys to corre- [sponding values.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py)

For the prefixes, the most obvious options are string, list of strings, or tuple of strings.

For the suffixes, one option is a list; another is a histogram (dictionary).

[How should you choose? The first step is to think about the operations you will need to](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [implement for each data structure. For the prefixes, we need to be able to remove words](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [from the beginning and add to the end. For example, if the current prefix is “Half a”, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) the next word is “bee”, you need to be able to form the next prefix, “a bee”.

[Your first choice might be a list, since it is easy to add and remove elements, but we also](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) need to be able to use the prefixes as keys in a dictionary, so that rules out lists. With tuples, you can’t append or remove, but you can use the addition operator to form a new tuple:

### def shift(prefix, word): [return prefix[1:] + (word,)](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

shift [takes a tuple of words,](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [prefix](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [, and a string,](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [word](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) , and forms a new tuple that has all the words in [prefix](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [except the first, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [word](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) added to the end.

[For the collection of suffixes, the operations we need to perform include adding a new](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [suffix (or increasing the frequency of an existing one), and choosing a random suffix.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[Adding a new suffix is equally easy for the list implementation or the histogram. Choosing](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [a random element from a list is easy; choosing from a histogram is harder to do efficiently](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) (see Exercise 13.7).

[So far we have been talking mostly about ease of implementation, but there are other fac-](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [tors to consider in choosing data structures. One is run time. Sometimes there is a theoreti-](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) cal reason to expect one data structure to be faster than other; for example, I mentioned that

### 13.10. Debugging

[the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [in](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [operator is faster for dictionaries than for lists, at least when the number of elements](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) is large.

But often you don’t know ahead of time which implementation will be faster. One option is [to implement both of them and see which is better. This approach is called](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) benchmarking . [A practical alternative is to choose the data structure that is easiest to implement, and then](https://thinkpython.com/code) [see if it is fast enough for the intended application. If so, there is no need to go on. If not,](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [there are tools, like the](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [profile](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) module, that can identify the places in a program that take [the most time.](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[The other factor to consider is storage space. For example, using a histogram for the col-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [lection of suffixes might take less space because you only have to store each word once, no](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book1.py) [matter how many times it appears in the text. In some cases, saving space can also make](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [your program run faster, and in the extreme, your program might not run at all if you run](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) out of memory. But for many applications, space is a secondary consideration after run [time.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[One final thought: in this discussion, I have implied that we should use one data structure](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [for both analysis and generation. But since these are separate phases, it would also be pos-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) sible to use one structure for analysis and then convert to another structure for generation. [This would be a net win if the time saved during generation exceeded the time spent in](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [conversion.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

## [13.10](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) Debugging

When you are debugging a program, and especially if you are working on a hard bug, [there are five things to try:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[Reading:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [Examine your code, read it back to yourself, and check that it says what you](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [meant to say.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[Running:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [Experiment by making changes and running different versions.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [Often if you](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) display the right thing at the right place in the program, the problem becomes obvious, but sometimes you have to build scaffolding.

Ruminating: [Take some time to think! What kind of error is it: syntax, runtime, or seman-](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) tic? What information can you get from the error messages, or from the output of the [program? What kind of error could cause the problem you’re seeing? What did you](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [change last, before the problem appeared?](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Rubberducking:](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [If you explain the problem to someone else, you sometimes find the](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [answer before you finish asking the question.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [Often you don’t need the other](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [person; you could just talk to a rubber duck.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) And that’s the origin of the well- [known strategy called](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [rubber duck debugging](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [I am not making this up; see](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[Retreating:](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [At some point, the best thing to do is back off, undoing recent changes, until](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [you get back to a program that works and that you understand. Then you can start](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) rebuilding.

```
Chapter 13. Case study: data structure selection
```

[Beginning programmers sometimes get stuck on one of these activities and forget the oth-](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ers. Each activity comes with its own failure mode.

[For example, reading your code might help if the problem is a typographical error, but](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [not if the problem is a conceptual misunderstanding. If you don’t understand what your](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [program does, you can read it 100 times and never see the error, because the error is in](https://thinkpython.com/code) your head.

[Running experiments can help, especially if you run small, simple tests.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [But if you run](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [experiments without thinking or reading your code, you might fall into a pattern I call](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [“random walk programming”, which is the process of making random changes until the](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [program does the right thing. Needless to say, random walk programming can take a long](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [time.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

You have to take time to think. Debugging is like an experimental science. You should have at least one hypothesis about what the problem is. If there are two or more possibilities, try [to think of a test that would eliminate one of them.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[But even the best debugging techniques will fail if there are too many errors, or if the code](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [you are trying to fix is too big and complicated. Sometimes the best option is to retreat,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) simplifying the program until you get to something that works and that you understand.

[Beginning programmers are often reluctant to retreat because they can’t stand to delete a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) line of code (even if it’s wrong). If it makes you feel better, copy your program into another [file before you start stripping it down. Then you can copy the pieces back one at a time.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[Finding a hard bug requires reading, running, ruminating, and sometimes retreating. If](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) you get stuck on one of these activities, try the others.

## 13.11 [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[deterministic:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Pertaining to a program that does the same thing each time it runs, given](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [the same inputs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

pseudorandom: Pertaining to a sequence of numbers that appears to be random, but is [generated by a deterministic program.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

default value: The value given to an optional parameter if no argument is provided.

override: [To replace a default value with an argument.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

benchmarking: [The process of choosing between data structures by implementing alter-](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [natives and testing them on a sample of the possible inputs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[rubber duck debugging:](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [Debugging by explaining your problem to an inanimate object](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [such as a rubber duck. Articulating the problem can help you solve it, even if the](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [rubber duck doesn’t know Python.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

## 13.12 [Exercises](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

Exercise 13.9. [The “rank” of a word is its position in a list of words sorted by frequency: the most](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) common word has rank 1, the second most common has rank 2, etc.

### 13.12. Exercises

[Zipf’s law describes a relationship between the ranks and frequencies of words in natural languages](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) ( [http: // en. wikipedia. org/ wiki/ Zipf’s_ law](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [). Specifically, it predicts that the frequency,](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [f , of the word with rank r is:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[−](https://thinkpython.com/code) [s](https://thinkpython.com/code)

[f](https://thinkpython.com/code) [=](https://thinkpython.com/code) [cr](https://thinkpython.com/code)

where s and c are parameters that depend on the language and the text. If you take the logarithm of [both sides of this equation, you get:](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[log](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [f](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [=](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [log](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [c](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [−](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [s](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [log](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [r](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[So if you plot log f versus log r, you should get a straight line with slope](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [−](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) s and intercept log c.

Write a program that reads a text from a file, counts word frequencies, and prints one line for each word, in descending order of frequency, with log f and log r. Use the graphing program of your [choice to plot the results and check whether they form a straight line. Can you estimate the value of](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [s?](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

[Solution:](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [https: // thinkpython. com/ code/ zipf. py](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [. To run my solution, you need the plot-](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [ting module](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) matplotlib . If you installed Anaconda, you already have [matplotlib](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) ; otherwise you [might have to install it.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

## Chapter 14

# Files

[This chapter introduces the idea of “persistent” programs that keep data in permanent stor-](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [age, and shows how to use different kinds of permanent storage, like files and databases.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

## [14.1](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [Persistence](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[Most of the programs we have seen so far are transient in the sense that they run for a short](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) time and produce some output, but when they end, their data disappears. If you run the [program again, it starts with a clean slate.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[Other programs are](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [persistent](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [: they run for a long time (or all the time); they keep at least](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [some of their data in permanent storage (a hard drive, for example); and if they shut down](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [and restart, they pick up where they left off.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[Examples of persistent programs are operating systems, which run pretty much whenever](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) a computer is on, and web servers, which run all the time, waiting for requests to come in [on the network.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

One of the simplest ways for programs to maintain their data is by reading and writing text files. We have already seen programs that read text files; in this chapter we will see [programs that write them.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

[An alternative is to store the state of the program in a database. In this chapter I will present](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [a simple database and a module,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [pickle](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) , that makes it easy to store program data.

## [14.2](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) [Reading and writing](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

[A text file is a sequence of characters stored on a permanent medium like a hard drive,](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [flash memory, or CD-ROM. We saw how to open and read a file in Section 9.1.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

[To write a file, you have to open it with mode](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [w](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [as a second parameter:](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

```
>>> fout = open( ' output.txt ' , ' w ' )
```

Chapter 14. Files

[If the file already exists, opening it in write mode clears out the old data and starts fresh,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) so be careful! If the file doesn’t exist, a new one is created.

[open](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [returns a file object that provides methods for working with the file.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [The](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [write](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [method puts data into the file.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

### >>> line1 = "This here ' [s the wattle,\n"](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) >>> fout.write(line1)

The return value is the number of characters that were written. The file object keeps track [of where it is, so if you call](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [write](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [again, it adds the new data to the end of the file.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

### [>>> line2 = "the emblem of our land.\n"](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [>>> fout.write(line2)](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

When you are done writing, you should close the file.

### >>> fout.close()

[If you don’t close the file, it gets closed for you when the program ends.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

## [14.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [Format operator](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py)

[The argument of](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [write](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [has to be a string, so if we want to put other values in a file, we](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point2_soln.py) have to convert them to strings. The easiest way to do that is with [str](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [>>> x = 52](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) [>>> fout.write(str(x))](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

An alternative is to use the format operator , % . When applied to integers, % is the modulus [operator. But when the first operand is a string,](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [%](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [is the format operator.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[The first operand is the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) format string , which contains one or more [format sequences](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [,](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [which specify how the second operand is formatted. The result is a string.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[For example, the format sequence](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) ' %d ' [means that the second operand should be format-](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [ted as a decimal integer:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

### >>> camels = 42 >>> ' %d ' % camels ' '

The result is the string ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [, which is not to be confused with the integer value](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

[A format sequence can appear anywhere in the string, so you can embed a value in a](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) sentence:

### >>> ' I have spotted %d camels. ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [% camels](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) ' [I have spotted 42 camels.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[If there is more than one format sequence in the string, the second argument has to be a](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) [tuple. Each format sequence is matched with an element of the tuple, in order.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[The following example uses](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) ' [%d](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [to format an integer,](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [%g](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [to format a floating-point num-](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) ber, and ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [%s](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [to format a string:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

>>> ' [In %d years I have spotted %g %s.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) ['](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [% (3, 0.1,](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) ['](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) [camels](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) ['](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) [)](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) ' In 3 years I have spotted 0.1 camels. '

### 14.4. Filenames and paths

[The number of elements in the tuple has to match the number of format sequences in the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [string. Also, the types of the elements have to match the format sequences:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[>>>](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ['](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [%d %d %d](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ['](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [% (1, 2)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) TypeError: not enough arguments for format string [>>>](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [%d](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [%](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [dollars](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [TypeError: %d format: a number is required, not str](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

[In the first example, there aren’t enough elements; in the second, the element is the wrong](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [type.](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[For more information on the format operator, see](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [https://docs.python.org/3/library/](https://docs.python.org/3/library/) [stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [A more powerful alternative is](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [the string format method, which you can read about at](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [https://docs.python.org/3/](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [library/stdtypes.html#str.format](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

## [14.4](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [Filenames and paths](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[Files are organized into](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [directories](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [(also called “folders”). Every running program has a](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) “current directory”, which is the default directory for most operations. For example, when [you open a file for reading, Python looks for it in the current directory.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[The](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [os](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [module provides functions for working with files and directories (“os” stands for](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) [“operating system”).](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [os.getcwd](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [returns the name of the current directory:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py)

### [>>> import os](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) >>> cwd = os.getcwd() [>>> cwd](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [/home/dinsdale](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

[cwd](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [stands for “current working directory”. The result in this example is](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [/home/dinsdale](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) which is the home directory of a user named dinsdale .

[A string like](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ['](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [/home/dinsdale](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [that identifies a file or directory is called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [path](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py)

A simple filename, like memo.txt is also considered a path, but it is a relative path because [it relates to the current directory. If the current directory is](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [/home/dinsdale](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [, the filename](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [memo.txt](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) would refer to /home/dinsdale/memo.txt .

A path that begins with / does not depend on the current directory; it is called an absolute path [. To find the absolute path to a file, you can use](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [os.path.abspath](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

### [>>> os.path.abspath(](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [memo.txt](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [)](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) ' [/home/dinsdale/memo.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

os.path [provides other functions for working with filenames and paths.](http://gutenberg.org/) [For example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [os.path.exists](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [checks whether a file or directory exists:](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

### [>>> os.path.exists(](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [memo.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [True](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[If it exists,](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [os.path.isdir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [checks whether it’s a directory:](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

[>>> os.path.isdir(](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [memo.txt](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [False](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [>>> os.path.isdir(](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) ['](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [/home/dinsdale](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) True

Chapter 14. Files

[Similarly,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [os.path.isfile](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [checks whether it’s a file.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

os.listdir [returns a list of the files (and other directories) in the given directory:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

```
>>> os.listdir(cwd)
[ ' music ' , ' photos ' , ' memo.txt ' ]
```

[To demonstrate these functions, the following example “walks” through a directory, prints](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [the names of all the files, and calls itself recursively on all the directories.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

### [def walk(dirname):](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [for name in os.listdir(dirname):](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [path = os.path.join(dirname, name)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

### [if os.path.isfile(path):](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [print(path)](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [else:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [walk(path)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[os.path.join](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [takes a directory and a file name and joins them into a complete path.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

The os module provides a function called walk that is similar to this one but more versatile. [As an exercise, read the documentation and use it to print the names of the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [files in a given directory and its subdirectories.](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [You can download my solution from](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) .

## 14.5 [Catching exceptions](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

A lot of things can go wrong when you try to read and write files. If you try to open a file [that doesn’t exist, you get an](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [FileNotFoundError](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [:](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

```
>>> fin = open( ' bad_file ' )
FileNotFoundError: [Errno 2] No such file or directory: ' bad_file '
```

[If you don’t have permission to access a file:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

```
>>> fout = open( ' /etc/passwd ' , ' w ' )
PermissionError: [Errno 13] Permission denied: ' /etc/passwd '
```

[And if you try to open a directory for reading, you get](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

```
>>> fin = open( ' /home ' )
IsADirectoryError: [Errno 21] Is a directory: ' /home '
```

To avoid these errors, you could use functions like [os.path.exists](http://gutenberg.org/) [and](http://gutenberg.org/) [os.path.isfile](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [but it would take a lot of time and code to check all the possibilities (if “](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [Errno 21](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [” is any](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [indication, there are at least 21 things that can go wrong).](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[It is better to go ahead and try—and deal with problems if they happen—which is exactly](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [what the](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [try](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [statement does. The syntax is similar to an](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [if...else](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [statement:](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

```
try:
fin = open( ' bad_file ' )
except:
print( ' Something went wrong. ' )
```

### 14.6. Databases

[Python starts by executing the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [try](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) clause. If all goes well, it skips the except clause and [proceeds. If an exception occurs, it jumps out of the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [try](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [clause and runs the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [except](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [clause.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorting_algorithm)

[Handling an exception with a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorting_algorithm) try statement is called catching [an exception. In this exam-](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [ple, the](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [except](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) clause prints an error message that is not very helpful. In general, catching [an exception gives you a chance to fix the problem, or try again, or at least end the program](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) gracefully.

## [14.6](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [Databases](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[A](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [database](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [is a file that is organized for storing data. Many databases are organized like a](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) dictionary in the sense that they map from keys to values. The biggest difference between [a database and a dictionary is that the database is on disk (or other permanent storage), so](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) it persists after the program ends.

[The module](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [dbm](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [provides an interface for creating and updating database files.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) As an example, I’ll create a database that contains captions for image files.

[Opening a database is similar to opening other files:](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

```
>>> import dbm
>>> db = dbm.open( ' captions ' , ' c ' )
```

[The mode](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [c](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [means that the database should be created if it doesn’t already exist. The](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) result is a database object that can be used (for most operations) like a dictionary.

[When you create a new item,](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [dbm](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [updates the database file.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

### [>>> db[](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [cleese.png](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) ['](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [] =](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) ['](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [Photo of John Cleese.](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) ['](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[When you access one of the items,](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [dbm](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [reads the file:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

```
>>> db[ ' cleese.png ' ]
b ' Photo of John Cleese. '
```

[The result is a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) bytes object , which is why it begins with b . A bytes object is similar to a string in many ways. When you get farther into Python, the difference becomes important, but for now we can ignore it.

[If you make another assignment to an existing key,](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) dbm replaces the old value:

[>>> db[](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [cleese.png](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [] =](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) Photo of John Cleese doing a silly walk. ' >>> db[ ' cleese.png ' ] [b](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [Photo of John Cleese doing a silly walk.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[Some dictionary methods, like](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [keys](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [items](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [, don’t work with database objects.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [But](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [iteration with a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [for](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [loop works:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

### for key in db.keys(): [print(key, db[key])](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[As with other files, you should close the database when you are done:](http://puzzlers.org/)

### >>> db.close()

Chapter 14. Files

## [14.7](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Pickling](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[A limitation of](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [dbm](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [is that the keys and values have to be strings or bytes. If you try to use](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [any other type, you get an error.](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

The pickle [module can help. It translates almost any type of object into a string suitable](https://thinkpython.com/code) [for storage in a database, and then translates strings back into objects.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

pickle.dumps takes an object as a parameter and returns a string representation ( dumps is [short for “dump string”):](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

### [>>> import pickle](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [>>> t = [1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [>>> pickle.dumps(t)](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) b ' [\x80\x03]q\x00(K\x01K\x02K\x03e.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) '

The format isn’t obvious to human readers; it is meant to be easy for pickle to interpret. pickle.loads [(“load string”) reconstitutes the object:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

### [>>> t1 = [1, 2, 3]](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [>>> s = pickle.dumps(t1)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) >>> t2 = pickle.loads(s) >>> t2 [[1, 2, 3]](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py)

[Although the new object has the same value as the old, it is not (in general) the same object:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

### [>>> t1 == t2](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [True](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) >>> t1 is t2 False

[In other words, pickling and then unpickling has the same effect as copying the object.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

You can use [pickle](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) to store non-strings in a database. In fact, this combination is so com- [mon that it has been encapsulated in a module called](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [shelve](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

## 14.8 [Pipes](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

Most operating systems provide a command-line interface, also known as a shell . Shells [usually provide commands to navigate the file system and launch applications. For exam-](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ple, in Unix you can change directories with cd , display the contents of a directory with [ls](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [,](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [and launch a web browser by typing (for example)](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [firefox](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

Any program that you can launch from the shell can also be launched from Python using a pipe object [, which represents a running program.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[For example, the Unix command](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [ls -l](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [normally displays the contents of the current di-](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [rectory in long format. You can launch](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [ls](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [with](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [os.popen](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

### [>>> cmd =](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [ls -l](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) ['](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) >>> fp = os.popen(cmd)

popen [is deprecated now, which means we are supposed to stop using it and start using the](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [subprocess](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newline) [module. But for simple cases, I find](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [subprocess](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [more complicated than necessary. So I am going to keep using](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) popen until they take it away.

### 14.9. Writing modules

[The argument is a string that contains a shell command. The return value is an object that](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [behaves like an open file. You can read the output from the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [ls](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [process one line at a time](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [with](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [readline](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [or get the whole thing at once with](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) read :

### [>>> res = fp.read()](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

When you are done, you close the pipe like a file:

### >>> stat = fp.close() [>>> print(stat)](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [None](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[The return value is the final status of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [ls](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [process;](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) None means that it ended normally [(with no errors).](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[For example, most Unix systems provide a command called](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [md5sum](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [that reads the contents](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) of a file and computes a “checksum”. You can read about MD5 at [http://en.wikipedia.](http://en.wikipedia.) org/wiki/Md5 . This command provides an efficient way to check whether two files have [the same contents. The probability that different contents yield the same checksum is very](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [small (that is, unlikely to happen before the universe collapses).](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[You can use a pipe to run](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [md5sum](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) from Python and get the result:

[>>> filename =](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [book.tex](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [>>> cmd =](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [md5sum](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) ['](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [+ filename](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [>>> fp = os.popen(cmd)](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [>>> res = fp.read()](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [>>> stat = fp.close()](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [>>> print(res)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [1e0033f0ed0656636de0d75144ba32e0](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) book.tex >>> print(stat) [None](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

## [14.9](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [Writing modules](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[Any file that contains Python code can be imported as a module. For example, suppose](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [you have a file named](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) wc.py with the following code:

### def linecount(filename): count = 0 [for line in open(filename):](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [count += 1](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) return count

### print(linecount( ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [wc.py](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [))](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[If you run this program, it reads itself and prints the number of lines in the file, which is 7.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [You can also import it like this:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

### [>>> import wc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

[Now you have a module object](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [wc](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

```
>>> wc
<module ' wc ' from ' wc.py ' >
```

Chapter 14. Files

[The module object provides](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [linecount](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### >>> wc.linecount( ' wc.py ' )

[So that’s how you write modules in Python.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[The only problem with this example is that when you import the module it runs the test](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) code at the bottom. Normally when you import a module, it defines new functions but it doesn’t run them.

[Programs that will be imported as modules often use the following idiom:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

| if __name__ ==   | '   | __main__ | '   | :   |
| ---------------- | --- | -------- | --- | --- |
| print(linecount( | '   | wc.py    | '   | ))  |

__name__ is a built-in variable that is set when the program starts. If the program is running [as a script,](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [__name__](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) has the value ' __main__ ' ; in that case, the test code runs. Otherwise, [if the module is being imported, the test code is skipped.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[As an exercise, type this example into a file named](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [wc.py](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) and run it as a script. Then run [the Python interpreter and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [import wc](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [. What is the value of](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [__name__](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) when the module is being imported?

[Warning: If you import a module that has already been imported, Python does nothing. It](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [does not re-read the file, even if it has changed.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[If you want to reload a module, you can use the built-in function](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [reload](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [, but it can be](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [tricky, so the safest thing to do is restart the interpreter and then import the module again.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

## 14.10 [Debugging](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

When you are reading and writing files, you might run into problems with whitespace. [These errors can be hard to debug because spaces, tabs and newlines are normally invisible:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

### >>> s = ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [1 2\t 3\n 4](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [>>> print(s)](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [1 2](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

The built-in function repr can help. It takes any object as an argument and returns a string [representation of the object. For strings, it represents whitespace characters with backslash](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) sequences:

### >>> print(repr(s)) ' 1 2\t 3\n 4 ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

This can be helpful for debugging.

[One other problem you might run into is that different systems use different characters to](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [indicate the end of a line. Some systems use a newline, represented](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [\n](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [. Others use a return](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [character, represented](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [\r](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [Some use both.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [If you move files between different systems,](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) these inconsistencies can cause problems.

[For most systems, there are applications to convert from one format to another. You can](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [find them (and read more about this issue) at](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newline](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) Or, of course, you could write one yourself.

### 14.11. Glossary

## [14.11](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

persistent: [Pertaining to a program that runs indefinitely and keeps at least some of its](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [data in permanent storage.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[format operator:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [An operator,](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [%](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [, that takes a format string and a tuple and generates a](https://thinkpython.com/code) [string that includes the elements of the tuple formatted as specified by the format](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) string.

[format string:](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [A string, used with the format operator, that contains format sequences.](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py)

[format sequence:](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [A sequence of characters in a format string, like](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) %d , that specifies how a [value should be formatted.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[text file:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [A sequence of characters stored in permanent storage like a hard drive.](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

[directory:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) A named collection of files, also called a folder.

path: A string that identifies a file.

relative path: [A path that starts from the current directory.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

absolute path: A path that starts from the topmost directory in the file system.

[catch:](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [To prevent an exception from terminating a program using the](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [try](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [except](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [state-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [ments.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[database:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [A file whose contents are organized like a dictionary with keys that correspond](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [to values.](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

bytes object: An object similar to a string.

[shell:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) A program that allows users to type commands and then executes them by starting [other programs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[pipe object:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) An object that represents a running program, allowing a Python program to [run commands and read the results.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[py](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [Exercise 14.2.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [If you download my solution to Exercise 12.2 from](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [https: // thinkpython. com/](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [code/ anagram_ sets. py](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [, you’ll see that it creates a dictionary that maps from a sorted string of](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [letters to the list of words that can be spelled with those letters. For example,](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) ['](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [opst](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) ['](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [maps to the](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [list](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [[](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) ['](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [opts](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) ' , ' post ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [pots](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [spot](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [stop](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [tops](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) []](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)
