## Chapter 15

# Classes and objects

[At this point you know how to use functions to organize code and built-in types to organize](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) data. The next step is to learn “object-oriented programming”, which uses programmer- [defined types to organize both code and data. Object-oriented programming is a big topic;](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [it will take a few chapters to get there.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[Code examples from this chapter are available from](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [https://thinkpython.com/code/](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [Point1.py](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point2_soln.py) ; solutions to the exercises are available from [https://thinkpython.com/code/](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Point1_soln.py](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

## 15.1 [Programmer-defined types](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[We have used many of Python’s built-in types; now we are going to define a new type. As](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [an example, we will create a type called](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Point](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [that represents a point in two-dimensional](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [space.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[In mathematical notation, points are often written in parentheses with a comma separating](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) the coordinates. For example, ( 0, 0 ) represents the origin, and ( x , y ) represents the point x units to the right and y units up from the origin.

[There are several ways we might represent points in Python:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

- [• We could store the coordinates separately in two variables,](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) x and y .

- We could store the coordinates as elements in a list or tuple.

- [• We could create a new type to represent points as objects.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[Creating a new type is more complicated than the other options, but it has advantages that](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [will be apparent soon.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

[A programmer-defined type is also called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [class](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) . A class definition looks like this:

### [class Point:](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) """Represents a point in 2-D space."""

Chapter 15. Classes and objects

### [Point](https://thinkpython.com/code/letters.py) [blank](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [x](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [3.0](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

### [y](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [4.0](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Figure 15.1: Object diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code)

[The header indicates that the new class is called](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [Point](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) . The body is a docstring that explains what the class is for. You can define variables and methods inside a class definition, [but we will get back to that later.](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py)

[Defining a class named](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [Point](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [creates a](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [class object](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) .

### >>> Point [<class](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ['](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [__main__.Point](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) ' >

Because [Point](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [is defined at the top level, its “full name” is](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) __main__.Point .

[The class object is like a factory for creating objects. To create a Point, you call](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) Point as if it were a function.

### >>> blank = Point() >>> blank [<__main__.Point object at 0xb7e9d3ac>](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[The return value is a reference to a Point object, which we assign to](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [blank](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py)

[Creating a new object is called](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [instantiation](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [, and the object is an](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [instance](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [of the class.](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py)

When you print an instance, Python tells you what class it belongs to and where it is stored [in memory (the prefix](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [0x](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) means that the following number is in hexadecimal).

[Every object is an instance of some class, so “object” and “instance” are interchangeable.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [But in this chapter I use “instance” to indicate that I am talking about a programmer-](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) defined type.

## [15.2](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [Attributes](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

You can assign values to an instance using dot notation:

### >>> blank.x = 3.0 >>> blank.y = 4.0

[This syntax is similar to the syntax for selecting a variable from a module, such as](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) math.pi or string.whitespace [. In this case, though, we are assigning values to named elements of](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [an object. These elements are called](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [attributes](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[As a noun, “AT-trib-ute” is pronounced with emphasis on the first syllable, as opposed to](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [“a-TRIB-ute”, which is a verb.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

[Figure 15.1 is a state diagram that shows the result of these assignments. A state diagram](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [that shows an object and its attributes is called an](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [object diagram](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [.](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

The variable [blank](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [refers to a Point object, which contains two attributes. Each attribute](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) refers to a floating-point number.

You can read the value of an attribute using the same syntax:

### 15.3. Rectangles

### [>>> blank.y](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) 4.0 [>>> x = blank.x](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [>>> x](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [3.0](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

The expression [blank.x](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [means, “Go to the object](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [blank](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [refers to and get the value of](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) x .” In [the example, we assign that value to a variable named](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) x [. There is no conflict between the](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [variable](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [and the attribute](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) x .

[You can use dot notation as part of any expression. For example:](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

[>>>](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [(%g, %g)](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [% (blank.x, blank.y)](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [(3.0, 4.0)](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) >>> distance = math.sqrt(blank.x**2 + blank.y**2) [>>> distance](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [5.0](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[You can pass an instance as an argument in the usual way. For example:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

### [def print_point(p):](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) print( ' (%g, %g) ' % (p.x, p.y))

[print_point](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [takes a point as an argument and displays it in mathematical notation. To](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poker) [invoke it, you can pass](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [blank](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [as an argument:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

### [>>> print_point(blank)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [(3.0, 4.0)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

Inside the function, p is an alias for blank , so if the function modifies p , blank changes.

[As an exercise, write a function called](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) distance_between_points [that takes two Points as](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [arguments and returns the distance between them.](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py)

## [15.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [Rectangles](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

Sometimes it is obvious what the attributes of an object should be, but other times you have to make decisions. For example, imagine you are designing a class to represent rectangles. What attributes would you use to specify the location and size of a rectangle? You can ig- [nore angle; to keep things simple, assume that the rectangle is either vertical or horizontal.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

[There are at least two possibilities:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

- [• You could specify one corner of the rectangle (or the center), the width, and the](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [height.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

- [• You could specify two opposing corners.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[At this point it is hard to say whether either is better than the other, so we’ll implement the](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) first one, just as an example.

Here is the class definition:

Chapter 15. Classes and objects

### [Rectangle](https://thinkpython.com/code/letters.py)

box [width](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [100.0](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [Point](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [height](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [200.0](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) x 0.0 [corner](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [y](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) 0.0

Figure 15.2: Object diagram.

class Rectangle: ["""Represents a rectangle.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

### [attributes: width, height, corner.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) """

[The docstring lists the attributes:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [width](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [height](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) are numbers; corner is a Point object that specifies the lower-left corner.

[To represent a rectangle, you have to instantiate a Rectangle object and assign values to the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) attributes:

### [box = Rectangle()](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [box.width = 100.0](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [box.height = 200.0](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [box.corner = Point()](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [box.corner.x = 0.0](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [box.corner.y = 0.0](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py)

The expression box.corner.x means, “Go to the object box [refers to and select the attribute](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) named [corner](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [; then go to that object and select the attribute named](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [.”](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Figure 15.2 shows the state of this object. An object that is an attribute of another object is](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [embedded](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

## 15.4 Instances as return values

[Functions can return instances. For example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [find_center](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [takes a](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [Rectangle](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) as an argument and returns a Point that contains the coordinates of the center of the [Rectangle](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

### [def find_center(rect):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) p = Point() [p.x = rect.corner.x + rect.width/2](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [p.y = rect.corner.y + rect.height/2](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) return p

[Here is an example that passes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [box](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [as an argument and assigns the resulting Point to](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) center :

### [>>> center = find_center(box)](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [>>> print_point(center)](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) (50, 100)

### 15.5. Objects are mutable

## [15.5](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Objects are mutable](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[You can change the state of an object by making an assignment to one of its attributes. For](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) example, to change the size of a rectangle without changing its position, you can modify [the values of](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [width](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [height](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

### box.width = box.width + 50 box.height = box.height + 100

You can also write functions that modify objects. [For example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [grow_rectangle](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [takes a](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [Rectangle object and two numbers,](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [dwidth](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [dheight](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [, and adds the numbers to the](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [width and height of the rectangle:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py)

### [def grow_rectangle(rect, dwidth, dheight):](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [rect.width += dwidth](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) rect.height += dheight

[Here is an example that demonstrates the effect:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

### >>> box.width, box.height (150.0, 300.0) [>>> grow_rectangle(box, 50, 100)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) >>> box.width, box.height [(200.0, 400.0)](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[Inside the function,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [rect](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [is an alias for](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [box](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [, so when the function modifies](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [rect](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [box](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [changes.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[As an exercise, write a function named](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [move_rectangle](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [that takes a Rectangle and two](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [numbers named](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) dx and dy . It should change the location of the rectangle by adding [dx](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [to](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [the](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [x](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [coordinate of](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [corner](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) and adding dy to the y coordinate of [corner](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

## [15.6](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) Copying

[Aliasing can make a program difficult to read because changes in one place might have](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) unexpected effects in another place. It is hard to keep track of all the variables that might [refer to a given object.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

Copying an object is often an alternative to aliasing. The copy module contains a function called copy that can duplicate any object:

### [>>> p1 = Point()](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [>>> p1.x = 3.0](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) >>> p1.y = 4.0

### >>> import copy [>>> p2 = copy.copy(p1)](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[p1](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [p2](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [contain the same data, but they are not the same Point.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

### [>>> print_point(p1)](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [(3, 4)](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [>>> print_point(p2)](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [(3, 4)](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [>>> p1 is p2](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) False

Chapter 15. Classes and objects

### [box](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [width](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [100.0](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [100.0](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) width box2

### height 200.0 [x](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [0.0](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [200.0](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [height](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

### [corner](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [y](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [0.0](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) corner

[Figure 15.3: Object diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

### [>>> p1 == p2](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) False

The is [operator indicates that](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [p1](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [p2](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [are not the same object, which is what we ex-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [pected.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [But you might have expected](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [==](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [to yield](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [True](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [because these points contain the](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [same data. In that case, you will be disappointed to learn that for instances, the default](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [behavior of the](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [==](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) operator is the same as the is operator; it checks object identity, not object equivalence. That’s because for programmer-defined types, Python doesn’t know [what should be considered equivalent. At least, not yet.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

If you use copy.copy [to duplicate a Rectangle, you will find that it copies the Rectangle](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [object but not the embedded Point.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

### [>>> box2 = copy.copy(box)](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [>>> box2 is box](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) False [>>> box2.corner is box.corner](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) True

Figure 15.3 shows what the object diagram looks like. This operation is called a shallow copy because it copies the object and any references it contains, but not the embedded objects.

For most [applications,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [this](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [is](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [not](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [what](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [you](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [want.](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [In](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [this](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [invoking](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) grow_rectangle on one of the Rectangles would not affect the other, [but invoking](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [move_rectangle](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [on either would affect both! This behavior is confusing and error-prone.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

| object but also the objects it refers to, and the objects | they      | refer to, and so on. You will not |
| --------------------------------------------------------- | --------- | --------------------------------- |
| be surprised to learn that this operation is called a     | deep copy | .                                 |

### >>> box3 = copy.deepcopy(box) >>> box3 is box False [>>> box3.corner is box.corner](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) False

box3 and box are completely separate objects.

[As an exercise, write a version of](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [move_rectangle](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [that creates and returns a new Rectangle](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [instead of modifying the old one.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

## 15.7 [Debugging](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[When you start working with objects, you are likely to encounter some new exceptions. If](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) you try to access an attribute that doesn’t exist, you get an AttributeError :

### 15.8. Glossary

[>>> p = Point()](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) >>> p.x = 3 [>>> p.y = 4](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [>>> p.z](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [AttributeError: Point instance has no attribute](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code) [z](https://thinkpython.com/code) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code) [If you are not sure what type an object is, you can ask:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [>>> type(p)](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [<class](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [__main__.Point](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) ' > [You can also use](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [isinstance](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [to check whether an object is an instance of a class:](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [>>> isinstance(p, Point)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [True](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [If you are not sure whether an object has a particular attribute, you can use the built-in](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [function](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [hasattr](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) : [>>> hasattr(p,](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) True [>>> hasattr(p,](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) ['](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [z](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) ['](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [)](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [False](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) The first argument can be any object; the second argument is a [string](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [that contains the name](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [of the attribute.](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

[You can also use a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [try](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [statement to see if the object has the attributes you need:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [try:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [x = p.x](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [except AttributeError:](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [x = 0](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) This approach can make it easier to write functions that work with different types; more [on that topic is coming up in Section 17.9.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

## [15.8](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[class:](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) A programmer-defined type. A class definition creates a new class object.

[class object:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) An object that contains information about a programmer-defined type. The class object can be used to create instances of the type.

instance: An object that belongs to a class.

instantiate: To create a new object.

[attribute:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [One of the named values associated with an object.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

embedded object: [An object that is stored as an attribute of another object.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[shallow copy:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [To copy the contents of an object, including any references to embedded](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [objects; implemented by the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [copy](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [function in the](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [copy](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [module.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

| deep copy: | To copy the contents of an object as well as any embedded objects, and any |
| ---------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| copy       | module.                                                                    |

[object diagram:](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [A diagram that shows objects, their attributes, and the values of the at-](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) tributes.

Chapter 15. Classes and objects

## Chapter 16

# Classes and functions

[Now that we know how to create new types, the next step is to write functions that take](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [programmer-defined objects as parameters and return them as results.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [In this chapter I](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [also present “functional programming style” and two new program development plans.](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

[Code examples from this chapter are available from](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [https://thinkpython.com/code/](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [Time1.py](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [. Solutions to the exercises are at](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [py](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point2_soln.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point2_soln.py)

## [16.1](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [Time](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

[As another example of a programmer-defined type, we’ll define a class called](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) Time that [records the time of day. The class definition looks like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[class Time:](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) ["""Represents the time of day.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

### attributes: hour, minute, second """

We can create a new Time object and assign attributes for hours, minutes, and seconds:

### time = Time() [time.hour = 11](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [time.minute = 59](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) time.second = 30

[The state diagram for the](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [Time](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [object looks like Figure 16.1.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[As an exercise, write a function called](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [print_time](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [that takes a Time object and prints it in](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [the form](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [hour:minute:second](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [. Hint: the format sequence](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) ['](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [%.2d](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) ['](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [prints an integer using](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [at least two digits, including a leading zero if necessary.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

[Write a boolean function called](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [is_after](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [that takes two Time objects,](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [t1](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newline) [and](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [t2](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [, and re-](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [turns](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [True](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [if](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [t1](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [follows](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [t2](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [chronologically and](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [False](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) [otherwise. Challenge: don’t use an](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_db.py) if statement.

## 16.2 [Pure functions](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[In the next few sections, we’ll write two functions that add time values. They demonstrate](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [two kinds of functions: pure functions and modifiers. They also demonstrate a develop-](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) ment plan I’ll call [prototype and patch](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [, which is a way of tackling a complex problem by](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) starting with a simple prototype and incrementally dealing with the complications.

[Here is a simple prototype of](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [add_time](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[def add_time(t1, t2):](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [sum = Time()](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) sum.hour = t1.hour + t2.hour [sum.minute = t1.minute + t2.minute](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [sum.second = t1.second + t2.second](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [return sum](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[The function creates a new](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [Time](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [object, initializes its attributes, and returns a reference to](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [the new object. This is called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [pure function](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [because it does not modify any of the objects](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) passed to it as arguments and it has no effect, like displaying a value or getting user input, [other than returning a value.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[To test this function, I’ll create two Time objects:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) start [contains the start time of a movie,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) like [Monty Python and the Holy Grail](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [, and](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [duration](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [contains the run time of the movie,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [which is one hour 35 minutes.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[add_time](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [figures out when the movie will be done.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [>>> start = Time()](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) >>> start.hour = 9 [>>> start.minute = 45](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) >>> start.second =

### >>> duration = Time() >>> duration.hour = 1 [>>> duration.minute = 35](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [>>> duration.second = 0](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### [>>> done = add_time(start, duration)](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [>>> print_time(done)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) 10:80:00

[The result,](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [10:80:00](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [might not be what you were hoping for.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [The problem is that this](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [function does not deal with cases where the number of seconds or minutes adds up to](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [more than sixty. When that happens, we have to “carry” the extra seconds into the minute](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) column or the extra minutes into the hour column.

Here’s an improved version:

### 16.3. Modifiers

[def add_time(t1, t2):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) sum = Time() [sum.hour = t1.hour + t2.hour](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [sum.minute = t1.minute + t2.minute](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [sum.second = t1.second + t2.second](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

### if sum.second >= 60: [sum.second -= 60](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [sum.minute += 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

### [if sum.minute >= 60:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [sum.minute -= 60](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [sum.hour += 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

### return sum

[Although this function is correct, it is starting to get big. We will see a shorter alternative](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [later.](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

## [16.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [Modifiers](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[Sometimes it is useful for a function to modify the objects it gets as parameters. In that case,](https://thinkpython.com/code/GoodKangaroo.py) the changes are visible to the caller. Functions that work this way are called modifiers .

[increment](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [, which adds a given number of seconds to a](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [Time](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) object, can be written naturally [as a modifier. Here is a rough draft:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

### def increment(time, seconds): [time.second += seconds](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

### [if time.second >= 60:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [time.second -= 60](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [time.minute += 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

### [if time.minute >= 60:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) time.minute -= 60 time.hour += 1

[The first line performs the basic operation; the remainder deals with the special cases we](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) saw before.

[Is this function correct? What happens if](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) seconds is much greater than sixty?

[In that case, it is not enough to carry once; we have to keep doing it until](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) time.second is [less than sixty. One solution is to replace the](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [if](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) statements with [while](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [statements. That](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [would make the function correct, but not very efficient.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [As an exercise, write a correct](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [version of](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [increment](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [that doesn’t contain any loops.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[Anything that can be done with modifiers can also be done with pure functions. In fact,](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [some programming languages only allow pure functions.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) There is some evidence that [programs that use pure functions are faster to develop and less error-prone than programs](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [that use modifiers. But modifiers are convenient at times, and functional programs tend to](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) be less efficient.

Chapter 16. Classes and functions

[In general, I recommend that you write pure functions whenever it is reasonable and resort](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) to modifiers only if there is a compelling advantage. [This approach might be called a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [functional programming style](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[As an exercise, write a “pure” version of](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [increment](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) that creates and returns a new Time object rather than modifying the parameter.

## [16.4](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [Prototyping versus planning](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[The development plan I am demonstrating is called “prototype and patch”. For each func-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [tion, I wrote a prototype that performed the basic calculation and then tested it, patching](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [errors along the way.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

This approach can be effective, especially if you don’t yet have a deep understanding of the problem. [But incremental corrections can generate code that is unnecessarily](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) complicated—since it deals with many special cases—and unreliable—since it is hard to [know if you have found all the errors.](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

An alternative is designed development , in which high-level insight into the problem can [make the programming much easier. In this case, the insight is that a Time object is really](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [a three-digit number in base 60 (see](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [). The](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [second](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [attribute is the “ones column”, the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [minute](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [attribute is the “sixties column”, and the](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [hour](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [attribute is the “thirty-six hundreds column”.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[When we wrote](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [add_time](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [increment](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [, we were effectively doing addition in base 60,](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) which is why we had to carry from one column to the next.

This observation suggests another approach to the whole problem—we can convert Time [objects to integers and take advantage of the fact that the computer knows how to do](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [integer arithmetic.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_rankings)

[Here is a function that converts Times to integers:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

### [def time_to_int(time):](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) minutes = time.hour * 60 + time.minute [seconds = minutes * 60 + time.second](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [return seconds](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

And here is a function that converts an integer to a Time (recall that divmod divides the first argument by the second and returns the quotient and remainder as a tuple).

def int_to_time(seconds): [time = Time()](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [minutes, time.second = divmod(seconds, 60)](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) time.hour, time.minute = divmod(minutes, 60) [return time](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py)

[You might have to think a bit, and run some tests, to convince yourself that these functions](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [are correct. One way to test them is to check that](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [time_to_int(int_to_time(x)) == x](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) for many values of [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [. This is an example of a consistency check.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[Once you are convinced they are correct, you can use them to rewrite](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [add_time](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

### [def add_time(t1, t2):](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [seconds = time_to_int(t1) + time_to_int(t2)](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) return int_to_time(seconds)

### 16.5. Debugging

[This version is shorter than the original, and easier to verify.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) As an exercise, rewrite increment using time_to_int [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [int_to_time](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[In some ways, converting from base 60 to base 10 and back is harder than just dealing with](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [times. Base conversion is more abstract; our intuition for dealing with time values is better.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[But if we have the insight to treat times as base 60 numbers and make the investment of](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) writing the conversion functions ( time_to_int and int_to_time [), we get a program that](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [is shorter, easier to read and debug, and more reliable.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set)

[It is also easier to add features later. For example, imagine subtracting two Times to find](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [the duration between them. The naive approach would be to implement subtraction with](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) borrowing. Using the conversion functions would be easier and more likely to be correct.

[Ironically, sometimes making a problem harder (or more general) makes it easier (because](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) there are fewer special cases and fewer opportunities for error).

## [16.5](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [Debugging](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

A Time object is well-formed if the values of [minute](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [second](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [are between 0 and 60](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [(including 0 but not 60) and if](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [hour](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [is positive.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [hour](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [minute](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [should be integer values,](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [but we might allow](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [second](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [to have a fraction part.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[Requirements like these are called](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [invariants](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [because they should always be true. To put](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [it a different way, if they are not true, something has gone wrong.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

Writing code to check invariants can help detect errors and find their causes. For example, [you might have a function like](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) valid_time [that takes a Time object and returns](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [False](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [if it](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [violates an invariant:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

[def valid_time(time):](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) if time.hour < 0 or time.minute < 0 or time.second < 0: [return False](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) if time.minute >= 60 or time.second >= 60: return False [return True](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

At the beginning of each function you could check the arguments to make sure they are valid:

[def add_time(t1, t2):](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) if not valid_time(t1) or not valid_time(t2): [raise ValueError(](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [invalid Time object in add_time](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) ' ) [seconds = time_to_int(t1) + time_to_int(t2)](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [return int_to_time(seconds)](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[Or you could use an](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [assert statement](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [, which checks a given invariant and raises an excep-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [tion if it fails:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

```
def add_time(t1, t2):
assert valid_time(t1) and valid_time(t2)
seconds = time_to_int(t1) + time_to_int(t2)
return int_to_time(seconds)
```

[assert](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [statements are useful because they distinguish code that deals with normal condi-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) tions from code that checks for errors.

Chapter 16. Classes and functions

## [16.6](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Glossary](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

prototype and patch: [A development plan that involves writing a rough draft of a pro-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [gram, testing, and correcting errors as they are found.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[designed development:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [A development plan that involves high-level insight into the](https://thinkpython.com/code) [problem and more planning than incremental development or prototype develop-](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [ment.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[pure function:](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) A function that does not modify any of the objects it receives as arguments. [Most pure functions are fruitful.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[modifier:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [A function that changes one or more of the objects it receives as arguments. Most](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [modifiers are void; that is, they return](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [None](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [.](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

[functional programming style:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) A style of program design in which the majority of func- [tions are pure.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[invariant:](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [A condition that should always be true during the execution of a program.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

assert statement: [A statement that checks a condition and raises an exception if it fails.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)
