## [Chapter 17](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

# [Classes and methods](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[Although we are using some of Python’s object-oriented features, the programs from the](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [last two chapters are not really object-oriented because they don’t represent the relation-](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) ships between programmer-defined types and the functions that operate on them. The next [step is to transform those functions into methods that make the relationships explicit.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[Code examples from this chapter are available from](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [https://thinkpython.com/code/](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [Time2.py](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [, and solutions to the exercises are in](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [https://thinkpython.com/code/Point2_](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [soln.py](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

## [17.1](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [Object-oriented features](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[Python is an](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [object-oriented programming language](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) , which means that it provides fea- [tures that support object-oriented programming, which has these defining characteristics:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

- Programs include class and method definitions.

- [• Most of the computation is expressed in terms of operations on objects.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

- Objects often represent things in the real world, and methods often correspond to the ways things in the real world interact.

[For example, the](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [Time](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) class defined in Chapter 16 corresponds to the way people record [the time of day, and the functions we defined correspond to the kinds of things people do](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [with times. Similarly, the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Point](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Rectangle](http://gutenberg.org/) [classes in Chapter 15 correspond to the](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [mathematical concepts of a point and a rectangle.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[So far, we have not taken advantage of the features Python provides to support object-](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [oriented programming.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [These features are not strictly necessary; most of them provide](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [alternative syntax for things we have already done. But in many cases, the alternative is](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [more concise and more accurately conveys the structure of the program.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

| For example, in         | Time1.py | there is no obvious connection between the class definition and |
| ----------------------- | -------- | --------------------------------------------------------------- |
| tion takes at least one | Time     | object as an argument.                                          |

Chapter 17. Classes and methods

[This observation is the motivation for](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [methods](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ; a method is a function that is associated [with a particular class. We have seen methods for strings, lists, dictionaries and tuples. In](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [this chapter, we will define methods for programmer-defined types.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Methods are semantically the same as functions, but there are two syntactic differences:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

- [• Methods are defined inside a class definition in order to make the relationship be-](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [tween the class and the method explicit.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

- [• The syntax for invoking a method is different from the syntax for calling a function.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[In the next few sections, we will take the functions from the previous two chapters and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [transform them into methods. This transformation is purely mechanical; you can do it by](http://www.thinkpython.com/) following a sequence of steps. If you are comfortable converting from one form to another, you will be able to choose the best form for whatever you are doing.

## 17.2 [Printing objects](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[In Chapter 16, we defined a class named](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) Time [and in Section 16.1, you wrote a function](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [named](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [print_time](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

### class Time: ["""Represents the time of day."""](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

### [def print_time(time):](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) print( ['](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [%.2d:%.2d:%.2d](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) ['](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) % (time.hour, time.minute, time.second))

[To call this function, you have to pass a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) Time object as an argument:

### [>>> start = Time()](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [>>> start.hour = 9](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [>>> start.minute = 45](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [>>> start.second = 00](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [>>> print_time(start)](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [09:45:00](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

To make [print_time](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) a method, all we have to do is move the function definition inside the class definition. Notice the change in indentation.

### class Time: def print_time(time): [print(](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [%.2d:%.2d:%.2d](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [% (time.hour, time.minute, time.second))](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

[Now there are two ways to call](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [print_time](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [. The first (and less common) way is to use](http://gutenberg.org/) function syntax:

### [>>> Time.print_time(start)](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) 09:45:00

[In this use of dot notation,](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [Time](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [is the name of the class, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [print_time](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [is the name of the](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [method.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [start](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) is passed as a parameter.

[The second (and more concise) way is to use method syntax:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

### [>>> start.print_time()](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) 09:45:00

### 17.3. Another example

[In this use of dot notation,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [print_time](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) is the name of the method (again), and start is [the object the method is invoked on, which is called the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [subject](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [Just as the subject of](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [a sentence is what the sentence is about, the subject of a method invocation is what the](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [method is about.](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py)

[Inside the method, the subject is assigned to the first parameter, so in this case](https://thinkpython.com/code) start is assigned to time .

By convention, the first parameter of a method is called [self](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [, so it would be more common](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [to write](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [print_time](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

### [class Time:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [def print_time(self):](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [print(](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [%.2d:%.2d:%.2d](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) ['](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [% (self.hour, self.minute, self.second))](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

The reason for this convention is an implicit metaphor:

- The syntax for a function call, [print_time(start)](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [, suggests that the function is the](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [active agent. It says something like, “Hey](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [print_time](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) ! Here’s an object for you to [print.”](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

- [• In object-oriented programming, the objects are the active agents. A method invoca-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [tion like](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [start.print_time()](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [says “Hey](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [start](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poker) [! Please print yourself.”](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py)

[This change in perspective might be more polite, but it is not obvious that it is useful. In the](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [examples we have seen so far, it may not be. But sometimes shifting responsibility from the](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [functions onto the objects makes it possible to write more versatile functions (or methods),](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [and makes it easier to maintain and reuse code.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[As an exercise, rewrite](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) time_to_int [(from Section 16.4) as a method. You might be tempted](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [to rewrite](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [int_to_time](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [as a method, too, but that doesn’t really make sense because there](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) would be no object to invoke it on.

## [17.3](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [Another example](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

Here’s a version of increment (from Section 16.3) rewritten as a method:

```
# inside class Time:
```

### [def increment(self, seconds):](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [seconds += self.time_to_int()](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [return int_to_time(seconds)](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

This version assumes that [time_to_int](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [is written as a method. Also, note that it is a pure](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [function, not a modifier.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[Here’s how you would invoke](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [increment](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

### >>> start.print_time() [09:45:00](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [>>> end = start.increment(1337)](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [>>> end.print_time()](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) 10:07:17

Chapter 17. Classes and methods

[The subject,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [start](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [, gets assigned to the first parameter,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [self](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) . The argument, , gets assigned to the second parameter, [seconds](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[This mechanism can be confusing, especially if you make an error.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [For example, if you](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [invoke](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [increment](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [with two arguments, you get:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

### >>> end = start.increment(1337, 460) TypeError: increment() takes 2 positional arguments but 3 were given

The error message is initially confusing, because there are only two arguments in paren- [theses. But the subject is also considered an argument, so all together that’s three.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

By the way, a [positional argument](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [is an argument that doesn’t have a parameter name;](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [that is, it is not a keyword argument. In this function call:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

### sketch(parrot, cage, dead=True)

parrot [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [cage](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) are positional, and dead is a keyword argument.

## 17.4 [A more complicated example](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

Rewriting [is_after](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [(from Section 16.1) is slightly more complicated because it takes two](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [Time objects as parameters. In this case it is conventional to name the first parameter](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [self](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [and the second parameter](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [other](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py)

```
# inside class Time:
```

### def is_after(self, other): [return self.time_to_int() > other.time_to_int()](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[To use this method, you have to invoke it on one object and pass the other as an argument:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

### [>>> end.is_after(start)](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) True

[One nice thing about this syntax is that it almost reads like English: “end is after start?”](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

## 17.5 The init method

The init method (short for “initialization”) is a special method that gets invoked when an [object is instantiated. Its full name is](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [__init__](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [(two underscore characters, followed by](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) init [, and then two more underscores). An init method for the](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [Time](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) class might look like this:

```
# inside class Time:
```

### [def __init__(self, hour=0, minute=0, second=0):](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [self.hour = hour](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [self.minute = minute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) self.second = second

[It is common for the parameters of](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [__init__](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) to have the same names as the attributes. The statement

### self.hour = hour

### 17.6. The __str__ method

[stores the value of the parameter](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [hour](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [as an attribute of](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) self .

The parameters are optional, so if you call [Time](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [with no arguments, you get the default](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [values.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [>>> time = Time()](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) >>> time.print_time() 00:00:00

If you provide one argument, it overrides hour :

### [>>> time = Time (9)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [>>> time.print_time()](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [09:00:00](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

[If you provide two arguments, they override](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [hour](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [and](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [minute](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

### [>>> time = Time(9, 45)](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [>>> time.print_time()](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) 09:45:00

[And if you provide three arguments, they override all three default values.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

As an exercise, write an init method for the Point class that takes [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [y](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [as optional](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [parameters and assigns them to the corresponding attributes.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal)

## [17.6](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [The](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) __str__ [method](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[__str__](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) is a special method, like __init__ , that is supposed to return a string representa- [tion of an object.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[For example, here is a](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [str](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [method for Time objects:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

```
# inside class Time:
```

### [def __str__(self):](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [return](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) %.2d:%.2d:%.2d ' [% (self.hour, self.minute, self.second)](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt)

[When you](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [print](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [an object, Python invokes the](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) str [method:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort)

### >>> time = Time(9, 45) >>> print(time) 09:45:00

When I write a new class, I almost always start by writing [__init__](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [, which makes it easier](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [to instantiate objects, and](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [__str__](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [, which is useful for debugging.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

As an exercise, write a str method for the [Point](http://gutenberg.org/) [class. Create a Point object and print it.](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py)

## [17.7](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [Operator overloading](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

[By](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [defining](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) other [special](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [methods,](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [you](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [can](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [specify](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [the](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [behavior](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [of](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [operators](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [on](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [programmer-defined types. For example, if you define a method named](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) __add__ for the Time class, you can use the [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [operator on Time objects.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

Here is what the definition might look like:

Chapter 17. Classes and methods

```
# inside class Time:
```

### [def __add__(self, other):](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) seconds = self.time_to_int() + other.time_to_int() [return int_to_time(seconds)](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

And here is how you could use it:

### [>>> start = Time(9, 45)](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [>>> duration = Time(1, 35)](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [>>> print(start + duration)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) 11:20:00

[When you apply the](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [operator to Time objects, Python invokes](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [__add__](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [. When you print](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [the result, Python invokes](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [__str__](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [. So there is a lot happening behind the scenes!](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

Changing the behavior of an operator so that it works with programmer-defined types is called [operator overloading](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [. For every operator in Python there is a corresponding spe-](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) cial method, like __add__ . For more details, see [http://docs.python.org/3/reference/](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/) datamodel.html#specialnames [.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

As an exercise, write an add method for the Point class.

## [17.8](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [Type-based dispatch](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[In the previous section we added two Time objects, but you also might want to add an](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [integer to a Time object.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) The following is a version of __add__ that checks the type of other [and invokes either](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [add_time](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [or](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) increment :

```
# inside class Time:
```

### [def __add__(self, other):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [if isinstance(other, Time):](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [return self.add_time(other)](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [else:](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [return self.increment(other)](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

### def add_time(self, other): seconds = self.time_to_int() + other.time_to_int() [return int_to_time(seconds)](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

### def increment(self, seconds): [seconds += self.time_to_int()](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [return int_to_time(seconds)](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[The built-in function](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [isinstance](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [takes a value and a class object, and returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [True](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [if the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [value is an instance of the class.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

[If](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [other](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [is a Time object,](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [__add__](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [invokes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [add_time](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [. Otherwise it assumes that the param-](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [eter is a number and invokes](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [increment](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [. This operation is called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [type-based dispatch](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [because it dispatches the computation to different methods based on the type of the argu-](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) ments.

Here are examples that use the + operator with different types:

### 17.9. Polymorphism

```
>>> start = Time(9, 45)
>>> duration = Time(1, 35)
>>> print(start + duration)
11:20:00
>>> print(start + 1337)
10:07:17
```

[Unfortunately, this implementation of addition is not commutative.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) If the integer is the first operand, you get

```
>>> print(1337 + start)
TypeError: unsupported operand type(s) for +: ' int ' and ' instance '
```

[The problem is, instead of asking the Time object to add an integer, Python is asking an](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [integer to add a Time object, and it doesn’t know how. But there is a clever solution for this](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [problem: the special method](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) __radd__ , which stands for “right-side add”. This method is invoked when a Time object appears on the right side of the + operator. Here’s the [definition:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

```
# inside class Time:
```

### def __radd__(self, other): [return self.__add__(other)](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

[And here’s how it’s used:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

### [>>> print(1337 + start)](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [10:07:17](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

As an exercise, write an add method for Points that works with either a Point object or a [tuple:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

- [• If the second operand is a Point, the method should return a new Point whose](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [coordinate is the sum of the](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [x](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [coordinates of the operands, and likewise for the](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk2.py) y [coordinates.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

- [• If the second operand is a tuple, the method should add the first element of the tuple](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [to the](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) x coordinate and the second element to the y coordinate, and return a new [Point with the result.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

## 17.9 [Polymorphism](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

[Type-based dispatch is useful when it is necessary, but (fortunately) it is not always neces-](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) sary. Often you can avoid it by writing functions that work correctly for arguments with different types.

[Many of the functions we wrote for strings also work for other sequence types. For exam-](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [ple, in Section 11.2 we used](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [histogram](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [to count the number of times each letter appears in](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [a word.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

### def histogram(s): [d = dict()](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [for c in s:](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [if c not in d:](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) d[c] = 1

Chapter 17. Classes and methods

### [else:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) d[c] = d[c]+1 [return d](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

This function also works for lists, tuples, and even dictionaries, as long as the elements of s [are hashable, so they can be used as keys in](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [d](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

```
>>> t = [ ' spam ' , ' egg ' , ' spam ' , ' spam ' , ' bacon ' , ' spam ' ]
>>> histogram(t)
{ ' bacon ' : 1, ' egg ' : 1, ' spam ' : 4}
```

[Functions that work with several types are called](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) polymorphic [.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [Polymorphism can fa-](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#printf-style-string-formatting) [cilitate code reuse. For example, the built-in function](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [sum](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) , which adds the elements of a [sequence, works as long as the elements of the sequence support addition.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

Since Time objects provide an add method, they work with [sum](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [>>> t1 = Time(7, 43)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [>>> t2 = Time(7, 41)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [>>> t3 = Time(7, 37)](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) >>> total = sum([t1, t2, t3]) >>> print(total) 23:01:00

[In general, if all of the operations inside a function work with a given type, the function](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [works with that type.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[The best kind of polymorphism is the unintentional kind, where you discover that a func-](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [tion you already wrote can be applied to a type you never planned for.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

## 17.10 [Debugging](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

[It is legal to add attributes to objects at any point in the execution of a program, but if](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [you have objects with the same type that don’t have the same attributes, it is easy to make](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [mistakes. It is considered a good idea to initialize all of an object’s attributes in the init](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [method.](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py)

[If you are not sure whether an object has a particular attribute, you can use the built-in](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) function [hasattr](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) (see Section 15.7).

Another way to access attributes is the built-in function vars , which takes an object and [returns a dictionary that maps from attribute names (as strings) to their values:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

```
>>> p = Point(3, 4)
>>> vars(p)
{ ' y ' : 4, ' x ' : 3}
```

[For purposes of debugging, you might find it useful to keep this function handy:](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

### [def print_attributes(obj):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [for attr in vars(obj):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [print(attr, getattr(obj, attr))](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

print_attributes [traverses the dictionary and prints each attribute name and its corre-](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) sponding value.

[The built-in function](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [getattr](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [takes an object and an attribute name (as a string) and returns](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) the attribute’s value.

### 17.11. Interface and implementation

## [17.11](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Interface and implementation](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[One of the goals of object-oriented design is to make software more maintainable, which](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) means that you can keep the program working when other parts of the system change, and [modify the program to meet new requirements.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

A design principle that helps achieve that goal is to keep interfaces separate from imple- [mentations. For objects, that means that the methods a class provides should not depend](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [on how the attributes are represented.](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[For example, in this chapter we developed a class that represents a time of day. Methods](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [provided by this class include](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [time_to_int](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book1.py) [is_after](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book1.py) [, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book1.py) [add_time](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book1.py) [.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format)

[We could implement those methods in several ways. The details of the implementation](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) depend on how we represent time. In this chapter, the attributes of a Time object are hour , [minute](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [second](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[As an alternative, we could replace these attributes with a single integer representing the](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [number of seconds since midnight. This implementation would make some methods, like](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) is_after , easier to write, but it makes other methods harder.

[After you deploy a new class, you might discover a better implementation. If other parts](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [of the program are using your class, it might be time-consuming and error-prone to change](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [the interface.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[But if you designed the interface carefully, you can change the implementation without](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) changing the interface, which means that other parts of the program don’t have to change.

## [17.12](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) Glossary

[object-oriented language:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [A](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [language](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [that](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [provides](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [features,](http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=k4RRi_ntQc8) [such](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [as](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [programmer-](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [defined types and methods, that facilitate object-oriented programming.](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt)

[object-oriented programming:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [A style of programming in which data and the operations](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) that manipulate it are organized into classes and methods.

method: A function that is defined inside a class definition and is invoked on instances of [that class.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

subject: [The object a method is invoked on.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

positional argument: [An argument that does not include a parameter name, so it is not a](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [keyword argument.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[operator overloading:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [Changing the behavior of an operator like](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [so it works with a](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [programmer-defined type.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

[type-based dispatch:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [A programming pattern that checks the type of an operand and in-](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [vokes different functions for different types.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

polymorphic: Pertaining to a function that can work with more than one type.

Chapter 17. Classes and methods

## [Chapter 18](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

# [Inheritance](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[The language feature most often associated with object-oriented programming is](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [inheri-](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [tance](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [. Inheritance is the ability to define a new class that is a modified version of an ex-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) isting class. In this chapter I demonstrate inheritance using classes that represent playing [cards, decks of cards, and poker hands.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

If you don’t play poker, you can read about it at [http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poker](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [but you don’t have to; I’ll tell you what you need to know for the exercises.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[Code examples from this chapter are available from](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [https://thinkpython.com/code/](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [Card.py](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) .

## [18.1](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Card objects](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[There are fifty-two cards in a deck, each of which belongs to one of four suits and one of](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [thirteen ranks. The suits are Spades, Hearts, Diamonds, and Clubs (in descending order in](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) bridge). The ranks are Ace, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, 10, Jack, Queen, and King. Depending on [the game that you are playing, an Ace may be higher than King or lower than 2.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

If we want to define a new object to represent a playing card, it is obvious what the attributes should be: rank and suit . It is not as obvious what type the attributes should be. [One possibility is to use strings containing words like](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [Spade](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) for suits and ' Queen ' for ranks. One problem with this implementation is that it would not be easy to compare cards [to see which had a higher rank or suit.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

An alternative is to use integers to [encode](http://gutenberg.org/) [the ranks and suits. In this context, “encode”](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [means that we are going to define a mapping between numbers and suits, or between](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [numbers and ranks.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) This kind of encoding is not meant to be a secret (that would be [“encryption”).](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

[For example, this table shows the suits and the corresponding integer codes:](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

| Spades   | →   |
| -------- | --- |
| Hearts   | →   |
| Diamonds | →   |
| Clubs    | →   |

Chapter 18. Inheritance

[This code makes it easy to compare cards; because higher suits map to higher numbers, we](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) can compare suits by comparing their codes.

[The mapping for ranks is fairly obvious; each of the numerical ranks maps to the corre-](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [sponding integer, and for face cards:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

| Jack  | →   |
| ----- | --- |
| Queen | →   |
| King  | →   |

I am using the [→](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [symbol to make it clear that these mappings are not part of the Python](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [program. They are part of the program design, but they don’t appear explicitly in the code.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[The class definition for](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [Card](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [looks like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

### [class Card:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) ["""Represents a standard playing card."""](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

### def __init__(self, suit=0, rank=2): [self.suit = suit](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) self.rank = rank

[As usual, the init method takes an optional parameter for each attribute. The default card](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [is the 2 of Clubs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py)

[To create a Card, you call](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [Card](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [with the suit and rank of the card you want.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

### [queen_of_diamonds = Card(1, 12)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

## 18.2 [Class attributes](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

In order to print Card objects in a way that people can easily read, we need a mapping [from the integer codes to the corresponding ranks and suits. A natural way to do that is](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [with lists of strings. We assign these lists to](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [class attributes](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt)

```
# inside class Card:
```

```
suit_names = [ ' Clubs ' , ' Diamonds ' , ' Hearts ' , ' Spades ' ]
rank_names = [None, ' Ace ' , ' ' , ' ' , ' ' , ' ' , ' ' , ' ' ,
' ' , ' ' , ' ' , ' Jack ' , ' Queen ' , ' King ' ]
```

### [def __str__(self):](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) return ' [%s of %s](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [% (Card.rank_names[self.rank],](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [Card.suit_names[self.suit])](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

Variables like suit_names [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [rank_names](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [, which are defined inside a class but outside](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [of any method, are called class attributes because they are associated with the class object](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) Card .

[This term distinguishes them from variables like](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [suit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [and](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [rank](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [, which are called](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [instance](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) attributes [because they are associated with a particular instance.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

[Both kinds of attribute are accessed using dot notation.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [For example, in](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [__str__](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newline) [,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newline) [self](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) is a Card object, [and](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [self.rank](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [is its rank.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [Similarly,](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) [Card](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) [is a class object,](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_db.py) Card.rank_names is a list of strings associated with the class.

### 18.3. Comparing cards

### [type](https://thinkpython.com/code/letters.py) [list](https://thinkpython.com/code/letters.py)

### [Card](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [suit_names](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

### list

### [rank_names](https://thinkpython.com/code)

### [Card](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set)

### card1 suit

### [rank](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[Figure 18.1: Object diagram.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py)

[Every card has its own](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) suit and rank , but there is only one copy of suit_names and [rank_names](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[Putting it all together, the expression](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Card.rank_names[self.rank]](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [means “use the at-](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [tribute](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [rank](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [from the object](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [self](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [as an index into the list](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) rank_names from the class Card , and select the appropriate string.”

[The first element of](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [rank_names](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [is](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [None](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [because there is no card with rank zero. By includ-](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [ing](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [None](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [as a place-keeper, we get a mapping with the nice property that the index 2 maps](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [to the string](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [, and so on. To avoid this tweak, we could have used a dictionary instead](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [of a list.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

With the methods we have so far, we can create and print cards:

### >>> card1 = Card(2, 11) [>>> print(card1)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [Jack of Hearts](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Figure 18.1 is a diagram of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [Card](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) class object and one Card instance. [Card](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) is a class [object; its type is](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [type](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [card1](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [is an instance of](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [Card](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [, so its type is](http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=k4RRi_ntQc8) [Card](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [. To save space, I](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [didn’t draw the contents of](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) suit_names [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [rank_names](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt)

## 18.3 Comparing cards

[For built-in types, there are relational operators (](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [<](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [>](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [==](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) , etc.) that compare values and determine when one is greater than, less than, or equal to another. For programmer-defined [types, we can override the behavior of the built-in operators by providing a method named](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [__lt__](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [, which stands for “less than”.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

__lt__ [takes two parameters,](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [self](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [other](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [, and returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [True](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [if](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [self](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [is strictly less than](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [other](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

[The correct ordering for cards is not obvious. For example, which is better, the 3 of Clubs](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [or the 2 of Diamonds? One has a higher rank, but the other has a higher suit. In order to](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [compare cards, you have to decide whether rank or suit is more important.](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d)

[The answer might depend on what game you are playing, but to keep things simple, we’ll](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [make the arbitrary choice that suit is more important, so all of the Spades outrank all of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) Diamonds, and so on.

Chapter 18. Inheritance

[With that decided, we can write](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [__lt__](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

```
# inside class Card:
```

[def __lt__(self, other):](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [# check the suits](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [if self.suit < other.suit: return True](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [if self.suit > other.suit: return False](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set)

### [# suits are the same... check ranks](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [return self.rank < other.rank](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

[You can write this more concisely using tuple comparison:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

```
# inside class Card:
```

### [def __lt__(self, other):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [t1 = self.suit, self.rank](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [t2 = other.suit, other.rank](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [return t1 < t2](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

As an exercise, write an __lt__ method for Time objects. You can use tuple comparison, [but you also might consider comparing integers.](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

## 18.4 [Decks](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

Now that we have Cards, the next step is to define Decks. Since a deck is made up of cards, [it is natural for each Deck to contain a list of cards as an attribute.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[The following is a class definition for](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [Deck](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [. The init method creates the attribute](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [cards](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [generates the standard set of fifty-two cards:](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py)

```
class Deck:
```

[def __init__(self):](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [self.cards = []](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [for suit in range(4):](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) for rank in range(1, 14): card = Card(suit, rank) [self.cards.append(card)](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

[The easiest way to populate the deck is with a nested loop. The outer loop enumerates the](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [suits from 0 to 3. The inner loop enumerates the ranks from 1 to 13. Each iteration creates](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [a new Card with the current suit and rank, and appends it to](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [self.cards](http://gutenberg.org/) [.](http://gutenberg.org/)

## [18.6](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) Add, remove, shuffle and sort

To deal cards, we would like a method that removes a card from the deck and returns it. [The list method](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [pop](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) provides a convenient way to do that:

# inside class Deck:

[Since](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [pop](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [removes the](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [last](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [card in the list, we are dealing from the bottom of the deck.](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt)

To add a card, we can use the list method append :

```
# inside class Deck:
```

### def add_card(self, card): [self.cards.append(card)](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

A method like this that uses another method without doing much work is sometimes called a veneer [. The metaphor comes from woodworking, where a veneer is a thin layer of good](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [quality wood glued to the surface of a cheaper piece of wood to improve the appearance.](http://gutenberg.org/)

[In this case](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [add_card](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) is a “thin” method that expresses a list operation in terms appropriate [for decks. It improves the appearance, or interface, of the implementation.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[As another example, we can write a Deck method named](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [shuffle](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) using the function [shuffle](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [from the](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [random](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [module:](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

## 18.7 Inheritance

Inheritance is the ability to define a new class that is a modified version of an existing class. [As an example, let’s say we want a class to represent a “hand”, that is, the cards held by](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [one player. A hand is similar to a deck: both are made up of a collection of cards, and both](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [require operations like adding and removing cards.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

A hand is also different from a deck; there are operations we want for hands that don’t make sense for a deck. For example, in poker we might compare two hands to see which [one wins. In bridge, we might compute a score for a hand in order to make a bid.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[This relationship between classes—similar, but different—lends itself to inheritance.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) To [define a new class that inherits from an existing class, you put the name of the existing](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) class in parentheses:

### [class Hand(Deck):](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) ["""Represents a hand of playing cards."""](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[This definition indicates that](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [Hand](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [inherits from](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [Deck](https://thinkpython.com/code/GoodKangaroo.py) [; that means we can use methods like](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [pop_card](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [add_card](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [for Hands as well as Decks.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[When a new class inherits from an existing one, the existing one is called the](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) parent and [the new class is called the](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [child](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

In this example, [Hand](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [inherits](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) __init__ from Deck , but it doesn’t really do what we want: [instead of populating the hand with 52 new cards, the init method for Hands should ini-](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) tialize [cards](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [with an empty list.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[If we provide an init method in the](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [Hand](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [class, it overrides the one in the](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [Deck](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [class:](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

```
# inside class Hand:
```

### [def __init__(self, label=](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [''](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [):](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) self.cards = [] self.label = label

[When you create a Hand, Python invokes this init method, not the one in](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [Deck](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) .

```
>>> hand = Hand( ' new hand ' )
>>> hand.cards
[]
>>> hand.label
' new hand '
```

[The other methods are inherited from](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [Deck](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [, so we can use](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [pop_card](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [add_card](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [to deal](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) a card:

### [>>> deck = Deck()](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [>>> card = deck.pop_card()](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [>>> hand.add_card(card)](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) >>> print(hand) King of Spades

### 18.8. Class diagrams

[A natural next step is to encapsulate this code in a method called](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) move_cards :

```
# inside class Deck:
```

### [def move_cards(self, hand, num):](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [for i in range(num):](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [hand.add_card(self.pop_card())](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

[move_cards](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [takes two arguments, a Hand object and the number of cards to deal. It modi-](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [fies both](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [self](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [hand](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) , and returns None .

[In some games, cards are moved from one hand to another, or from a hand back to the](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [deck. You can use](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [move_cards](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [for any of these operations:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [self](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [can be either a Deck or a](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [Hand, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [hand](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [, despite the name, can also be a](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [Deck](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [.](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

Inheritance is a useful feature. Some programs that would be repetitive without inheritance [can be written more elegantly with it. Inheritance can facilitate code reuse, since you can](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [customize the behavior of parent classes without having to modify them. In some cases,](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [the inheritance structure reflects the natural structure of the problem, which makes the](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [design easier to understand.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[On the other hand, inheritance can make programs difficult to read. When a method is](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [invoked, it is sometimes not clear where to find its definition. The relevant code may be](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [spread across several modules. Also, many of the things that can be done using inheritance](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [can be done as well or better without it.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

## [18.8](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Class diagrams](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[So far we have seen stack diagrams, which show the state of a program, and object dia-](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [grams, which show the attributes of an object and their values. These diagrams represent](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [a snapshot in the execution of a program, so they change as the program runs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[They are also highly detailed; for some purposes, too detailed. A class diagram is a more](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) abstract representation of the structure of a program. Instead of showing individual ob- [jects, it shows classes and the relationships between them.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

There are several kinds of relationship between classes:

- [• Objects in one class might contain references to objects in another class. For example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) each Rectangle contains a reference to a Point, and each Deck contains references to many Cards. [This kind of relationship is called](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [HAS-A](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) , as in, “a Rectangle has a [Point.”](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

- [• One class might inherit from another. This relationship is called](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [IS-A](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [, as in, “a Hand](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [is a kind of a Deck.”](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

- [• One class might depend on another in the sense that objects in one class take ob-](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [jects in the second class as parameters, or use objects in the second class as part of a](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [computation. This kind of relationship is called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) dependency .

[A](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [class diagram](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [is a graphical representation of these relationships.](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) [For example, Fig-](https://thinkpython.com/code/interlock.py) ure 18.2 shows the relationships between Card , Deck and Hand .

Chapter 18. Inheritance

[Deck](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[*](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Card](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law)

[Hand](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[Figure 18.2: Class diagram.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set)

[The arrow with a hollow triangle head represents an IS-A relationship; in this case it indi-](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [cates that Hand inherits from Deck.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[The standard arrow head represents a HAS-A relationship; in this case a Deck has refer-](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [ences to Card objects.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

The star ( [*](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [) near the arrow head is a](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [multiplicity](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) ; it indicates how many Cards a Deck has. A multiplicity can be a simple number, like , a range, like 5..7 or a star, which indicates that a Deck can have any number of Cards.

There are no dependencies in this diagram. They would normally be shown with a dashed [arrow. Or if there are a lot of dependencies, they are sometimes omitted.](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

[A more detailed diagram might show that a Deck actually contains a](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [list](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [of Cards, but](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [built-in types like list and dict are usually not included in class diagrams.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

## 18.9 [Debugging](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

Inheritance can make debugging difficult because when you invoke a method on an object, [it might be hard to figure out which method will be invoked.](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[Suppose you are writing a function that works with Hand objects. You would like it to](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [work with all kinds of Hands, like PokerHands, BridgeHands, etc. If you invoke a method](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) like shuffle [, you might get the one defined in](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [Deck](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [, but if any of the subclasses override](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) this method, you’ll get that version instead. This behavior is usually a good thing, but it [can be confusing.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

Any time you are unsure about the flow of execution through your program, the simplest solution is to add print statements at the beginning of the relevant methods. If Deck.shuffle [prints a message that says something like](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [Running Deck.shuffle](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [, then as](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [the program runs it traces the flow of execution.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

[As an alternative, you could use this function, which takes an object and a method name](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [(as a string) and returns the class that provides the definition of the method:](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

### [def find_defining_class(obj, meth_name):](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [for ty in type(obj).mro():](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [if meth_name in ty.__dict__:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [return ty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging)

Here’s an example:

```
>>> hand = Hand()
>>> find_defining_class(hand, ' shuffle ' )
<class ' __main__.Deck ' >
```

### 18.10. Data encapsulation

[So the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [shuffle](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [method for this Hand is the one in](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) Deck .

find_defining_class [uses the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [mro](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [method to get the list of class objects (types) that will be](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [searched for methods. “MRO” stands for “method resolution order”, which is the sequence](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [of classes Python searches to “resolve” a method name.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

[Here’s a design suggestion: when you override a method, the interface of the new method](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [should be the same as the old. It should take the same parameters, return the same type,](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) and obey the same preconditions and postconditions. If you follow this rule, you will find [that any function designed to work with an instance of a parent class, like a Deck, will also](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [work with instances of child classes like a Hand and PokerHand.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

[If you violate this rule, which is called the “Liskov substitution principle”, your code will](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [collapse like (sorry) a house of cards.](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

## [18.10](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) Data encapsulation

[The previous chapters demonstrate a development plan we might call “object-oriented](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) design”. We identified objects we needed—like [Point](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [Rectangle](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [Time](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) —and defined [classes to represent them.](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [In each case there is an obvious correspondence between the](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [object and some entity in the real world (or at least a mathematical world).](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[But sometimes it is less obvious what objects you need and how they should interact. In](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [that case you need a different development plan.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [In the same way that we discovered](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) function interfaces by encapsulation and generalization, we can discover class interfaces [by](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [data encapsulation](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[Markov analysis, from Section 13.8, provides a good example.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [If you download my](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [code from](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [, you’ll see that it uses two global](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [variables—](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) suffix_map and prefix —that are read and written from several functions.

### [suffix_map = {}](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [prefix = ()](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[Because these variables are global, we can only run one analysis at a time. If we read two](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) texts, their prefixes and suffixes would be added to the same data structures (which makes for some interesting generated text).

To run multiple analyses, and keep them separate, we can encapsulate the state of each analysis in an object. Here’s what that looks like:

```
class Markov:
```

### [def __init__(self):](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [self.suffix_map = {}](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [self.prefix = ()](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[Next, we transform the functions into methods. For example, here’s](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [process_word](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) :

### [def process_word(self, word, order=2):](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [if len(self.prefix) < order:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [self.prefix += (word,)](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [return](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators)

Chapter 18. Inheritance

[try:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [self.suffix_map[self.prefix].append(word)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [except KeyError:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) # if there is no entry for this prefix, make one [self.suffix_map[self.prefix] = [word]](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

### self.prefix = shift(self.prefix, word)

Transforming a program like this—changing the design without changing the behavior—is [another example of refactoring (see Section 4.7).](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[This example suggests a development plan for designing objects and methods:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

1. [1. Start by writing functions that read and write global variables (when necessary).](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

2. Once you get the program working, look for associations between global variables [and the functions that use them.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

3. [3. Encapsulate related variables as attributes of an object.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

4. [4. Transform the associated functions into methods of the new class.](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

[As an exercise,](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [download my Markov code from](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [https://thinkpython.com/code/](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [markov.py](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py) [, and follow the steps described above to encapsulate the global variables](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [as attributes of a new class called](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [Markov](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [Solution:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [https://thinkpython.com/code/](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) markov2.py [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

## 18.11 Glossary

encode: [To represent one set of values using another set of values by constructing a map-](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py) [ping between them.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[class attribute:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [An attribute associated with a class object.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [Class attributes are defined](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [inside a class definition but outside any method.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[instance attribute:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [An attribute associated with an instance of a class.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

veneer: A method or function that provides a different interface to another function without doing much computation.

inheritance: [The ability to define a new class that is a modified version of a previously](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [defined class.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

parent class: [The class from which a child class inherits.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

child class: [A new class created by inheriting from an existing class; also called a “sub-](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [class”.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py)

[IS-A relationship:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [A relationship between a child class and its parent class.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[HAS-A relationship:](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [A relationship between two classes where instances of one class con-](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [tain references to instances of the other.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

dependency: [A relationship between two classes where instances of one class use in-](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) stances of the other class, but do not store them as attributes.

### 18.12. Exercises

[class diagram:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) A diagram that shows the classes in a program and the relationships between them.

[multiplicity:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) A notation in a class diagram that shows, for a HAS-A relationship, how [many references there are to instances of another class.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

data encapsulation: [A program development plan that involves a prototype using global](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [variables and a final version that makes the global variables into instance attributes.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set)

flush: five cards with the same suit

full house: three cards with one rank, two cards with another

## [Chapter 19](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set)

# [The Goodies](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[One of my goals for this book has been to teach you as little Python as possible. When](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [there were two ways to do something, I picked one and avoided mentioning the other. Or](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) sometimes I put the second one into an exercise.

[Now I want to go back for some of the good bits that got left behind. Python provides a](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [number of features that are not really necessary—you can write good code without them—](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [but with them you can sometimes write code that’s more concise, readable or efficient, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [sometimes all three.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

## [19.1](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) Conditional expressions

[We saw conditional statements in Section 5.4.](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [Conditional statements are often used to](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) choose one of two values; for example:

### [if x > 0:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [y = math.log(x)](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [else:](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) [y = float(](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [nan](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) ['](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [)](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

This statement checks whether x is positive. If so, it computes math.log . If not, math.log would raise a ValueError. To avoid stopping the program, we generate a “NaN”, which is [a special floating-point value that represents “Not a Number”.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

[We can write this statement more concisely using a](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) conditional expression :

### [y = math.log(x) if x > 0 else float(](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ' nan ' )

[You can almost read this line like English: “](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [y](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [gets log-](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [if](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [x](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [is greater than 0; otherwise it](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [gets NaN”.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html)

[Recursive functions can sometimes be rewritten using conditional expressions. For exam-](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [ple, here is a recursive version of](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [factorial](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

### def factorial(n): [if n == 0:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [return 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [else:](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) return n * factorial(n-1)

Chapter 19. The Goodies

[We can rewrite it like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### def factorial(n): [return 1 if n == 0 else n * factorial(n-1)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Another use of conditional expressions is handling optional arguments. For example, here](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) is the init method from GoodKangaroo [(see Exercise 17.2):](https://thinkpython.com/code)

```
def __init__(self, name, contents=None):
self.name = name
if contents == None:
contents = []
self.pouch_contents = contents
```

[We can rewrite this one like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

### def __init__(self, name, contents=None): [self.name = name](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [self.pouch_contents = [] if contents == None else contents](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)
