[In general, you can replace a conditional statement with a conditional expression if both](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [branches contain simple expressions that are either returned or assigned to the same vari-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) able.

## [19.2](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [List comprehensions](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[In Section 10.7 we saw the map and filter patterns. For example, this function takes a list](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [of strings, maps the string method](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) capitalize to the elements, and returns a new list of strings:

### [def capitalize_all(t):](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) res = [] [for s in t:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [res.append(s.capitalize())](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [return res](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[We can write this more concisely using a](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) list comprehension :

### [def capitalize_all(t):](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) return [s.capitalize() for s in t]

The bracket operators indicate that we are constructing a new list. The expression inside [the brackets specifies the elements of the list, and the](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [clause indicates what sequence](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) we are traversing.

[The syntax of a list comprehension is a little awkward because the loop variable,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) s in this example, appears in the expression before we get to the definition.

[List comprehensions can also be used for filtering. For example, this function selects only](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) the elements of [t](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [that are upper case, and returns a new list:](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

### [def only_upper(t):](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [res = []](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) for s in t: [if s.isupper():](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [res.append(s)](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) return res

### 19.3. Generator expressions

[We can rewrite it using a list comprehension](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### def only_upper(t): [return [s for s in t if s.isupper()]](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[List comprehensions are concise and easy to read, at least for simple expressions. And they](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [are usually faster than the equivalent for loops, sometimes much faster. So if you are mad](https://thinkpython.com/code) [at me for not mentioning them earlier, I understand.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

[But, in my defense, list comprehensions are harder to debug because you can’t put a print](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [statement inside the loop. I suggest that you use them only if the computation is simple](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [enough that you are likely to get it right the first time. And for beginners that means never.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

## [19.3](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) Generator expressions

Generator expressions are similar to list comprehensions, but with parentheses instead of square brackets:

### [>>> g = (x2 for x in range(5))](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [>>> g](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [<generator object <genexpr> at 0x7f4c45a786c0>](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[The result is a generator object that knows how to iterate through a sequence of values. But](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) [unlike a list comprehension, it does not compute the values all at once; it waits to be asked.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [The built-in function](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [next](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [gets the next value from the generator:](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

### [>>> next(g)](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### >>> next(g)

[When you get to the end of the sequence,](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [next](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) raises a StopIteration exception. You can [also use a](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [loop to iterate through the values:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

### [>>> for val in g:](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [...](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) print(val)

The generator object keeps track of where it is in the sequence, so the for loop picks up where [next](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [left off. Once the generator is exhausted, it continues to raise](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [StopIteration](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

### [>>> next(g)](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [StopIteration](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Generator expressions are often used with functions like](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [sum](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) , max , and min :

### [>>> sum(x2 for x in range(5))](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

#### [19.4](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [any](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [all](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[Python provides a built-in function,](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [any](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) [, that takes a sequence of boolean values and re-](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) turns True if any of the values are True . It works on lists:

Chapter 19. The Goodies

### [>>> any([False, False, True])](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) True

[But it is often used with generator expressions:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[>>> any(letter ==](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [t](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [for letter in](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [monty](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) True

That example isn’t very useful because it does the same thing as the in operator. But we [could use](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [any](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [to rewrite some of the search functions we wrote in Section 9.3. For example,](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [we could write](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [avoids](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

### [def avoids(word, forbidden):](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py) [return not any(letter in forbidden for letter in word)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[The function almost reads like English, “](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [word](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [avoids](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [forbidden](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [if there are not any forbid-](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [den letters in](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [word](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [.”](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py)

Using any [with a generator expression is efficient because it stops immediately if it finds a](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) True value, so it doesn’t have to evaluate the whole sequence.

[Python provides another built-in function,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [all](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [, that returns](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [True](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) if every element of the sequence is True . As an exercise, use all to re-write uses_all from Section 9.3.

## [19.5](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [Sets](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[In Section 13.6 I use dictionaries to find the words that appear in a document but not in a](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) word list. The function I wrote takes d1 , which contains the words from the document as keys, and [d2](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) , which contains the list of words. It returns a dictionary that contains the keys from d1 [that are not in](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [d2](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation)

### [def subtract(d1, d2):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [res = dict()](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [for key in d1:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [if key not in d2:](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) res[key] = None [return res](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

In all of these dictionaries, the values are None because we never use them. As a result, we waste some storage space.

[Python provides another built-in type, called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [set](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [, that behaves like a collection of dic-](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [tionary keys with no values. Adding elements to a set is fast; so is checking membership.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [And sets provide methods and operators to compute common set operations.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[For example, set subtraction is available as a method called](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [difference](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) or as an operator, - [. So we can rewrite](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [subtract](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

### [def subtract(d1, d2):](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [return set(d1) - set(d2)](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[The result is a set instead of a dictionary, but for operations like iteration, the behavior is](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) the same.

[Some of the exercises in this book can be done concisely and efficiently with sets.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [For](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_db.py) example, here is a solution to has_duplicates , from Exercise 10.7, that uses a dictionary:

### 19.6. Counters

### [def has_duplicates(t):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) d = {} [for x in t:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [if x in d:](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [return True](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) d[x] = True return False

When an element appears for the first time, it is added to the dictionary. If the same element [appears again, the function returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [True](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[Using sets, we can write the same function like this:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

### [def has_duplicates(t):](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [return len(set(t)) < len(t)](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

An element can only appear in a set once, so if an element in t appears more than once, the [set will be smaller than](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [t](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [. If there are no duplicates, the set will be the same size as](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) t .

[We can also use sets to do some of the exercises in Chapter 9. For example, here’s a version](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [of](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) [uses_only](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [with a loop:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

### def uses_only(word, available): [for letter in word:](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [if letter not in available:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [return False](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) return True

[uses_only](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [checks whether all letters in](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [word](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [are in](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [available](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [. We can rewrite it like this:](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py)

### [def uses_only(word, available):](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) return set(word) <= set(available)

The <= [operator checks whether one set is a subset of another, including the possibility that](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [they are equal, which is true if all the letters in](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [word](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [appear in](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [available](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk2.py) .

[As an exercise, rewrite](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [avoids](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [using sets.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

## [19.6](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) Counters

A Counter is like a set, except that if an element appears more than once, the Counter [keeps track of how many times it appears. If you are familiar with the mathematical idea](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) of a multiset , a Counter is a natural way to represent a multiset.

[Counter is defined in a standard module called](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [collections](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) , so you have to import it. You [can initialize a Counter with a string, list, or anything else that supports iteration:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

```
>>> from collections import Counter
>>> count = Counter( ' parrot ' )
>>> count
Counter({ ' r ' : 2, ' t ' : 1, ' o ' : 1, ' p ' : 1, ' a ' : 1})
```

[Counters behave like dictionaries in many ways; they map from each key to the number of](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [times it appears. As in dictionaries, the keys have to be hashable.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[Unlike dictionaries, Counters don’t raise an exception if you access an element that doesn’t](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) appear. Instead, they return 0:

Chapter 19. The Goodies

### [>>> count[](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [d](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) []](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[We can use Counters to rewrite](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [is_anagram](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [from Exercise 10.6:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [def is_anagram(word1, word2):](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) return Counter(word1) == Counter(word2)

If two words are anagrams, they contain the same letters with the same counts, so their [Counters are equivalent.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[Counters provide methods and operators to perform set-like operations, including ad-](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [dition, subtraction, union and intersection.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [And they provide an often-useful method,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [most_common](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) , which returns a list of value-frequency pairs, sorted from most common to least:

[>>> count = Counter(](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) parrot ' ) [>>> for val, freq in count.most_common(3):](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) ... print(val, freq) [r 2](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) p 1 a 1

#### [19.7](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [defaultdict](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[The](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [collections](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [module also provides](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) [defaultdict](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [, which is like a dictionary except that](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) if you access a key that doesn’t exist, it can generate a new value on the fly.

When you create a defaultdict, you provide a function that’s used to create new values. A [function used to create objects is sometimes called a](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [factory](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [. The built-in functions that](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [create lists, sets, and other types can be used as factories:](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

### [>>> from collections import defaultdict](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [>>> d = defaultdict(list)](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

[Notice that the argument is](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) list , which is a class object, not list() , which is a new list. [The function you provide doesn’t get called unless you access a key that doesn’t exist.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

### >>> t = d[ ' new key ' ] >>> t []

[The new list, which we’re calling](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [t](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) , is also added to the dictionary. So if we modify t , the change appears in d :

```
>>> t.append( ' new value ' )
>>> d
defaultdict(<class ' list ' >, { ' new key ' : [ ' new value ' ]})
```

[If you are making a dictionary of lists, you can often write simpler code using](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [defaultdict](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [In my solution to Exercise 12.2, which you can get from](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_duck_debugging) [https://thinkpython.com/code/](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) anagram_sets.py [, I make a dictionary that maps from a sorted string of letters to the list of](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [words that can be spelled with those letters. For example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [’opst’](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) maps to the list [’opts’, ’post’, ’pots’, ’spot’, ’stop’, ’tops’] [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

Here’s the original code:

### 19.8. Named tuples

[def all_anagrams(filename):](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) d = {} [for line in open(filename):](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [word = line.strip().lower()](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [t = signature(word)](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) if t not in d: d[t] = [word] [else:](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [d[t].append(word)](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [return d](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[This can be simplified using](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [setdefault](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [, which you might have used in Exercise 11.2:](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book1.py)

[def all_anagrams(filename):](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) d = {} for line in open(filename): [word = line.strip().lower()](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) t = signature(word) [d.setdefault(t, []).append(word)](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [return d](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[This solution has the drawback that it makes a new list every time, regardless of whether](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [it is needed. For lists, that’s no big deal, but if the factory function is complicated, it might](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [be.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[We can avoid this problem and simplify the code using a](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [defaultdict](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

[def all_anagrams(filename):](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py) d = defaultdict(list) [for line in open(filename):](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) word = line.strip().lower() [t = signature(word)](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [d[t].append(word)](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [return d](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

My solution to Exercise 18.3, which you can download from [https://thinkpython.com/](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) code/PokerHandSoln.py , uses setdefault in the function has_straightflush . This solu- [tion has the drawback of creating a](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [Hand](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [object every time through the loop, whether it is](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) needed or not. As an exercise, rewrite it using a defaultdict.

## 19.8 Named tuples

[Many simple objects are basically collections of related values.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) For example, the Point object defined in Chapter 15 contains two numbers, [x](http://gutenberg.org/) [and](http://gutenberg.org/) [y](http://gutenberg.org/) [. When you define a class like](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) [this, you usually start with an init method and a str method:](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

```
class Point:
```

### [def __init__(self, x=0, y=0):](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) self.x = x [self.y = y](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

### [def __str__(self):](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) return ' (%g, %g) ' % (self.x, self.y)

Chapter 19. The Goodies

[This is a lot of code to convey a small amount of information.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) Python provides a more concise way to say the same thing:

```
from collections import namedtuple
Point = namedtuple( ' Point ' , [ ' x ' , ' y ' ])
```

[The first argument is the name of the class you want to create. The second is a list of the](https://thinkpython.com/code) [attributes Point objects should have, as strings. The return value from](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [namedtuple](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [is a class](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [object:](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

### [>>> Point](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) <class ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [__main__.Point](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [>](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

Point [automatically provides methods like](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [__init__](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [__str__](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [so you don’t have to](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) write them.

To create a Point object, you use the Point class as a function:

### [>>> p = Point(1, 2)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [>>> p](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) Point(x=1, y=2)

The init method assigns the arguments to attributes using the names you provided. The [str method prints a representation of the Point object and its attributes.](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

[You can access the elements of the named tuple by name:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

### [>>> p.x, p.y](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [(1, 2)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py)

But you can also treat a named tuple as a tuple:

### >>> p[0], p[1] (1, 2)

### >>> x, y = p [>>> x, y](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) (1, 2)

Named tuples provide a quick way to define simple classes. The drawback is that simple [classes don’t always stay simple. You might decide later that you want to add methods](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) to a named tuple. In that case, you could define a new class that inherits from the named tuple:

### class Pointier(Point): # add more methods here

[Or you could switch to a conventional class definition.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

## 19.9 [Gathering keyword args](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number)

[In Section 12.4, we saw how to write a function that gathers its arguments into a tuple:](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

### [def printall(*args):](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) [print(args)](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[You can call this function with any number of positional arguments (that is, arguments that](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) don’t have keywords):

### 19.10. Glossary

### [>>> printall(1, 2.0,](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) (1, 2.0, ' ' )

[But the](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [*](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [operator doesn’t gather keyword arguments:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### [>>> printall(1, 2.0, third=](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [TypeError: printall() got an unexpected keyword argument](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) ['](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [third](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) '

[To gather keyword arguments, you can use the](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [**](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) operator:

### [def printall(*args, kwargs):](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [print(args, kwargs)](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

[You can call the keyword gathering parameter anything you want, but](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [kwargs](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#str.format) [is a common](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [choice. The result is a dictionary that maps from keywords to values:](http://www.thinkpython.com/)

```
>>> printall(1, 2.0, third= ' ' )
(1, 2.0) { ' third ' : ' ' }
```

[If you have a dictionary of keywords and values, you can use the scatter operator,](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) ** to [call a function:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

### >>> d = dict(x=1, y=2) [>>> Point(d)](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [Point(x=1, y=2)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[Without the scatter operator, the function would treat](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [d](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [as a single positional argument, so](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [it would assign](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [d](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) to x [and complain because there’s nothing to assign to](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [y](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py) [:](https://thinkpython.com/code/birthday.py)

```
>>> d = dict(x=1, y=2)
>>> Point(d)
Traceback (most recent call last):
File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>
TypeError: __new__() missing 1 required positional argument: ' y '
```

[When you are working with functions that have a large number of parameters, it is often](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [useful to create and pass around dictionaries that specify frequently used options.](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt)

## 19.10 Glossary

[conditional expression:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [An expression that has one of two values, depending on a condi-](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [tion.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

list comprehension: An expression with a [for](http://gutenberg.org/) [loop in square brackets that yields a new](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) list.

[generator expression:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [An expression with a](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [loop in parentheses that yields a genera-](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [tor object.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

multiset: [A mathematical entity that represents a mapping between the elements of a set](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [and the number of times they appear.](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py)

factory: A function, usually passed as a parameter, used to create objects.

Chapter 19. The Goodies

## [19.11](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Exercises](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Exercise 19.1.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [The following is a function that computes the binomial coefficient recursively.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

```
def binomial_coeff(n, k):
"""Compute the binomial coefficient "n choose k".
```

### n: number of trials [k: number of successes](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

### [returns: int](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) """ [if k == 0:](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [return 1](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [if n == 0:](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [return 0](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

### [res = binomial_coeff(n-1, k) + binomial_coeff(n-1, k-1)](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [return res](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

Rewrite the body of the function using nested conditional expressions.

[One note: this function is not very efficient because it ends up computing the same values over and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) over. You could make it more efficient by memoizing (see Section 11.6). But you will find that it’s [harder to memoize if you write it using conditional expressions.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

## [Appendix A](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

# [Debugging](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[When you are debugging, you should distinguish among different kinds of errors in order](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [to track them down more quickly:](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

- [• Syntax errors are discovered by the interpreter when it is translating the source code](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [into byte code. They indicate that there is something wrong with the structure of the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [program. Example: Omitting the colon at the end of a](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [def](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [statement generates the](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) somewhat redundant message SyntaxError: [invalid syntax](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

- Runtime errors are produced by the interpreter if something goes wrong while the program is running. Most runtime error messages include information about where the error occurred and what functions were executing. Example: An infinite recur- [sion eventually causes the runtime error “maximum recursion depth exceeded”.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

- [• Semantic errors are problems with a program that runs without producing error mes-](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [sages but doesn’t do the right thing. Example: An expression may not be evaluated](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) in the order you expect, yielding an incorrect result.

The first step in debugging is to figure out which kind of error you are dealing with. Although the following sections are organized by error type, some techniques are applicable in more than one situation.

#### [A.1](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [Syntax errors](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[Syntax errors are usually easy to fix once you figure out what they are.](https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html) [Unfortunately,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [the error messages are often not helpful. The most common messages are](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book3.py) [SyntaxError:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [invalid syntax](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [and](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [SyntaxError:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake) [invalid token](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [, neither of which is very informa-](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [tive.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

[On the other hand, the message does tell you where in the program the problem occurred.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [Actually, it tells you where Python noticed a problem, which is not necessarily where the](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [error is.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [Sometimes the error is prior to the location of the error message, often on the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) preceding line.

Appendix A. Debugging

[If you are building the program incrementally, you should have a good idea about where](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) the error is. It will be in the last line you added.

[If you are copying code from a book, start by comparing your code to the book’s code](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [very carefully. Check every character. At the same time, remember that the book might be](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) wrong, so if you see something that looks like a syntax error, it might be.

Here are some ways to avoid the most common syntax errors:

1. Make sure you are not using a Python keyword for a variable name.

2. [2. Check that you have a colon at the end of the header of every compound statement,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [including](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [for](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [while](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [,](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [if](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [, and](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [def](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py) [statements.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

3. Make sure that any strings in the code have matching quotation marks. Make sure that all quotation marks are “straight quotes”, not “curly quotes”.

4. If you have multiline strings with triple quotes (single or double), make sure you [have terminated the string properly. An unterminated string may cause an](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) invalid token [error at the end of your program, or it may treat the following part of the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) program as a string until it comes to the next string. In the second case, it might not [produce an error message at all!](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py)

5. [5. An unclosed opening operator—](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [(](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [{](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [, or](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [[](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [—makes Python continue with the next line](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [as part of the current statement. Generally, an error occurs almost immediately in the](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [next line.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py)

6. [6. Check for the classic](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) = instead of == inside a conditional.

7. Check the indentation to make sure it lines up the way it is supposed to. [Python](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) [can handle space and tabs, but if you mix them it can cause problems. The best way](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [to avoid this problem is to use a text editor that knows about Python and generates](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [consistent indentation.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

8. [8. If you have non-ASCII characters in the code (including strings and comments), that](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) might cause a problem, although Python 3 usually handles non-ASCII characters. Be careful if you paste in text from a web page or other source.

If nothing works, move on to the next section...

A.1.1 I keep making changes and it makes no difference.

[If the interpreter says there is an error and you don’t see it, that might be because you and](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) the interpreter are not looking at the same code. Check your programming environment to [make sure that the program you are editing is the one Python is trying to run.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[If you are not sure, try putting an obvious and deliberate syntax error at the beginning of](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [the program. Now run it again. If the interpreter doesn’t find the new error, you are not](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [running the new code.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

[There are a few likely culprits:](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

- [• You edited the file and forgot to save the changes before running it again.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [Some](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_db.py) programming environments do this for you, but some don’t.

A.2. Runtime errors

- [• You changed the name of the file, but you are still running the old name.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law)

- [• Something in your development environment is configured incorrectly.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

- [• If you are writing a module and using](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) import , make sure you don’t give your module [the same name as one of the standard Python modules.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

- If you are using [import](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [to read a module, remember that you have to restart the](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [interpreter or use](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [reload](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) to read a modified file. If you import the module again, it [doesn’t do anything.](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[If you get stuck and you can’t figure out what is going on, one approach is to start again](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [with a new program like “Hello, World!”, and make sure you can get a known program to](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [run. Then gradually add the pieces of the original program to the new one.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py)

#### [A.2](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [Runtime errors](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

[Once your program is syntactically correct, Python can read it and at least start running it.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [What could possibly go wrong?](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[A.2.1](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [My program does absolutely nothing.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[This problem is most common when your file consists of functions and classes but does](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) not actually invoke a function to start execution. This may be intentional if you only plan [to import this module to supply classes and functions.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

If it is not intentional, make sure there is a function call in the program, and make sure the flow of execution reaches it (see “Flow of Execution” below).

[A.2.2](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [My program hangs.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[If a program stops and seems to be doing nothing, it is “hanging”. Often that means that it](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [is caught in an infinite loop or infinite recursion.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

- [• If there is a particular loop that you suspect is the problem, add a](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [print](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [statement](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) immediately before the loop that says “entering the loop” and another immediately after that says “exiting the loop”.

[Run the program.](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [If you get the first message and not the second, you’ve got an](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) infinite loop. Go to the “Infinite Loop” section below.

- [• Most of the time, an infinite recursion will cause the program to run for a while and](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [then produce a “RuntimeError: Maximum recursion depth exceeded” error. If that](http://gutenberg.org/) [happens, go to the “Infinite Recursion” section below.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[If you are not getting this error but you suspect there is a problem with a recursive](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [method or function, you can still use the techniques in the “Infinite Recursion” sec-](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [tion.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

- [• If neither of those steps works, start testing other loops and other recursive functions](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [and methods.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

- [• If that doesn’t work, then it is possible that you don’t understand the flow of execu-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collatz_conjecture) tion in your program. Go to the “Flow of Execution” section below.

Appendix A. Debugging

[Infinite Loop](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[If you think you have an infinite loop and you think you know what loop is causing the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [problem, add a](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2.py) [print](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorting_algorithm) statement at the end of the loop that prints the values of the variables [in the condition and the value of the condition.](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

For example:

### [while x > 0 and y < 0 :](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [# do something to x](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) [# do something to y](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

| print( | '   | x:  | '   | , x) |
| ------ | --- | --- | --- | ---- |
| print( | '   | y:  | '   | , y) |

[Now when you run the program, you will see three lines of output for each time through](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) the loop. The last time through the loop, the condition should be [False](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [. If the loop keeps](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [going, you will be able to see the values of](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [x](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [and](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [y](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) , and you might figure out why they are [not being updated correctly.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[Infinite Recursion](https://thinkpython.com/code/walk.py)

[Most of the time, infinite recursion causes the program to run for a while and then produce](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [Maximum recursion depth exceeded](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [error.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

If you suspect that a function is causing an infinite recursion, make sure that there is a base case. There should be some condition that causes the function to return without making a recursive invocation. If not, you need to rethink the algorithm and identify a base case.

[If there is a base case but the program doesn’t seem to be reaching it, add a](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) print state- [ment at the beginning of the function that prints the parameters. Now when you run the](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [program, you will see a few lines of output every time the function is invoked, and you](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) will see the parameter values. If the parameters are not moving toward the base case, you [will get some ideas about why not.](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py)

Flow of Execution

[If you are not sure how the flow of execution is moving through your program, add](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py) [print](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) [statements to the beginning of each function with a message like “entering function](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods) foo ”, where foo [is the name of the function.](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

[Now when you run the program, it will print a trace of each function as it is invoked.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

A.2.3 [When I run the program I get an exception.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

[If something goes wrong during runtime, Python prints a message that includes the name](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [of the exception, the line of the program where the problem occurred, and a traceback.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[The traceback identifies the function that is currently running, and then the function that](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) called it, and then the function that called that , and so on. In other words, it traces the

A.2. Runtime errors

[sequence of function calls that got you to where you are, including the line number in your](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) file where each call occurred.

[The first step is to examine the place in the program where the error occurred and see if](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [you can figure out what happened. These are some of the most common runtime errors:](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py)

NameError: [You are trying to use a variable that doesn’t exist in the current environment.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [Check if the name is spelled right, or at least consistently. And remember that local](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [variables are local; you cannot refer to them from outside the function where they are](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [defined.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py)

[TypeError:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [There are several possible causes:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

- [• You are trying to use a value improperly. Example: indexing a string, list, or](http://www.thinkpython.com/) tuple with something other than an integer.

- There is a mismatch between the items in a format string and the items passed [for conversion. This can happen if either the number of items does not match or](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [an invalid conversion is called for.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

- [• You are passing the wrong number of arguments to a function. For methods,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) look at the method definition and check that the first parameter is [self](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [. Then](https://thinkpython.com/code/Map.py) [look at the method invocation; make sure you are invoking the method on an](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [object with the right type and providing the other arguments correctly.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

[KeyError:](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [You are trying to access an element of a dictionary using a key that the dictio-](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1.py) [nary does not contain. If the keys are strings, remember that capitalization matters.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

AttributeError: You are trying to access an attribute or method that does not exist. Check [the spelling! You can use the built-in function](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [vars](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [to list the attributes that do exist.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

[If an AttributeError indicates that an object has](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) [NoneType](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) , that means that it is None . [So the problem is not the attribute name, but the object.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[The reason the object is none might be that you forgot to return a value from a func-](https://thinkpython.com/code/list_exercises.py) tion; if you get to the end of a function without hitting a [return](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [statement, it returns](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [None](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [. Another common cause is using the result from a list method, like](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [sort](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [, that](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [returns](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) None .

IndexError: The index you are using to access a list, string, or tuple is greater than its length minus one. Immediately before the site of the error, add a print statement to display the value of the index and the length of the array. Is the array the right size? [Is the index the right value?](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py)

The Python debugger ( pdb [) is useful for tracking down exceptions because it allows you to](http://gutenberg.org/) [examine the state of the program immediately before the error. You can read about](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [pdb](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [at](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci_number) [https://docs.python.org/3/library/pdb.html](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

[A.2.4](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) I added so many [print](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [statements I get inundated with output.](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py)

[One of the problems with using](https://thinkpython.com/code/double.py) [print](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [statements for debugging is that you can end up](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [buried in output.](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) [There are two ways to proceed: simplify the output or simplify the](https://wiki.python.org/moin/HowTo/Sorting) program.

Appendix A. Debugging

[To simplify the output, you can remove or comment out](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [print](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) statements that aren’t help- [ing, or combine them, or format the output so it is easier to understand.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral)

[To simplify the program, there are several things you can do. First, scale down the problem](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [the program is working on. For example, if you are searching a list, search a](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) small list. If [the program takes input from the user, give it the simplest input that causes the problem.](https://thinkpython.com/code)

[Second, clean up the program. Remove dead code and reorganize the program to make](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) it as easy to read as possible. For example, if you suspect that the problem is in a deeply [nested part of the program, try rewriting that part with simpler structure. If you suspect a](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [large function, try splitting it into smaller functions and testing them separately.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pi.py)

[Often the process of finding the minimal test case leads you to the bug. If you find that](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [a program works in one situation but not in another, that gives you a clue about what is](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [going on.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Similarly, rewriting a piece of code can help you find subtle bugs. If you make a change](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [that you think shouldn’t affect the program, and it does, that can tip you off.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

#### [A.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [Semantic errors](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[In some ways, semantic errors are the hardest to debug, because the interpreter provides](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) no information about what is wrong. Only you know what the program is supposed to do.

The first step is to make a connection between the program text and the behavior you are seeing. You need a hypothesis about what the program is actually doing. One of the things [that makes that hard is that computers run so fast.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[You will often wish that you could slow the program down to human speed, and with](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [some debuggers you can. But the time it takes to insert a few well-placed](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [print](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [statements](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) is often short compared to setting up the debugger, inserting and removing breakpoints, and “stepping” the program to where the error is occurring.

A.3.1 My program doesn’t work.

You should ask yourself these questions:

- [• Is there something the program was supposed to do but which doesn’t seem to be](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [happening? Find the section of the code that performs that function and make sure](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [it is executing when you think it should.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

- [• Is something happening that shouldn’t? Find code in your program that performs](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) [that function and see if it is executing when it shouldn’t.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

- [• Is a section of code producing an effect that is not what you expected? Make sure that](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [you understand the code in question, especially if it involves functions or methods in](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [other Python modules. Read the documentation for the functions you call. Try them](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) out by writing simple test cases and checking the results.

A.3. Semantic errors

[In order to program, you need a mental model of how programs work.](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) If you write a [program that doesn’t do what you expect, often the problem is not in the program; it’s in](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [your mental model.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[The best way to correct your mental model is to break the program into its components](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [(usually the functions and methods) and test each component independently. Once you](https://thinkpython.com/code) [find the discrepancy between your model and reality, you can solve the problem.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py)

[Of course, you should be building and testing components as you develop the program.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [If you encounter a problem, there should be only a small amount of new code that is not](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [known to be correct.](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

A.3.2 [I’ve got a big hairy expression and it doesn’t do what I expect.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

Writing complex expressions is fine as long as they are readable, but they can be hard to [debug. It is often a good idea to break a complex expression into a series of assignments to](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [temporary variables.](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames)

[For example:](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py)

### [self.hands[i].addCard(self.hands[self.findNeighbor(i)].popCard())](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal)

[This can be rewritten as:](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies)

### [neighbor = self.findNeighbor(i)](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) pickedCard = self.hands[neighbor].popCard() [self.hands[i].addCard(pickedCard)](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

The explicit version is easier to read because the variable names provide additional documentation, and it is easier to debug because you can check the types of the intermediate [variables and display their values.](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

[Another problem that can occur with big expressions is that the order of evaluation may](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project)

[x](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[not be what you expect. For example, if you are translating the expression](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py)

[π](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

into Python, [you might write:](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

### y = x / 2 * math.pi

[That is not correct because multiplication and division have the same precedence and are](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) evaluated from left to right. So this expression computes x π /2.

A good way to debug expressions is to add parentheses to make the order of evaluation explicit:

### [y = x / (2 * math.pi)](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

Whenever you are not sure of the order of evaluation, use parentheses. Not only will the [program be correct (in the sense of doing what you intended), it will also be more readable](http://gutenberg.org/) [for other people who haven’t memorized the order of operations.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[A.3.3](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [I’ve got a function that doesn’t return what I expect.](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py)

[If you have a](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict) return [statement with a complex expression, you don’t have a chance to](https://thinkpython.com/code/reverse_pair.py) [print the result before returning. Again, you can use a temporary variable. For example,](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) instead of:

### return self.hands[i].removeMatches()

Appendix A. Debugging

[you could write:](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

### count = self.hands[i].removeMatches() [return count](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[Now you have the opportunity to display the value of](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) count before returning.

[A.3.4](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [I’m really, really stuck and I need help.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[First, try getting away from the computer for a few minutes. Computers emit waves that](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate.py) [affect the brain, causing these symptoms:](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py)

- [• Frustration and rage.](https://thinkpython.com/code/structshape.py)

- Superstitious beliefs (“the computer hates me”) and magical thinking (“the program only works when I wear my hat backward”).

- Random walk programming (the attempt to program by writing every possible pro- [gram and choosing the one that does the right thing).](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

If you find yourself suffering from any of these symptoms, get up and go for a walk. When [you are calm, think about the program. What is it doing? What are some possible causes](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [of that behavior? When was the last time you had a working program, and what did you](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) do next?

[Sometimes it just takes time to find a bug.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) [I often find bugs when I am away from the](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers) [computer and let my mind wander. Some of the best places to find bugs are trains, showers,](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) and in bed, just before you fall asleep.

A.3.5 [No, I really need help.](https://thinkpython.com/code/wordlist.py)

[It happens. Even the best programmers occasionally get stuck. Sometimes you work on a](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [program so long that you can’t see the error. You need a fresh pair of eyes.](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py)

Before you bring someone else in, make sure you are prepared. Your program should be as [simple as possible, and you should be working on the smallest input that causes the error.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) You should have [print](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) statements in the appropriate places (and the output they produce should be comprehensible). You should understand the problem well enough to describe it concisely.

[When you bring someone in to help, be sure to give them the information they need:](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods)

- [• If there is an error message, what is it and what part of the program does it indicate?](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py)

- [• What was the last thing you did before this error occurred? What were the last lines](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [of code that you wrote, or what is the new test case that fails?](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

- [• What have you tried so far, and what have you learned?](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koch_snowflake)

[When you find the bug, take a second to think about what you could have done to find it](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [faster. Next time you see something similar, you will be able to find the bug more quickly.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py)

[Remember, the goal is not just to make the program work. The goal is to learn how to make](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) the program work.

## [Appendix B](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

# [Analysis of Algorithms](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py)

[This appendix is an edited excerpt from](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Think Complexity](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) , by Allen B. Downey, [also published by O’Reilly Media (2012). When you are done with this book,](https://thinkpython.com/code/has_duplicates.py) you might want to move on to that one.

[Analysis of algorithms](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [is a branch of computer science that studies the performance of](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [algorithms, especially their run time and space requirements. See](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [http://en.wikipedia.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1_soln.py) org/wiki/Analysis_of_algorithms .

The practical goal of algorithm analysis is to predict the performance of different algo- [rithms in order to guide design decisions.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e)

[During the 2008 United States Presidential Campaign, candidate Barack Obama was asked](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [to perform an impromptu analysis when he visited Google. Chief executive Eric Schmidt](http://wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Project) jokingly asked him for “the most efficient way to sort a million 32-bit integers.” Obama [had apparently been tipped off, because he quickly replied, “I think the bubble sort would](https://thinkpython.com/code/reducible.py) [be the wrong way to go.” See](https://thinkpython.com/code/PokerHandSoln.py) [http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=k4RRi_ntQc8](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt)

This is true: bubble sort is conceptually simple but slow for large datasets. [The an-](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix_sort) [swer Schmidt was probably looking for is “radix sort” (](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/) Radix_sort ) .

The goal of algorithm analysis is to make meaningful comparisons between algorithms, [but there are some problems:](https://thinkpython.com/code/palindrome_soln.py)

- [• The relative performance of the algorithms might depend on characteristics of the](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) [hardware, so one algorithm might be faster on Machine A, another on Machine B.](http://gutenberg.org/) [The general solution to this problem is to specify a](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) machine model and analyze the [number of steps, or operations, an algorithm requires under a given model.](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py)

- [• Relative performance might depend on the details of the dataset. For example, some](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk3.py) [sorting algorithms run faster if the data are already partially sorted; other algorithms](http://www.speech.cs.cmu.edu/cgi-bin/cmudict)

[But if you get a question like this in an interview, I think a better answer is, “The fastest way to sort a million](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [integers is to use whatever sort function is provided by the language I’m using. Its performance is good enough](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) [for the vast majority of applications, but if it turned out that my application was too slow, I would use a profiler](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [to see where the time was being spent. If it looked like a faster sort algorithm would have a significant effect on](https://thinkpython.com/code/typewriter.py) performance, then I would look around for a good implementation of radix sort.”

Appendix B. Analysis of Algorithms

[run slower in this case. A common way to avoid this problem is to analyze the](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) worst case [scenario. It is sometimes useful to analyze average case performance, but that’s](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [usually harder, and it might not be obvious what set of cases to average over.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

- [• Relative performance also depends on the size of the problem. A sorting algorithm](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) that is fast for small lists might be slow for long lists. The usual solution to this [problem is to express run time (or number of operations) as a function of problem](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [size, and group functions into categories depending on how quickly they grow as](http://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#types-set) [problem size increases.](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt)

[The good thing about this kind of comparison is that it lends itself to simple classification](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function) [of algorithms. For example, if I know that the run time of Algorithm A tends to be pro-](https://thinkpython.com/code/cartalk1.py) [portional to the size of the input,](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [, and Algorithm B tends to be proportional to](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [, then I](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) expect A to be faster than B, at least for large values of n .

[This kind of analysis comes with some caveats, but we’ll get to that later.](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

#### B.1 Order of growth

[Suppose you have analyzed two algorithms and expressed their run times in terms of the](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_frequencies) [size of the input: Algorithm A takes 100](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/most_frequent.py) + [1 steps to solve a problem with size](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time1_soln.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [; Algo-](https://thinkpython.com/code/Point1.py) [rithm B takes](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov2.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [1 steps.](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py)

[The following table shows the run time of these algorithms for different problem sizes:](https://thinkpython.com/code/do_four.py)

| Input  | Run time of | Run time of |
| ------ | ----------- | ----------- |
| size   | Algorithm A | Algorithm B |
| 1 000  | 100 001     | 1 001 001   |
| 10 000 | 1 000 001   | 100 010 001 |

At n = 10, Algorithm A looks pretty bad; it takes almost 10 times longer than Algorithm [B. But for](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [n](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [=](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html) [100 they are about the same, and for larger values A is much better.](http://docs.python.org/3/library/datetime.html)

The fundamental reason is that for large values of n , any function that contains an n term will grow faster than a function whose leading term is n . The leading term is the term with the highest exponent.

[For Algorithm A, the leading term has a large coefficient, 100, which is why B does better](https://thinkpython.com/code/flower.py) than A for small [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/polygon.py) [. But regardless of the coefficients, there will always be some value of](http://gutenberg.org/) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/sed.py) where an > bn [, for any values of](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [a](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [b](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/koch.py)

[The same argument applies to the non-leading terms. Even if the run time of Algorithm A](https://thinkpython.com/code/pie.py) were n + [1000000, it would still be better than Algorithm B for sufficiently large](https://thinkpython.com/code/inlist.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/anagram_sets.py)

[In general, we expect an algorithm with a smaller leading term to be a better algorithm for](https://thinkpython.com/code/c06d) [large problems, but for smaller problems, there may be a](https://thinkpython.com/code/pronounce.py) crossover point where another [algorithm is better. The location of the crossover point depends on the details of the algo-](https://thinkpython.com/code/metathesis.py) [rithms, the inputs, and the hardware, so it is usually ignored for purposes of algorithmic](http://wiki.python.org/moin/BitwiseOperators) analysis. But that doesn’t mean you can forget about it.

B.1. Order of growth

[If two algorithms have the same leading order term, it is hard to say which is better; again,](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf's_law) [the answer depends on the details. So for algorithmic analysis, functions with the same](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral) [leading term are considered equivalent, even if they have different coefficients.](http://www.cartalk.com/content/puzzlers)

[An](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) [order of growth](https://thinkpython.com/code/spiral.py) is a set of functions whose growth behavior is considered equivalent. [For example, 2](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [, 100](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [and](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [+](https://thinkpython.com/code/grid.py) [1 belong to the same order of growth, which is written](https://thinkpython.com/code) O ( n ) in Big-Oh notation [and often called](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [linear](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [because every function in the set grows linearly](https://thinkpython.com/code/Time2_soln.py) [with](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [n](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/) [.](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/)

[All functions with the leading term](https://thinkpython.com/code/emma.txt) n belong to [O](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [(](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [; they are called](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [quadratic](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py) [.](https://thinkpython.com/code/invert_dict.py)

[The following table shows some of the orders of growth that appear most commonly in](https://thinkpython.com/code/analyze_book2.py) [algorithmic analysis, in increasing order of badness.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function)

[Order of](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann_memo.py) [Name](http://www.thinkpython.com/) [growth](https://thinkpython.com/code/ackermann.py) [O](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [(](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) [)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Md5) constant [O](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [(](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [log](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py)

b

[n](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [logarithmic (for any](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [b](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/Circle.py) [O](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [(](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [n](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [)](http://docs.python.org/3/reference/datamodel.html#specialnames) [linear](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [O](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [(](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [log](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[b](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox)

[n](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) [linearithmic](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birthday_paradox) O ( n ) quadratic [O](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [(](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [n](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/BadKangaroo.py) [cubic](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal) [O](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [(](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [c](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[n](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[)](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [exponential (for any](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [c](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py) [)](https://thinkpython.com/code/draw.py)

[For the logarithmic terms, the base of the logarithm doesn’t matter; changing bases is the](https://thinkpython.com/code/GoodKangaroo.py) [equivalent of multiplying by a constant, which doesn’t change the order of growth. Sim-](https://thinkpython.com/code/rotate_pairs.py) [ilarly, all exponential functions belong to the same order of growth regardless of the base](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) of the exponent. Exponential functions grow very quickly, so exponential algorithms are [only useful for small problems.](http://tinyurl.com/thinkpython2e) [Exercise B.1.](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation) Read the Wikipedia page on Big-Oh notation at http: // en. wikipedia. org/ [wiki/ Big_ O_ notation](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py) [and answer the following questions:](https://thinkpython.com/code/markov.py)

Before you start multiplying, remember](https://thinkpython.com/code/words.txt) that you only need the leading term.
